THE RED PAGE.
A LIVING ARTINT
A BOOK recently published in Sydney,
The First Discovery of Australia and
New Guinea , by George Collingridge de
Tourcey, draws attention anew to the
remarkable career of a remarkable man. This
book is a fully-illustrated summary, for the use of
schools and students, of the author’s larger work,
The Discovery of Australia, published in 1895.
it would be difficult in a survey of Australasian
literature to point to a work upon which more
intelligent labour has been spent. It was indeed a
labourof love, and one for which—asdesigner, wood-
engraver, and linguist—Collingridge was singu-
larly well equipped. Though in the field of
Australian cartography there had been pioneers
before him, there was no one with greater learn-
lnS' —f lO 9 ne "'ho carried inquiry so far. As a
contribution to geographical science the value of
The Discovery of Australia consists chiefly in this:
that it traces the maps of the earliest Portuguese
and Spanish discoveries to their first known
sources, and eliminates errors that had made
previous accounts of these discoveries something
like a geographer’s myth. Where others stopped
short, Collingridge went as nearly as possible to
the fountain heads, and his investigation is an
exemplary piece of comparative geography. As a
piece of book-making, The Discovery of Australia
brought for the first time copies of the earliest
maps of the earliest voyages—thanks to Colling-
ridge s artistic talents within the reach of
ordinary readers.
Even now there is much that is vague—much
that never will be cleared up. The paradox of
early American discovery is paralleled by that of
Australia. It is belibved that Columbus landed
in the Bahamas, yet in all the islands of the
Bahamas there is not one that bears out his
description of the harbour that he found—large
enough to float the navies of the world. And
when, previous to the American World’s Fair of
1893, • the task of settling upon which island
Columbus landed was given to three eminent
geographers, each decided upon a different
island. Concerning Torres and De Queiros and
their voyages, there is much also that can be but
guessed at. Torres coasted Northern Australia,
through the straits that bear his name, and never
knew that he had discovered a continent : he
thought that the headlands he saw were merely
the capes of an island. De Queiros, on the other
hand, reaching the New Hebrides, was proud of
the discovery of a continent.
George Collingridge was led to his inquiry
fourteen or fifteen years ago, when some copies of
rare old manuscript maps of Australian voyages
were made for the public libraries of Sydney,
Melbourne, and Adelaide. These maps were re-
ported upon by the Sydney press and the Geo-
graphical Society. Collingridge was not satisfied
with the conclusions arrived at. and went to the
Sydney Library to inspect the maps. A conti-
nental land named Jave-la-Grande, supposed to
represent Australia, had been reported in The
S. AT. Herald as being New Guinea. Running
the longitudes and latitudes, Collingridge saw that
this was an error. So he was led to make a
special study of the old documents. After some
years he grew so interested in his work that he
made a point of reading everything that had been
written on the subject in English, French,
Italian, Spanish, and Portuguese ; and he studied
the cartography, accumulating a valuable library
of maps, photographs, and books. Asked by a
firm of publishers whether he would undertake to
write upon the subject, he accepted the pro-
position, and The Discovery of Australia was the
outcome. It is a noble monument of learned
labour.
jfi,
George Alphonse Collingridge de Tourcey was
born at Godington, Oxfordshire, England, on 29th
October, 1848. He lives to-day at “Jave-la-
Grande,” Hornsby, near Sydney, in his 58th year;
after many narrow escapes of dying. The first was
when he jumped down from a haystack as a boy,
and was picked up apparently moribund. The last
occurred only a few weeks ago, when he jumped
upon a moving train, missed his footing, hiing bv
one hand to the guard-rail until the train was
stopped—and was picked up bruised and bleeding
to remain five days in bed. A whole career of
reckless gallantry lies between.
Collingridge sprang from an artistic family, and
was educated to art in France. He should, he
declares, have become an architect; and he was
hesitating about becoming a pupil of Viollet-le-
Duc’s when an advantageous offer was made him
to undergo an apprenticeship in wood-engraving.
The year was 1863,"when Gustave Dore was pro-
ducing his masterpieces, and xylography was at
its apogee. Young Collingridge’s merit was such,
and the prices paid for his work were so good, that
at the end of the first year he was earning £25 a
week for his teacher. When his time expired,
wishing to make a special study of painting, he
entered Chapon’s studio, and followed classes with
Harpignies. About that time an appeal was made
for Catholic volunteers to fight Garibaldi. Many
of Colhngridge’s friends were going to Italy
—some to study art, others to enlist. The
regiment of the Papal Zouaves, after the
disaster of Castel Fidardo, had been reorganised ;
Garibaldi was threatening an invasion of the
Papal States; and Collingridge joined De
Charetfee’s corps, and remained in Italy two years.
During his military career he was in seventeen
different engagements with the Garibaldians,
including Monte Rotondo, Nerola, Mentana, and
Lungaretta, and received several decorations for
distinguished bravery.
Returning to France in 1869, he continued work-
ing as a wood-engraver until the Franco-Prussian-
war broke out. During the siege of Paris he went
to London, coming back on the proclamation of
peace. After he had journeyed to Spain in the
seventies with Yierge, his brother Arthur, who
had settled in Australia, wrote a request to
“come over and help on The Illustrated Sydney
News.” Collingridge arrived in time for the
International Exhibition, and shortly afterwards
the brothers founded the Art Society of N.S.
Wales. Presently Collingridge married and
“ settled down ” —to teach art, to write books, to
cultivate his garden, and to agree that a rightly-
governed Australia might be for a fine race of men
and women the finest country in the world....if
only the Italian proverb were untrue, that “ the
fish stinks first at the head.”
SHINING STAGERS
JR. TUTIN, of Albert Avenue, Hull,
# appears to be the unusual publisher
who prints poetry because he loves
it, and sells it for a little more than
a song with intent to edify the unedifying
England of to-day. One can scarcely con-
ceive the British workman being led aside
from beer and betting to contemplation of
Sir Philip Sidney ; or “the smart set”
ceasing the pursuit of bridge to enjoy the
society of “The Matchless Orinda.” Bu;
degenerated cities and brutalised villages a
not all England ; and there is in the Englis i
character a valorous quality that persists : n
many a mind and home—a homely virt
that does not sparkle and can no longer sin r,
yet remains staunch in its instincts of n -
bility and beauty. Though learning is
longer set on high, and poetry is a seldo
occupation, there must be (if one could fine
them !) many readers in England to who
Tutin’s booklets will be a treasure. In Au
tralia there are a few.
Jt
The authors are chosen mostly from the
17th century—that Eflglish age when the
high-leaping flames of the great Elizabethan
had died down, but the embers of romanc
were still red and glowing. ’T was an age o :
fancy rather than of imagination—of fane
sporting in a thousand ingenious devices o’
conception and phrase, scarcely thrillin
doubtfully inspiring, but packed with such
steadfast thought, such dainty imagery, tha ;
a musing mind can find no better solace.
The booklets are well printed in a gooc
form, and the choice of poems possibly coui
not be varied with advantage. Time, indeec,
exercises upon verse the settling and ripei
ing influence that it has upon wine : th
sediment sinks to the bottom of the flask
and the clear liquor offers itself to posterit
scarcely called upon to make a consciou
judgment. Tutin is able to give his forty-
eight pages for fourpence, sixty-four for
sixpence ; and at these prices there is no on
who cannot afford to enjoy the fruit of h: s
enthusiasm. One book is devoted t
Katherine Philips—“The Matchless Orinda
to whom Gosse gave a chapter in his Seven
teenth Century Studies. Abraham Cowley
has thus summed up her poetry :
“’Tis solid and ’tis manly all,
Or rather’t is angelical ;
For as in angels we
Do in thy verses see
Both improved sexes eminently meet:
Ibey are than Man more strong, and mor
than Woman sweet.”
J*
Cowley would have been interested in the
controversy over the sex of angels tha;
excited New York last year, when Gutzon
Borglum, a sculptor commissioned by the
Cathedral of St. John the Divine to execute
statues of the Angel of Annunciation and the
Angel of Resurrection, duly delivered two
female angels which the Cathedral autho
rities refused, on the ground that angels
have always been held to be masculine. The
dispute ended by the sculptor going to the
church with a mallet and chisel and destroy-
ing his female angels. One need not deal so
vigorously with Cowley’s too-complimentary
opinion. Orinda, one fears, was but an
echo, though an agreeable echo, and “ the
wonder is not that she has written so well,
but that she has written so at all.” Though
she died in 1664, her woman’s weakness
seems to anticipate the frigidity of the
eighteenth century, when all but the last
heat had left the poetical ashes, and writers
contented themselves with ornamentation of
the reliquary urn.
One likes the eulogy of the “ Irish Grey-
hound,” with its closing couplet:
Few men of him to do great things have learned,
And when th’ are done to be. so unconcerned !
The difficult word business was referred to
recently, yet it comes quite easily in Orinda’s
manner. To cite the passage from her lines
on “ Friendship ” will give a sufficient indi-
cation of her talent :
Let the dull brutish world that know not love
Continue heretics, and disapprove
That noble flame ; but the refined know
’T is all the heaven we have here below.
Nature subsists' by love, and they do tie
Things to their causes but by sympathy.
Love chains the different elements in one
Great harmony, linked to the heavenly Throne.
And as on earth, so the blest quire above
Of saints and angels are maintained by love:
That is their business and felicity,
And will be so, to all eternity.
That is the ocean : our affections here
Are but streams borrowed from the fountain there;
And’t is the noblest argument to prove
A beauteous mind, that it knows how to love.
Those kind impressions which fate can’t control.
Are Heaven’s mintage on a worthy soul,
For love is all the arts’ epitome,
And is the sum of all divinity.
JX
Thomas Carew was closer to the great age,
and his Pegasus is a steed of brighter mettle.
Those who know him only from anthologies
will assuredly be glad to receive for four-
pence this little bookful of his merit. The
introductory note, correct in the main, leaves
an insufficient impression of his value. We
have almosb*iost the art of conceits such as
Carew finds for his mistress’ lips :
When they part themselves asunder,
She breathes Oracles of wonder.
Or his declaration that her
Every step begpts a lover.
And we can rarely turn verses like these :
In her fair cheeks two pits do lie,
To bury those slain by her eye ;
So, ’spite of death, this comforts me,
That fairly buried I shall be,
My grave with rose and lily spread ;
O’t is a life to be so dead !
Come then, and kill me with thy eye :
For, if thou let me live, I die.
When I behold those lips again,
Reviving what those eyes have slain,
With kisses sweet, whose balsam pure
Love’s wounds, as soon as made, can cure,
Methinks ’t is sickness to be sound,
And there’s no health, to such a wound.
Come, then, and kill me with thy eye :
For, if thou let me live, I die...
Another booklet collects English versions
of the odes and fragments of Sappho, ver-
sions that are more curious than efficacious.
One finds none better than Maurice Thomp-
son’s paraphrases, here given some years
ago, and one misses lines like that for
Atthis,
Loved once, loved madly, loved so long ago...
or the couplet,
Dark petals of a hyacinth, odorous still,
Crushed by rude feet on many a windy hill!
Probably the translators, being men, have
lost that feminine note which, as P. L.
Bickley wrote in The Academy, is Sappho’s
alone—“ the entire abandonment to passion,
an all-pervading and. over whelming emotion.
Probably, indeed,” Bickley continued in
lines well meditated, “it is this very emo-
tion which in its omnipotence stifles articu-
late poetic utterance in women, being too
great for words. But this one time it has
found expression, if not allayment, in
splendidly articulate song.” And a few
lines further :
Shelley, whose nature had in it so much that
was feminine, approaches Sappho more closely
than any other in this respect. Such verses as
those beginning:
“ Far, far away, O ye
Halcyons of Memory,"
such a phrase as “ I wander and wane like the
weary moon,” are expressions of a more essential
emotion than is perceptible in the most passionate
utterance of Mr. Swinburne or Catullus. Mr.
Yeats has something of this feeling too, with the
difference that his poetry is not Greek in spirit,
but Celtic ; grey, not golden.
Other booklets reprint selections from Sir
Phillip Sidney and his sister, from Thomas
Vaughan, John Norris of Bemerton, and
from Bertram Dobell’s recent discovery,
Thomas Traherne. Traherne was worth
discovery. His crystal mind, his simple
expression, remind one of Blake :
The streets were paved with golden stones,
The boys and girls were mine,
Oh how did all their lovely faces shine !
Yet he has ideas that are quite modern in
their scope : as in this “Of Contentment,”
to conclude the survey.
Contentment is a sleepy thing !
If it in Death alone must die,
A quiet Mind is worse than Poverty,
Unless it from Enjoyment spring !
That ’s.Blessedness alone that makes a King !
Wherein the Joys and Treasures are so great,
They all the powers of the Soul employ,
And fill it with a Work compleat,
While it doth all enjoy.
True Joys alone Contentment do inspire.
Enrich Content and make our Courage higher.
Content alone’s a dead and silent Stone ;
The real life of Bliss
Is Glory reigning in a Throne.
Where all enjoyment is,
The Soul of Man is so inclin’d to see,
Without his Treasures no man’s Soul can be,
Nor rest content Uncrown’d !
Desire and Love
Must in the height of all their Rapture move,
Where there is true Felicity.
Employment is the very life and ground
Of Life itself ; whose pleasant Motion is
The form of Bliss:
All Blessedness a life with Glory Crown’d :
Life ! Life is all; in its most full extent
Stretcht out to all things, and with all Content!
Seven Angels of the Renascence, by Sir Wyke
Bayliss (Lond., Pitman; 10s. 6cf. net), is a book
that does not preserve the promise of its begin-
ning. The “angels” are “messengers”—those
who carried the tidings of the Renascence, and
Bayliss gives the name to Cimabue, Leonardo,
Michael Angelo, Titian, Raphael, Correggio, and
Claude Lorraine. His book sketches the life and
work of each of these men in their relation to the
general progress of art; and as he is painter him-
self (President of the Royal Society of British
Artists) his material is usually well chosen, and
his comments are often apt. Much labour and
knowledge are needed to make such a survey.
Bayliss fails as Ruskin failed (he reads curiously
like a Ruskin who has adapted himself to journal-
ism) —because throughout he has seen his subject in
a false light. He has not been content to take his
artists as they were, and to consider their work as
it stood or stands, without reference to his own
preconceptions. Like Ruskin, he endeavours to
state Art in terms of his notion of the Glory of
God, and he judges the painters of the Renascence
partly from the standpoint of Christian dogma
One naturally enquires what he has to say about
Leonardo—that great artist and sceptic. It is a
pity, but Leonardo will not fit Bayliss’s Christian
scheme: he is the ugly duckling who cannot be
brought into the religious brood. “ I wish I cou
be as sure in my estimate of the relation in whic
Leonardo da Vinci stands to the renascence o
art,” says Bayliss, rather sadly.—This defect robs
the book of permanent value. Still, for peop
who can endure partial glimpses, it is not a bac
book ; and people who prefer to know all that ca
be known may find here an addition to their sui
of knowledge. Such a summary as the followin
is convenient, and has some justice:
The foundations of the renascence of art were laid b
da Vinci on the finest lines of scholarship and SGientif i
knowledge. A new splendour came upon it through tl
impassioned imagination of Michael Angelo. It wi
enriched with colour through the palette of Titian,
became more lovely through the tenderness and grace o
Correggio—more stately and balanced through the com
pleteneSs of Raphael.
Bayliss repeats a Cellini anecdote with a pro
found moral. Cellini kicked his heels at th
court of France for an appointment. He was
offered one with a salary of 300 crowns only, anc
straightway remembered that he had vowed t
visit the Holy Sepulchre and devote his life anc
art to religious themes. So he started. Th
king sent after him the pleasant tidings that the
salary would be raised to 700 crowns, with a pre-
liminary bonus of 500 and extra pay for wore
done—and Cellini forgot all about the Hoi
Sepulchre.
As the book proceeds, Bayliss has too man
words that give too little help. Some of hi
chapters are only reviews of other men’s books
some merely a dilution of Vasari; and hi
sentiments grow stodgy, his sermon stale
What one really approves is the remarkabl
series of portraits and illustrations, admirabl e
chosen—sometimes with curious autographs, saic
to be original, attached to the portraits. Some o'
these illustrations have an air of being dragged
by head and shoulders—like the pictures of Jesu
to illustrate the author’s theory that the picto
rial representation of Jesus has not varied
throughout the ages ; —but pictures and portrait
are so interesting that one cannot wish onq away
The Melbourne maga. Life has had its share o;:
uncompromising criticism, so it seems fair to say
that the Feb. number prints an interesting yarn
from Charles Nuttall abaut the American girl
Nuttall doesn’t draw badly, but one is inclinec
to say that he writes better than he draws. This
little picture of the American at home in New
York is perfect:
A gentleman who occupies rooms next to mine, in an
apartment house, is in the habit of coming to visit me
and, incidentally, to smoke, chew, expectorate, and tc
furnish me with information concerning the ways of his
compatriots. Only last week he was speaking on this
subject of social precedence. “ Yassir,” he said, aiming
at my cuspidor, and scoring a miss that was much worse
than a mile. “ Yassir ; the women-folks here see that
each other toes the line. Now, yew b’lieve me. The gir
who does shorthand and plays on a typewriter won’t be
seen walkin’ with a mere clerk. An’ the mere clerk, she
reaches for her smellin’-bottle when she passes a
saleslady. An’ the saleslady throws a fit ii
a milliner speaks to her. An’ the milliner,
if she gets into the factory department by mis-
take, she curls up on the floor an’ hollers for the police
An’ the factory girl, when she sees a domestic servant
sweepin’ off a front stoop, why she goes home in a ambu-
lance, overcome with cerebro-spinal-meningitis ! Yassir,
it’s a fact. An’then ag’in. Right, here in this hash-mil'
that you an’ me is the bright partickler ornaments of,
there’s an old hairpin on the floor above whose name is
Van Dopper ; an’ she’s an aristocrat. Yassir, she ain’t got
enough money to buy herself warm socks in the win-
ter, an’ she’s shaped like three water-melons. But
her name’s Van Dopper, an’ that’s enough for her an’ the
other women. She won’t look at any of ’em except the
landlady, who’s a reduced duchess of some kind. (Never
knew a landlady that was born to the business. No
matter how well they’re doin’, they’ve always come
down.) Yassir. An’ she takes the landlady in for a cup
of tea-royal twice a week, whiles the other women grieves
alone in their rooms. There ain’t one of ’em, ’at would
n’t keep a samovar goin’all day if she’d only come in,
an’ she knows it, an’ that’s herway of enj’yin’ life.”
■Jt
Thomas E. Spencer, a Bulletin contributor—
whose remarks in verse upon O’Toole and
M'Sharry, and how McDougall topped the score,
appear in The Bulletin Reciter —makes his bow in
a book of his own, published by N.S. W. Bookstall
00. (3s. 6d.), with a portrait of a pleasant-faced
elderly gentleman and illustrations complete.
The title of the volume is (once more) How
McDougall Topped the Score —and other Verses
and Sketches—some of which appear to share
M‘DougaH’s celebrated fitness for bush recitation.
Spencer makes no especial literary claim, but his
rhymes have an individual character, and some of
them are brightly pointed and neatly versified.
His sentimental pieces scarcely read as well as the
humorous, and need a voice and a platform to
make them real—though one owns to being hit by
the rhyme of the London-Australian who has
made his pile :
. “The Rouseabout ” displays Spencer’s comical
idiosyncrasy. One likes “Did You Ever,” and
“The Irony of Fate,” and “The Haunted
Lagoon,” and “The Stockman’s Serenade,” but
clings to “ O’Toole and M'Sharry ” after all.
Spencer has a mind with a really humorous kink
m it, and if the book were on a level with his best
one would like him as well as Leland. Luckily or
unluckily, he has had to mix his ore for the
public crushing. The second part of the book is
occupied with the reflections and reminiscences
of Mrs. Bridget M‘Sweeney, which begin by
saying :
The great drawback to this climate is that the hottest
days all come in the summer, whin we could do without
’em, and the cold ones in winter, whin we don’t want
era—
and look likely to be popular.
A.S.R.
But the Voice keeps on saying “ Come Back
To the bush and the wallaby track,
To the bush with its sadness,
Its grandeur and gladness,
Its space and its freedom. Come back 1 ”

Model 1905 Self-Loading Rifle
This rifle is a six shot, hammerless take-down, made in .32 and .35 calibers
the cartridges being of the modern smokeless powder type. The recoil of
the exploded cartridge ejects the empty shell, cocks the hammer and feeds
a fresh cartridge from the magazine into the chamber, leaving the rifle ready
to shoot upon the operator’s pulling the trigger. The self-loading system
adds much to the pleasure of rifle shooting, either at target or game. The
list price of this model is $28.00.
These Rifles For Sale By All Dealers.

ANOTHER CONVINCING PROOF.
THIS TIME AT MERRYLANDS.
THE CASE OF MR . H. ALMOND .
(BY A LOCAL REPORTER.)
Whilst much of the anguish and grief which exist in some families as the result of ill-health is hid from
the gaze of the outside world, and is known only to those who are immediately concerned, sufficient tran-
spires to excite the sympathy of every genuine philanthropist, and to bring forth strenuous efforts on behalf
of suffering humanity. Here is an instance in which the reader will recognise a desire on the part of Mr.
Henry Almond, storekeeper, Park-road, Merrylands, to extend a helping hand to any who may be afflicted as
he was some years back.
“In what way was your health affected?” inquired the reporter.
“ Well, for two years previous to coming here, and for a good while after starting this business, I was
always mad with neuralgia, and you may guess that having a complaint like that so long must have had
a direful effect upon my system generally. Really, sometimes I was fairly raging, owing to the agony I
was in, and many a time wished that I was dead and out of the world altogether. Oh, it was a life of fearful misery,
and I pity any poor soul in similar straits. You may rest assured that I resorted to pretty well every known treat-
ment in the hope of gaining some relief, but success evaded all my efforts. My gums, my jaws, the cheekbones were
the parts where the torture principally existed ; but, curiously enough, there" were days when one side of my face
and head was affected till nightfall, and then the pains shifted over to the other side. And, as for sleep—well, do
you think anybody could sleep under such adverse circumstances? I know I couldn’t, as a rule ; though, at long
intervals, when nature could not hold out any longer, I lapsed into a state of unconsciousness that might be called
sleep, and whilst it lasted I had the worst dreams you could think of.”
“ I don’t suppose you were usually fit to do much work ?”
“ Indeed, I was not; and I was also so low-spirited that I did not seem to care whether I was ever able to work
again or not. Shortly after the neuralgia attacked me I noticed that my digestion was getting bad, and so it con-
tinued to fail, until eventually my internal organs were so completely upset that even the lightest food could not be
assimilated. My tongue had a dirty coating, and the taste in my mouth of a morning was most disagreeable—clearly
indicating the bilious state I was in ; and another thing that troubled me was my sight—very poor indeed sometimes—
while my eyes felt twice their proper size, and as heavy as lead. Between my shoulders I had more pains to bear,
and I ached so much across the loins that it was painful to walk even a short distance. But you’ve no idea of the
fearfu| state of nervousness I got into—used to give little jumps and twitches, as though I had St. Vitus’s Dance
sometimes, and the unsettled frame of mind I was in-gave rise to much mental misery also. Simple matters irritated
me, and caused undue excitement, and I tell you candidly I couldn’t find a moment’s peace or happiness anyhow.”
“After so much ineffective, treatment, how did you.manage to get well again?”
“This way: A new customer came to the shop one day, and, seeing how ill I was, said, ‘Did you ever
try Clements Tonic? Well, try a bottle; it won’t break you;’ and at last I did, for which I am devoutly
thankful. Oh ! it was like something you read about in a novel the way Clements Tonic served me ; but
it was a joyful reality nevertheless. To think that I had suffered for years, and only then had got a
remedy to do me good, was something to be thankful for, and I was, too. Why, Clements Tonic began
to soothe my nerves when I had only taken two or three bottles, and you couldn’t credit the amount of ease
it had given me already. It roused the weakened energies of my system, and stimulated my appetite to
such a degree that before long I was eating as well as ever—when the neuralgia had entirely gone, of course
you can guess how grateful I was. It is also in regard to indigestion that Clements Tonic is deserving of
recognition at my hands, for that was, after all, one of the principal things from which I suffered. Yes ;my last
remedy fairly opened my eyes as to what medicine really can do when it is of the right kind, for, in spite of every-
thing else failing, Clements Tonic set to work and put me right in no time. A few weeks’use of it made such an
altered man of me that I had nearly forgotten what it was like to get up feeling half dead with weariness. My breath
was sweet, and I had no unpleasant taste in my mouth of a morning ; my eyes were bright, and the sight good ;
I slept soundly each night, every pain had gone, so what more could any man wish for? You can publish my story
in any way you wish.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, HENRY ALMOND, of Park-road, Merrylands, in the State of New South Wales, Commonwealth of Australia,
do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully read the annexed document, and that it contains and is a true
and faithful account of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also contains my full permission to publish in any
way my statements, which I give voluntarily, without receiving any payment; and I make this solemn declaration
conscientiously believing the same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900.
(Sgd.) HENRY ALMOND.
Subscribed and declared at Merrylands this twenty-third day of September, one thousand nine hundred and four,
before me.
GEORGE J. FINLAYSON, J.P.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Will Someone Sing a Song?
I want to sing a song—
The song Australia needs
To set the warm blood gushing
In the veins of stern men rushing
On to brave and mighty deeds.
Its music (O, so sweet and strong !)
Shall thrill the nation’s heart
With its rhythmic passion tender,
Rich in all the magic splendor
Of the true-born singer’s art.
Aye, that grand, inspiring song shall be
Of Young Australia’s history
An era and a part
I want to sing a song—
The song my land awaits,
With its longings ripe for stirring,
With its great heart fit for daring
All the fair or frenzied Fates.
Its music shall be rich and rare
And bear no paltry theme,
But shall rise in exultation
For the youngest, fairest nation
In the great all-worldly scheme.
Yea, its melody shall roll and rise
In grandeur to the arching skies
Like songs of which we dream.
Australia waits a song,
But the years roll swiftly by,
And O ! my brother singers—
Young Australia’s message-bringers—
Who shall sing it, you or I ?
Its music must be rich and rare,
Inspiring, deep, and strong ;
It shall be our nation’s greeting,
Sung to set the warm hearts beating
In the bosoms of the throng.
Yes, a blazoned part that song shall be
Of our Australia’s destiny-
. . . Will someone sing Her Song !
N.S.W.
GILROONEY.
Deep Down.
Death comes in with his icy hand,
Places it on your forehead damp,
Upsets the things that you lately planned,
And sets you off on your last long tramp.
He whispers softly you must not stay,
It’s fully time you were on the track,
An 1 tells your friends you’re going away,
And it’s more than likely you won’t be back.
Bury me deep, bwry me deep.
Shoot me down a thousand feet,
Lay me not where the violets creep ;
Drop me down ivhere the breakers beat.
Bury me deep, bury me deep;
What does it matter when I die ?
So when at last I fall asleep,
On the bed of the ocean let me lie.
“ When I am dead,” the rich man said,
“Give me a funeral great and grand,
And raise a monument over my head,
Marble hewn by some master hand.
Sing my praises o’er all the earth,
Whisper my name in a reverent tone,
Tell the world of my sterling worth,
Give me in heaven a golden throne.”
So when he died they buried him deep,
The multitude followed and bowed its head,
And the minister said, ‘ ‘ May he peaceful sleep,
The great good man who now lies dead.”
But they did 'not state that he sold his soul
For land and gold and cattle and sheep ; ■
Down with the worm and the snouted mole
On the road to Sheol they buried him deep.
VIC.
BURNETT GRAY.
Bluebells.
They told me it was fancy when I heard the
bluebells ring,
But I turned away and smiled at them in scorning ;
Will they tell me it was fancy that I heard the
magpie sing,
As I wandered in the bushland in the morning ?
And the chimes on every breeze
Were wafted through the trees,
Such a dainty pealof fairy music ringing ;
And when the breeze was past,
And thp chimes grew faint at last,
Then I fancied I could hear the fairies singing.
The daisies bloom so sweetly, and the grasses
proudly wave,
Where the plains beneath the drowsy sun are
sleeping ;
For the Springtime’s borrowed garlands deck the
Autumn staid and grave,
Though afar I see old Winter slyly peeping.
But he’s far and far away
This sunny noon in May,
When the earth with golden radiance is glowing.
While the sky is densely blue,
And the same fair azure hue
Is reflected in the bluebells round me growing.
Every pulse is madly thrilling with the joy of
being here,
In the world which people say is dull and dreary,
For the singing and the ringing to my heart are
very dear,
How in Nature’s arms can I be sad and weary ?
Other folic may toil and strive
For the right to be alive
In the city lanes ’mid sin and sorrow sighing ;
But I cross the plains of Day,
And my heart is light and gay
As the bluebells, and the birds above me flying.
N.S.W.

MAN from the other side of Mooroobooloo —the land of Anchor Pence—writes : “There wasn’t any Ruberoid
Roofing advertisement in The Bulletin last week.” No; the space was used for Giant Paper, and on
the 7th of December it was occupied with Anchor Pence. GIANT PAPER and ANCHOR PENCE are
just as necessary to, the man on the land as RUBEROID ROOPJNG. With GIANT PAPER he lines and ceils
his house, making it dust, damp, wind, moisture and insect proof. With ANCHOR PENCE he has the cheapest,
safest, and most durable fence that money can buy. Later on, we will tell you something about AYENARIUS,
the destroyer of White Ants, a preservative of wood above and below ground or water.

II ow to A ddress Betters.
Correspondents to Tiie Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (ivith regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, MARCH 1, 1906.
OUR BLOATED COUNTRY.
The latest batch of banking returns show
Australia to be in the same remarkable position
which it occupied when The Bulletin last dealt
with this subject a few months ago. There have
been three good seasons in succession, and just
now this is a bloated land which doesn’t know
what to do with its money—and at the same time
it is an enormously indebted land which is still
borrowing, and which doesn’t know what on
•arth to do with its liabilities. These anomalies
may seem impossible, but, thanks to an amazing
course of bad management, they exist. Australia
just now is full of cash and running over. There
was never, in all its history, so much money lying
idle in its banks—the unemployed gold has
increased by over £5,000,000 in twelve months—
yet just because the money is lying idle
through bad management, it doesn’t help the
worker to find employment. And, curiously
enough, so far as producing an income
goes, the superfluity doesn’t help the capitalist
much more than it does the worker. The
banks have had to cut down their interest
rates in order to discourage the inrush of
deposits for which they can’t find an
investment, and £lO,OOO on deposit now
only yields about as much return as £7OOO or
£BOOO did a while ago. It may be pleasanter to
have the £lO,OOO, but as far as the profit goes
there is no more advantage in it. There are only
a handful of securities quoted on the Stock Ex-
changes in which money can be invested, and in
the rush to find investments for money they have
risen till in many cases £lO,OOO of capital returns
no more than £5OOO did a year or two ago. All
the favorite stocks have gone up till they are
hardly worth buying at the price; yet, at the
same time, high as they are, they are hardly worth
selling at the price, because the man who realises
on the stock that has gone up doesn’t know what
to do with the proceeds except invest them in
another stock which has gone up m the same
crazy fashion. One striking example of the situ-
ation is the Commercial Bank of Australia, which
has still a deficit of nearly £1,200,000 to make
good out of profits ; there isn’t the ghost of a
visible show that its ordinary shareholders
will get a dividend for a whole string
of years ; yet the ordinary shares, which, on the
face of things, are worth less than half the
10s. per share paid up on them have lately sold
at 10s. 3d., and 11s. is now demanded. People
are actually buying at more than its face value a
stock which pays no dividend at all. And this
hilarious jest is not an isolated case.
* * * . .
The man who has money to invest may, of
course, put it into land or bricks and mortar, but
they have also gone up till they are hardly worth
buying. And meanwhile all this bulging out of
the aggregate bag of gold does hardly anything to
provide work for the people. The fact that in-
vestors, by reason of an unusual abundance of
cash, are paying ridiculous prices for the ordinary
shares of the Commercial Bank, furnishes no
toiler with a job. The fact that land has gone up
doesn’t make the land employ a single extra man ;
it merely causes the State to be robbed of something
extra when it repurchases the land for closer
settlement. An increased number of people are
going away to spend an unusually gorgeous holi-
day in Europe, and to buy there the clothes and
jewellery they would otherwise get in Australia, but
that doesn’t appreciably assist the toiling masses.
And at a time when the banks are being offered
far more money than they want at per cent,
the fool Government of N.S.W. is borrowing
money in London at rather over that figure, and
the public debt keeps increasing, and the struggle
to renew the old debts as they fall due is as severe
as ever. And while the N.S.W. Government
borrows money in London at 3J per cent, or more,
Australian money is being lent in London—in
some cases at much under 3J per cent. —to prevent
it lying wholly idle. It is about the most elabo-
rately capsized system of finance on record.
• • * »
This is a time when, if Australia had a sane
tariff, its manufactures would be growing at a
pace compared to which Jonah’s gourd would
seem a lame and overloaded caterpillar, and the
Commonwealth would be starting out to overtake
the United States in population and import-
ance. The country wants, merely for its own
supply, cotton, woollen, linen, silk and jut©
mills, carpet factories, steel, iron and zinc
works, copper and brass foundries, establish-
ments for the making of machinery, and countless
other things. Also it wants population, and the
way to get population is to supply employment.
The money to create these industries is spilling
about the country loose ; the desirable white men
to work them are wandering about the world
without the necessary coin to get even reasonably
tight. But Labor legislation has put up wages
and reduced working hours till it is impossible for
any industry which enters into competition with
the importer to exist except under high Pro-
tection, and the Labor party, by refusing to put
high Protection, or any other kind of Protection,
on its programme, has paralysed all efforts to get
a tariff which will make great industries possible.
Even if there were forty times as much unem-
ployed money in Australia as there is, it wouldn’t
help the establishment of a single industry while
the Australian manufacturer is required to pay
almost the highest wages on earth for the shortest
hours on earth, and yet compete, with very little
Protection —sometimes with none at all—against
all the world, and while his infant industry is
expected to hold its own, on level terms, against
huge concerns which, by the size of their opera-
tions, can raise economy to a science.
% * * *
Again, the Commonwealth owes over £231,000,000
of public debt, and is greatly harassed over the
renewal of the huge sums that are constantly fall-
ing due. Every year now there are big loan* to be
floated for the repayment of other big loans; and
every time the operation has to be done this country
is tottering on the verge of the Pit. London has,
before now, flatly refused to put up the money
asked for, but these were new loans, so nothing
very startling happened by reason of the refusal.
The day London refuses to put up the money for
a huge renewal loan, Australia, so long as it does
nothing to help itself, is liable to become bank-
rupt. If the trouble strikes it in a bad season,
bankruptcy is almost certain. The present is the
best time Australia has seen in the last 20
years for scraping together five or six or
ten millions by economy, extra taxation, and
other means, and putting them into a sinking
fund for the reduction of those liabilities which
constitute such a dizzy gamble. It would be a.
case of investing five or six or ten millions qf our
own money in our own securities at a little over
3| per cent., and Australia has just now a lot of
money for which it isn’t getting 3| per cent. And
when a few millions were taken off the over-loaded,
money market the amount which remained would
fetch so much better interest that the investor
would be better off than he is now. There would
be an immediate dropping of stocks to something
more like their real value, and it would no longer
be necessary to pay 10s. 3d. for a 10s. share that
pays no dividends. The country would save
enormously in taxation later on through the
reduction of its debt, and with a sinking fund it
would be far less likely to be remorselessly
squeezed over its renewals or to meet the
spectre of Public Insolvency suddenly in its
path. The blank indifference of the Labor
party to this question is a mattter which
The Bulletin has always regarded with amaze-
ment. A sinking fund is the most socialistic in-
stitution that could be imagined. It is a common
fund, in which every citizen has a common in-
terest. It is a scheme for gradually buying
£231,000,000 worth of our securities out of the
hands of the individual rich man—the individual
usurer and money-lender—and making them public-
property. It is a method whereby the poor man
will ultimately cease to pay interest to the rich
man, and it is that paying of interest which makes
the rich man constantly richer and enables him to
get a bigger mortgage On the poor. The party
which makes such a fuss about nationalising a
tobacco industry, worth a poor, miserable million
or two, and yet takes no interest in nationalising
an investment worth £231,000,000, and carrying
an income of over £8,000,000 a year, is an anomaly.
* •* * *
There are a few other things that might well be
done, by public or private enterprise, in this time
of sudden abundance. There is money enough
floating around to found one or two new banks
and pastoral and finance companies, and half-a-
dozen new fire insurance concerns —State-owned
or privately-owned ; but to make room for them
it would be necessary to discourage a number of
foreign institutions right off our premises by
means of an absentee-tax. Has anyone heard of
the absentee-tax lately? Again, the two trans-
continental railways which are absolutely essen-
tial, for defence purposes and for many other
purposes, are still waiting to be built —out of
revenue. They would return perhaps 2 per cent.,
and a great deal of Australian money is not even
yielding 2 per cent, just now. Then this is a
time when the small farmer who wants to buy out
his unwilling landlord, and who requires to raise
money on mortgage for the purpose, can get money
more cheaply than usual, therefore this is especi-
ally the time for the Federal graduated land-tax
to appear, in order to force the reluctant landlord
to put his property on the market. There is a.
little more talk than usual about the Federal
graduated land-tax, but it still looks painfully
remote. In short this is, above all times, a time
for Australia to be up and doing with feverish
energy, and Australia is not up and doing by any
means.
• * * *
Apparently, under the present system of sleepy
and incompetent government, there is nothing to
be done with our momentary superfluity of wealth
save make a Boom with it. On present appear-
ances it must be employed in gambling in town
and suburban allotments and in land generally, in
share-jobbing, in wild-cat mines, in trips to
Europe, in clothes, yachts, and liquor, in expen-
diture on fast women and slow horses. If it can’t
be put into manufactures by reason of the fool
tariff, if no Government has sense enough to
reduce the public liabilities with part of the
surplus or to build publicly-owned railways with
it, it it can’t go into legitimate agriculture because
the big landlord is in the way—then there seems to
be no alternative save to make a Boom and a Public
Drunk with it, followed by a memorable Bust if
the good seasons last long enough to make the
Boom a fine large one. The drought will come
back, of course, and if the present policy continues
the end of the good times will find this country
with a bigger debt burden and a larger interest
bill than it had at the beginning, and with no
better a show, in proportion to population, of
manufacturing industries —the drought-proof in-
dustries which should tide the country over bad
years—-than it had before. Then, presumably,
the big loans, and the public works which are
really relief works, will start again, only perhaps
London will be tired of Australian loans by that
time, and will not want to hear from Owe’Sul-
livan. And in that case the result will be plain
Famine—the kind they have in India and Japan.
THE CONGRESS OF THE HIGHLY
CIVILISED RACES.
According to all the available evidence, there are
more people on the globe to-day than at any pre-
vious period in history. The birth-rate, despite
all the discussion about its falling off, keeps
further ahead of the death-rate than it has been
known to do since statistics commenced, and of
the multiplication of humanity there is no visible
end. Sanitation has reduced mortality. The
white man wanders about the earth interfering
with the methods—slavery, cannibalism, tribal
war, infanticide, &c. —by which his backward and
undesirable colored brother used to diminish his
own numbers. The missionary passes round the
hat with feverish energy to get funds for the pre-
servation of races which have never shown them-
selves worth preserving—races which take up
ground that might otherwise be occupied by pro-
gressive and energetic peoples—and by so doing
he restricts the growth and increase of these pro-
gressive and energetic peoples. And the awful
Bruce Smith kind of person wants to promote the
further expansion of the races which have never
done anything for humanity, and seeks to trans-
plant them to the few patches of the world which
the white man occupies, and to restrict the multi-
plication of the white peoples —if not to actually
kill them off—by crowding them out with niggers,
Chinamen, and sundries. The human race, in the
aggregate, keeps increasing, but a great deal too
much of the increase is the wrong kind of increase.
* • * •
Then there is an uneasy theory that the habit-
able part of the globe is growing smaller, that the
ice-cap is creeping southward from one Pole
and northward from another, and that there is
less room while there are more people wanting
room. Gut of this idea a French scientist evolved
a more or less comforting theory of a periodical
deluge which is to clear off most of the human
race and give the globe a fresh start. Isow and
then, he reasoned, in the interminable procession
of centuries, the two Polar ice-burdens grow so
heavy that the earth suddenly loses its balance.
The last ounce of ice makes it spin round and
start revolving on a new axis; the tropics shift
into the polar regions and the polar regions
become the tropics; and the sudden whirl tears
the seas from their beds and sends them hurtling
over the land, washing away all save the inhabi-
tants of the highest tablelands or the furthest
inlands. Then the ice melts in its new loca-
tion ; the weary old globe—now a much larger
globe so far as its habitable area is
concerned —recovers its ancient balance and
goes back to rotate on its old axis; and
again the sudden whirl tears the seas from
their location and starts them careering over
the land. And when all this shake-up is
finished the mere handful of people and animals
that remain crawl down from their highlands to
inspect their lone and muddy earth, and find dead
whales decaying up in the Alps, and all civilisa-
tion washed away ; and they start to people the
world afresh. It is an interesting theory, but
being a very remote and dubious one it may be
ignored so far as concerns any question of the
possible overcrowding of this very small speck of
circular dust.
* • * *
The human population consists of races that are
■worth preserving, and other races. Roughly
speaking, the progressive races, or those capable
of real civilisation, are distinguished by three
prominent characteristics. They have the self-
governing capacity—a thing which all white
peoples have shown to a greater or less degree,
and of which the colored tribes have exhibited
hardly a trace. They have the national instinct i
the nation holds together by its own cohesion, and
is not simply an aggregation of units whose terri-
torial borders consist of as much land as the
tyrant of the moment can grab, or as little as he
can save from the clutch of the adjacent tyrant.
All the white races and some of the colored ones
have this national instinct; a large section of the
•colored races seem wholly without it. And, by
way ot a third distinction, the progressive white
races have an almost illimitable capacity for the
development and use of machinery ; the unpro-
gressive colored ones show little or no ability in
this direction. As a general rule, too, it may be
set down that the race which never showed any
ability to develop a real civilisation of its own
can’t permanently assimilate the civilisation of a
more advanced people. It may be veneered or
painted or patched with it, as the Maori, the
Kanaka, the Chinaman, the Turk, the Malay,
and the Javanese have been, but if the white man
•ceased to veneer him by main force the colored
brother would go back to his old savagery in a
handful of years, and be the same incurably
inferior person as of old.
* * ■* *
Some day, if the world becomes overcrowded,
the scientific and educated white man will begin
to think hard over these matters. He will find
that the white race has ceased to increase because
a community which wants to rear its progeny on
the expensive lines of modern progress can t
afford to increase any further. He will notice
that the colored races, which can live more cheaply
by dispensing to a great extent with clothes, fur-
niture, and education, are still multiplying. H e
will bring up with a shock against the choice of
two crimes—the wholesale extirpation of the less
desirable races to make room, or the whole-
sale murder of the unborn progeny of the
highly civilised races to economise room. Then
he will probably decide, in a odious, scientific
way, that the more useless races are boin"
preserved and encouraged in their multiplica"
tion chiefly to feed the vanity of the mission-
ary and to make profit for the employer of cheap
labor. It will probably be about this time that
the Congress of Highly Civilised Races will meet
at Berlin or Paris or London or Philadelphia to
discuss means for the obliteration, by some
tremendous and painless process, of one or two
continents-full of the unprogressive tribes—the
ones that can best be spared—so that the more
progressive communities may resume the process
of expansion. And then, also, there will be
scrambled together, at Pekin or Timbuctoo or
elsewhere, the wild, staring, panic-stricken Con-
gress of the ¥nprogressive Races, to devise means
for tumultuously taking up its axe and spear, and
its antiquated gun, and its lassoo, and its old clead-
and-gone cannon and its broken rock, against the
wrath to come. One will be a congress with a
bulging forehead and a cold business-like eye and a
brain stuffed full of knowledge. The other will
be a frantic combination which has dropped its
Gainfully acquired veneer of civilisation, and gone
ack frankly to first principles. And between the
two camps will probably rage the wild unpractical
preacher with his old unheeded tale that all men
are equal—a frantic individual with a long-tailed
coat whirling in the wind and his mouth full of
texts and dogmas, vainly trying in the midst of
the world’s greatest tragedy to take up the last
collection for the conversion of the pagan and for
the purchase of pants for the small nigger.
« * *
And somewhere in between the two camps there
will also be the tragic figure of the nation whicli
wasn’t invited to attend the Congress of Highly
Civilised Races, but which has worn its veneer of
culture so gracefully and with such an air of
realism that it can hardly believe that it is really
intended to leave it out of the list of the de-
stroyers, or to include it among the destroyed.
Its capital will be a surging mass of frantic
humanity, all asking if this horror can be pos-
sible, and its statesmen will be pouring in then-
assurances of support to the utterly unresponsive
Congress of Highly Civilised Races, and its boss
tyrant will either be drunk in his palace or hur-
riedly tying up his bag of wealth and preparing
to fly and leave things to their fate. And, mean-
while, from the Conference of Lost Races will
stream in perspiring brown and black and yellow
ambassadors urging the undecided community to
realise its doom, and to boldly throw in its lot
with the Lost, and thereby so strengthen them
that they may, perhaps, win in the great
struggle after all. And the boss tyrant of the
nation that is still hoping against hope-
the tyrant who is either drunk or wildly
packing his carpet-bag for flight—will probably
decapitate some of these ambassadors (not neces-
sarily for publication, but as evidence of good
faith) and send their heads to the Congress of
Highly Civilised Races with a frenzied assurance
that he is with the Congress heart and soul, and
that, though his invitation unfortunately mis-
carried, his envoy is on the way to join in the pro-
ceedings, and sool the Congress on to kill every-
body. And still there will be no reply. And
meanwhile more ambassadors, hotter, madder,
fiercer and muddier than the first lot—brown,
yellow and black ambassadors, each frothing at
the mouth with rage—will keep pouring in to
state that, if they get no satisfactory reply, the
Lost Nations will at least do one great and
righteous act of vengeance before they go under,
for they will stamp flat and utterly destroy and
root out the people who tried to sit on a
rail, and refused to join them in their
hour of trouble. And after that the boss
tyrant of the nation, which is spurned by one side
and which still dare not join the other, will pro-
bably go crazy with terror in his palace, and make
his last public appearance gibbering on a gndea
minaret, with his crown over his ear, and ms
Grand Vizier or Prime Minister or Commander-
in-Chief trying to pull him down by the leg, while
below him millions of his subjects surge to and
fro, and try to drown their horror in drink, and
aim futile rocks at the elevated potentate who
could save neither them nor himself.
* * * ,
Then will come the last act, when the forces of
science and progress start out to clear the ear
and make room for more science and more
progress, while on the other side the motley
battle line of the Unprogressive Races sketches
500 miles long as it goes to its *ast hope
less fight. It will be the boss discord ot
history—soo miles of brown, black, yeUow and
copper-colored humanity, 500 miles 0
and pigtails and shaven cramums, 500 mi es
of mixed languages, ignorance, retrogression,
savagery, frenzy, antiquated weapons, old battle
cries, excited priests, bonzes, dervishes, and othe
clerics, 500 miles of all the old circumstances of
the earth seen under the strangest, maddest con
ditions ever known beneath the sun As it rnoves
two small specks will be engulfed before it like
ants in the Atlantic-the iast namsionary trymg to
fixuo a reconciliation and the last cheap la
advocate urging the inadvisability of destroying
so much valuable and innumerable coolie. An
then the science of the 30th century will
with the tumult that was left over the back
regions of the ages, with the usual result, ana
man will have entered on a new epoch.
* * * *
After the breakage of that great line the
rest will be just plain butchery. Also, an ad
“ement will appe.r in tl.e papers celling to
tenders for the disposal of the dead.

The Political Flicker.
George Reid last week collected togeth< b ral
10 followers to decide on a policy for next^
election, and the ten made very little progre^
Reid’s own idea is to run the elec
Socialism, which is a poor skinny dewme
promise. A programme which doesn‘ tbin „ is
to do anything, but only to be agamst someth g
a very sad little affair, even when it s strong
against a well-defined something , wbl . P an
which consists solely of vague
ill-defined something is miserable. Ihe d
party doesn’t profess to be Sociahst pa _ D
though some of its members may candi-
a matter of fact, theN.S.W. Socialists run »
dates of their own quite apart from th
party. Not being a Socialist party, it bein g
need to define Socialism ; but Ge°RGe ReiD,
anti-Socialist, certainly needs to define**** He
in order to explain what he is wor k,
hasn’t made much progress in tms


so far, but he is energetically whacking at a mist
with a feather. George desires to sink the fiscal
issue so far as to leave the present tariff alone, and
to combine the retrogressives of both fiscal parties
in his anti-Socialist campaign. Conroy barks
back at him that Protection is Socialism, being
a paternal and interfering device to benefit the
manufacturing and artisan class, and that a
person like himself, who lives up in a tall, cold
atmosphere of frozen contempt for any scheme for
benefiting anybody, can have no truck with Pro-
tectionists. Conroy might go a bit further and
add that the carrying of the farmer’s produce at
unprofitable rates to help the man on tne land is
also Socialism, but he doesn’t. And though he
professes himself a real all-wool Freetrader, he
seems to be the same old fraud that the N.S.W.
Freetrader has always been. At least, the world
still waits in vain for a declaration from Conroy
that he is in favor of abolishing the Protectionist
duties imposed by N.S.W. Freetraders in favor of
beer, sugar, wine, tobacco, and the like ; that he
wants to knock off every Protectionist duty ; that,
where duties must be levied —for the sake of
revenue —on articles that can be made locally, he
advocates an exactly equal excise on the local
product; and that he is in favor of extinguishing
all the surreptitious advantages given to local pro-
ducts by reduced railway freights, reduced harbor
dues, etc. So Conroy, the sham Freetrader, and
Reid, the sinker of the fiscal issue, have appar-
ently split over their various degrees of bogusness;
and the small party which has no policy except
that of promising to combat in some way not
specified something that it doesn’t define,
threatens to go to the country in two halves.
At present these are the parties that have risen,
or are likely to rise, on the political horizon :
(1) The Protectionist Democratic party.
(2) The Labor party divided into
(a) Protectionist Laborists.
(b) Allegedly Freetrade ditto.
(3) The anti-Socialist Opposition divided into
(a) Freetrade retrogressionists.
(b) A few Protectionist ditto.
(4) The other Opposition which calls itself Freetrade
and anti-Socialist, but which will have nothing to do with
the Protectionist retrogressionists on the ground that no
Protectionist can retrogress far enough. This party con-
sists at present of Conroy.
(5) The as yet completely missing Real Freetrade party
which wishes to lives up to its creed and give no
preference of any kind to any Australian product.
(6) The Dill-Macky crowd.
(7) Reid’s dog, if it can be found.
If a real Freetrade party would only turn up
things might sort themselves out a little at the
December elections, but the continued absence of
that institution and the impossibility of getting
the anti-Socialists to define the thing they are
“ anti ” leaves politics looking like the flicker of a
biograph.

The American Life Assurance Problem.
That great American spread-eagle life assurance
co., the Equitable of New York, has definitely
announced its intention of retiring from the pur-
suit of business in Australia. The other two
“ American Giants,” it is alleged, may also retire,
but as regards the Equitable the announcement is
definite. This opens a long vista of possibilities
for Australian policy-holders. If the Equitable
sells its big buildings, realises its other Australian
investments, and leaves only an agent to collect
premiums on existing policies and remit the cash
to the Land of the Fried Nigger, the security, left
for Australian policy-holders will become both
small and precarious. It will consist only of any
•current premiums that can be attached in the
hands of the agent; and as policies turn into
claims, and living men who pay pre-
miums are replaced by dead men whose
representatives want large sums of money,
the security, which is likely to be very
insufficient to begin with, will become less and
less. In the end it will probably become so much
less that for the last batch of policy-holders it will
be a minus quantity. According to all appear-
ances a time will come when the Equitable, if it
pleases, may refuse to pay any Australian claims,
and a judgment against it in the Australian
courts will, in the absence of assets, be worth
only the blot on the legal document. The judg-
ment, of course, could be carried to the United
States courts. What they are most Australians
only know, or guess, by reading American papers
and magazines, and American facts and American
fiction or alleged fiction ; but if any reliance can
be placed on these authorities, Sodom and Gom-
orah were about as promising places to
secure justice as the United States when
a wealthy corporation is the defendant. In
modern tales by American writers the
honest judge is the amazing and incredible ex-
ception, and the bought judge is the rule.
Of course, it may be said that a company like the
Equitable would never refuse to pay a just claim
even if, in the absence of assets here and the'
absence of justice in the U.S. courts, the claim
could not be enforced. That may be so, but, in
view of recent revelations, outsiders do not feel
too wildly confident just now in the honor of U.S.
life assurance magnates, and they would like a
better guarantee. In this case, the example of
Germany—sober, sensible, prompt, business-like'
Germany, where they rise to the occasion and Do-
Things—isworth noting. When a great American
life assurance concern proposed to clear out of
Germany, the State promptly put in a receiver to'
take charge of the German assets and the German
income from premiums, rents and interest on
investments, and to see that not one penny of the
accessible funds left Germany till every local
policy-holder was paid. Any surplus, when affairs
are cleared up, will go back to the United States,
but the interests of the German policy-holder come
first in the ideas of the German Government, and it
has no idea of trusting these interests to any justice
that may be obtained by scrambling in the U.S.
Courts. The Bulletin has much too humble an
opinion of the courage and energy of the Australian
Government to expect that it will follow this-
example, but it quotes the example on spec. If
the Australian Parliament and the Deakin
Cabinet are what they ought to be, there will be-
an early session, and one of the first doings will be-
the passing of an Act to lay hands on all the
accessible funds of any life office which is clearing
out of Australia, and to put them under a respon-
sible State manager, who will hold them till the
last Australian policy matures and is paid in full..

Trades Union Law.
The Royal Commission appointed in Great-
Britain to consider the “ Taff Yale decision,” has-
presented a report, but does not take the respon-
sibility of making a recommendation on the one-
vital issue referred to it. The “Taff Vale decision,”'
and subsequent judgments amplifying it, stripped
trade-unions of all the privileges and immunities-
previously allowed to them by law in the
•organisation of strikes. The British com-
mission reports that those privileges and
immunities were never, strictly speaking,
legal, and that the Judges who now declare
against them are quite in tne right. That much,
with all deference to the eminent lawyers forming
the Commission, was quite well known from the
first. A strike is necessarily an illegal weapon.
What one man may do legally—leave his work —a
collection of men may not agree to do simultane-
ously with the object of embarrassing their em-
ployer, for that is a conspiracy. But British law,
wiser in practice than in theory, had, for many
years before the “ Taff Yale decision,” decided to
tolerate the illegality of strikes, the “ conspiracy ”
of workmen to hold up their employers’ works, and
even the efforts of those workmen to prevent others
from taking their places. It would be difficult to
over-estimate the value of this tolerance to the
peaceful growth of British freedom. Unrest,
which might have become liot, perhaps revolu-
tion, was kept by tolerance within bounds. Em-
ployers found their tendency to tyranny checked
by the fact that their workers could —if they cared
to face the test of hunger—stop work and go to
serious lengths to prevent “blacklegs” from
taking their places. Workers found some sense
of justice and amelioration in a social system
which gave them very little, but did allow them
the right to strike when goaded beyond endurance,
and even winked at a certain amount of violence
when “blacklegs” put in an appearance to com-
plicate the issue. But there was never any legal
basis for the tolerance of the strike, which alvvays
was, in the eyes of the Common Law, an injurious
conspiracy; or the boycott, or the picket. It
advantages nothing, therefore, for a Royal Com-
mission to solemnly give an assurance that the “ Taff
Vale decision ” was “ good law.” Of course it was
good law, but was it good sense ? That is the real
problem. Should the safety-valve of strikes be
locked down, and the discontent which theyallowed
to dissipate be kept from expression until it
reaches explosive force ? Or should some more
civilised means of dealing with labor’s grievance
than the tolerance of the strike be designed ? In
New South Wales B. R. Wise made a courageous
attempt to deal with the problem by an Arbitra-
tion Act, which admitted and punished the ille-
gality of the strike, but at the same time set up a
tribunal of justice to take the place of the arbitra-
ment of force. With strange folly the Tory party
in the State has carried on a strenuous
campaign of slander against that Act; the
Judges have taken the opportunity of every
legal quibble to whittle away its powers;
and at the present time the labor unions are
seriously considering whether they will not have
to revert to the strike method. (Yet in Maori-
land an Arbitration Act on the same lines has
been a conspicuous success, and there is never
mention of a strike in that country.) The position
which the Tory in every civilised country must
face is that, education having taught the workers
something of their rights and powers, they will
not be content with anything less than justice.
All that the rich can hope for is that the process
of change will be made as gradual as possible and
be accompanied with as little harsh unsettlement
of existing conditions as possible. But the
change itself is inevitable. It may be effected
peacefully and gradually by arbitration laws and
other legislation appropriating more and more of
the worker’s product for himself, or it may come
by the rough road of Revolution. Come it must.
If British law forbids strikes, it must supplement
the prohibition with means for arbitration, other-
wise the suppressed discontent will one day ex-
plode.
The Financiers.
[For The Bulletin.]
Peace and war to-day are in the hands of a group of
cosmopolitan capitalists.—John Burns.
We are the people that pull the strings
Behind the scenes, and the puppets dance.
Ministers, princes, and queens and kings,
In all of their pomp and circumstance,
Wait on our orders ; and if, perchance,
We say the word that a million slays—
Sullies the sword and reddens the lance—
What do we care—so long as it pays ?
'The high heart’s hope that the poet sings—
The smile of a Phryne fresh from France—
Little it matters, so either brings
Grist to the mills of high finance.
Nothing so low as to miss our glance,
Nothing so high as beyond our gaze.
Let the race recede, let the race advance—
What do we care—so long as it pays ?
The patriot’s heart to one land clings.
We know no home, for the world’s our stance.
We follow the nations’ bickerings,
Like the evil birds of old romance.
, We shed the light of our countenance
Where streets run red and roof-trees blaze.
The poor and weak —they must tak*> their
chance —
What do we care —so long as it pays ?
You may curse us kings of high finance,
And speak of the ape and the tiger’s ways;
But it’s honi soit qui mal y pense,
What do we care—so long as it pays ?
MAORILAND. TAIWA.

The N.S.W. Bench and the Lands Scandals.
It is rumored that there will be shortly one or two
judicial resignations in N.S.W., and that the
Carruthers Government is preparing to fill the
vacancies. The Bulletin hopes that existing
Judges will be sufficiently patriotic to retain their
seats on the Bench until, at least, Parliament
meets. It is, of course, possible that Parliament,
when it does meet, will be just as friendly to this
Willis Government as it was last session,
in spite of the latest developments pointing
more strongly than ever to Cabinet insincerity in
the prosecution of the Lands Inquiry. But there
is, at any rate, some reason to hope that, even at
the eleventh hour, Parliament will act with
honesty and courage. The community, for the
present, has the right to benefit by that hope. It
would be a serious scandal if, whilst the Govern-
ment is under suspicion, almost under trial, it
should be able to exercise the power of filling one
or two Supreme Court vacancies. The State
might thus be cursed with a friendly-to- Willis
Bench, as well as a friendly-to- Willis Govern-
ment, and in the near future the spectacle may
be seen of a Judge baulking at the execution of
his duty as this'Government has baulked. The
Lands revelations, so far as they have gone, point
with deep suspicion to several friends of the
Government; with less suspicion, but still with
suspicion, they point to certain members of the
Government. It is even a conceivable possibility
that in the future, with a rigorous and honest
inquiry—if such is ever secured —steps would have
to be taken against some members of the Govern-
ment. That, it is to be hoped, will not be the
case ; but the public is deprived of any asaurauoe
that it will not by the sedulous smothering up
of the inquiry. In such an event what would be the
public view if the country saw a Judge appointed
by the present Government trying the cases . Or,
indeed, what would the public view be of a Judge
appointed by this Government trying any of the
Lands scandals cases which may happen to come
to a judicial trial ? The State has a right to
expect of Chief Justice Dahi.ey and his colleagues
that they will protect the Bench from being
touched by the breath of the Lands scandals;
and any judicial appointment by the present
Government is likely to set the Lands scandals
breathing very heavily around the Bench.

Another Freetrade Lie.
When the Tariff Commission was in Westralia,
the local attorneys for the absentee dividend-
grabbers wailed for the removal of all duties on
mining machinery, declaring that the machines
which Westralia wanted “could not be made in
Australia.’' To support this yarn, a list of a dozen
classes of machinery which Australia “ could 11 1
produce” was prepared. Since then the Com-
mission has been in Melbourne, where, having
been blessed with Protection for some years, they
know how to make many things; and Manager
Rigby (of the Austral-Otis Engineering Co.) was
asked what he had to say on the subject of that
list. In effect this is what he had to say :
Tubular boilers : Babcock and Wilcox’s have been built
in Australia. When prospects of increased duty appeared,
B. and W. began negotiations to have them made regu-
larly in Australia. .
Blowers for furnaces: A common and Simple class of
manufacture.
High-class air compressors : Have been made already,
with royalties paid for patented parts.
Biedler valves : One Arm (Thompson, Castlemaine)
makes them in every form, denying the validity of the
patent. .
Patented rock crushers : None known to be in opera-
tion in the Commonwealth. Witness’s co. prepared to
make any kind. . .
Gates’ crusher : Has been manufactured m victoria tor
years.
Patented briquette machinery : The only such ma-
chinery tried was in Westralia, and that was a total
failure.
Patented conveyors: As far as construction goes, can
be made in a blacksmith’s shop. Protection would
enforce their manufacture here.
Concentrating tables: Wilfley tables do nine-tenths of
the work done in the Commonwealth ; and by arrange-
ment the Austral Otis Co. makes them, paying royalties.
Converters : Half a dozen in use in the Commonwealth,
some made locally.
Condensing plant, with special fittings: Possibly
patented parts, such as valves, would need the payment
of royalty. , ~ ..
Rock drills : Have been manufactured all over the
States for many years. Australian drills have been sent
to America as models.
Consumers, heat-economisers and super-heaters: Easiest
and roughest kinds of engineering work.
Winding and mill engines: Witness’s co. built the
milling engine at the Great Boulder (750h.p.) and the big
winding engine at the Boulder Perseverance.
Which evidence seems to dispose in a practical
way of the little list from Westralia, and shows
up that list as the usual mean Freetrade lie
devised for the destruction of Australian industry.
Also Rigby disposes of the shriek that, unless the
duties are removed, Westralia will have to reduce
wages, or import coolies, or let vast quantities of
ore go untreated. And if there is any part of the
yarn that it doesn’t knock out, then the experience
of, say, Bendigo disposes of it, because Bendigo, in
protected Victoria, for years past has paid divs.
out of stone which no Freetrade country on earth
can look at.
An Australian Brotherhood.
The following is a sample of the literature circu-
lated by a society calling itself the Australian
Brotherhood (its address is Post Office, Emma-
ville, N.S.W.). On its principles the Brother-
hood deserves the support of every Australian
who is even remotely worthy of the name :
We wish to place before you a few suggestions as to
Unification, or the placing of the government of the
States in the hands of one Government—that is, the Aus-
tralian or Commonwealth Government. The franchise
for this is the most liberal in the world, and it is in our
power to make it a thoroughly Australian and Democratic
one if we use our own judgment instead of allowing the
unreliable lying press of Sydney and elsewhere to form
our opinions for us. By doing away with the State Legis-
latures we would knock on the head those reactionary
Legislative Councils. We would also do away with the
Imported Figureheads, the Agents-General, and many
superfluous frills now costing us so dearly.
Remember that the animosity to the Australian Federa-
tion or Commonwealth comes from the Calico Jimmy
push and sore-headed tinpot politicians who have been
left behind in the talking-shop at Macquarie-street.
There is not one AUSTRALIAN SENTIMENT in the
crowd. They are insensate parochialists, unworthy of
the least notice, unless it is to cast them into outer dark-
ness at the coming elections. They are men of too paltry
minds to grasp the National phase of politics. These
people who now howl against the Commonwealth Govern-
ment have been disappointed in Federation, as they had
hoped that the Plutocratic boodling crowd would have
been installed in power ; but, instead, the Democratic or
People’s Party triumphed. Hence those tears.
The Australian people would save a quarter of a mil-
lion sterling per annum alone by abolishing the six State
Legislatures, not to mention the blessing of being rid of
a crowd of small-minded legislators. Remember, the
People elect every member of the Commonwealth Go-
vernment, both House of Representatives and Senate,
and if we put the wrong men in it is our funeral. In the
States we have no voice in electing the Councils, and no
power over them. They are simply there to thwart the
Democratic aspirations of the people. If, instead of
yelping about the Capital Site and trying to restrict it to
the size of a cabbage garden as near as possible to Sydney,
we n-ave the whole State as a site for the Federal Capital,
and abolished the Sydney talking shop, then what is maud-
linly termed the Mother State would be in the first position,
beino- the chief State of the Federation, with the supreme
Government. Then there would be some chance to see
about making Australia ready to defend itself, to popu-
late the island by making work for people in making our
own goods, and a hundred other things of vital import-
ance. As it is, there is little done, and we are deafened
with the dismal jeremiads of the un-Australian or anti-
Australian crowd, masquerading as Liberals, &c. Most of
these people would as soon be under Japanese as British
rule, and they are one and all potential National Scouts.
We ask you to remember that by Unification, or one
Government for the whole Island, you would get rid of
about 500 of the common or garden politicians ; at least
500 of the Upper House fossils, who are not paid and are
dear at the price ; six imported Figureheads, costing at
least £lOO,OOO per annum ; six Agents-General, costing at
least £20,000 per annum ; and numerous other frills which
are neither useful nor ornamental. In all, you would save
at least £250,000 per year, which would go to pay Cohen
some of the vast debt owing to him, or for an Australian
Navy. There would then be some chance of National
business going ahead when the parochial crowd had been
sat upon, none of whom have a soul above buttons.
Now, paste this last part in your hats. Do not vote for
a pro-nigger advocate, who tells you the way to get rich
is to give what little work you have to the nigger ; the
Foreign Trader, who tells you you will get rioh by buying
your goods from any country but your own ; the Sectarian
Savage, who believes in dividing the people into factions ;
the man who favors Trusts and Monopolies ; and you can
hardly make a mistake 99 times out of 100 if you add
Lawyers to the list. All these are noxious animals
(politically), and should be treated as such.
