A Side-Light on Bent.
Bent’s abortive attempt to provide Attorney-
General Davies with a present judgeship and a
subsequent retiring pension has raised a ques-
tion as to whether there is any need for appoint-
ing anybody to the billet which Iceberg Irvine
and Davies (under pressure) have lately declined.
The work of the Victorian Supreme Court is a
diminishing quantity. Litigants are fewer than
of yore, because the public is more wary of having
its leg pulled by legal advisers. 1 Also the juris-
diction of the County Court has been widened to
admit of cases under the Employers Liability
Act being settled with a reasonable regard tor
costs, and there is an Act of Parliament
passed a year ago, but not yet adopted by the
antagonistic Crown Law Department, which will
convert the County Court into a minor Criminal
Court. Moreover, the Federal High Court is
beginning to take appeal cases from the hands of
the local Full Court. In short, the old gag that
Chief Justice Madden and his Supreme colleagues
are overworked is no longer an effective gag.
It deceives nobody. Though they sometimes
appeared to have too much to do, a good
deal of what they did—each in turn—has
been work that demands no supreme ability from
the Judge—work that could be done just as well,
or even better, by a court of common sense. Cases
involving delicate points of law and matters of
grave interest to many people have been kept
waiting whilst an “over-worked” Judge of the
Supreme order was taking in the indelicate points
of divorce suits and trying to look shocked, whilst
he was merely noting details for a smoking-room
story. Under the improved legal system, which
the Crown Law Department has hitherto delayed,
these spicy divorce bits will fall to the lot of the
County Court Judges, and the banquets, as a rule,
will be less prolonged than of old. People are
asking how it happened that Bent’s “ Reform
Government came to pigeon-hole an Act of Par-
liament intended to lighten the duties of the
existing Supreme Court Judges. A theory readily
suggests itself. The Crown Law Department,
controlled by Attorney-General Davies, delibe-
rately ignored the new Act in order to give Bent
a color of excuse for appointing one of his political
pals to an unnecessary judicial billet!

“ T.D.” FROM London :
Here in Britain I have seen the making of yet another
Labor party. Its advent as a strong political force
closely resembles the inception of the party. which
entered the N.S.W. Parliament in the early nineties.
Men like John Burns and Burt and Keir Hardie had
proved a sound investment as Parliamentarians, and after
experimenting with them, the British workers thought
they would be justified in sending a definite party of the
same type to represent them. Their organisations got
quietly to work, and even the success which they obtained
at one or two bye-elections didn’t open the eyes of their
opponents to the strength of the new force, for these
bye-elections, notably that of Will Crooks at Woolwich,
were fought in essentially working-man constituencies.
There were several reasons why the old-established
parties should hold them in derision. First, the workers
hadn’t the advantage of an all-round manhood suffrage.
Second, it is the habit of the young lordlings
and esquires of Great Britain to nurse consti-
tuencies for four or five years before they yenture
to offer themselves as candidates. They start with the
support of their own families and their own tenants, and
the parsons over whom they hold the patronage, and
numerous other people of a parasitic tendency. Their
“ nursing ” consists in frequent attendance on the plat-
forms at local meetings and bun-struggles, repeated para-
graphic beslobberment in the local papers, and a liberal
distribution of guineas among village cricket clubs, parish
pump-erection committees and such institutions. None
of these things were possible to the prospective working
man candidate. Even had he the time to attend the
average village meeting, he would be forcibly ejected if
he attempted to take a place among the frock-coats on
the platform or to make a few remarks. Therefore, in
the majority of cases, the fact that the working man
cherished political ambitions was quite unknown, and he
entered the arena a month or two before the election as a
surprise and a subject for sneers. But he has arrived 50
strong or thereabouts (at time of writing there are many
seats still to be contested), and once the Labor party arn ves
as a definite entity, Australasian experience has proved
that it stays, and assumes increasing force with the
years. One thing the new force will make imperative,
and that is the more frequent attendance of members of
the British Commons in their places, and the conversion
of the mother of Parliaments from the status of an excel-
lent club to that of a national business machine. Seddon
and others have lasted as long as the extinct Salisbury-
cum-BALFOUR Ministry, but they did it by sheer work,
whilst Balfour and his crowd simply lay under the
Official Fig-tree, and eventually expired by effluxion of
time. With Labor in, a domestic policy will simply have
to be, whereas the Balfour Parliament utterly forgot that
there was any necessity for laws for home consumption.
+
Australia is abundantly cursed with a silly kind
of newspaper which holds that this is a poor, help-
less dog of a country which can never be expected
to do anything for itself. A recent case in point
was a screech in Sydney Evening News headed
“ Playing at Navy-making. 5 ’ Its burden was that
Australia, for some unexplained reason, can never
maintain any vessels for its own defence, and the
one argument advanced in support of the theory
was that Minister Playford used to grow cab-
bages. France, with nine times our population,
maintains about 360 serviceable vessels, so it looks
as if we could maintain 40 with no great strain.
Britain, with ten times our population, maintains
about 410 vessels, so again it looks as if we might
support 40. Holland, with not much more- than
our population, has nine serviceable battleships
and eight modern cruisers, and is building 53
torpedo craft. But the Australian, according to
the logic of the Evening Snooze, is a miserable,
low-down wretch, who can’t do anything for his
own defence Or the defence of the empire, and if
any evidence is needed the fact that Senator
Playford once ran a market-garden settles every-
thing. If Playford had once been a solicitor
the position might be different.
* *
The Federal Commission on Old Age Pensions
has completed its work, but it can’t be said that
it has completed it very well. It recommends a
scheme which is estimated to cost £1,400,000 or
£1.500,000 a year, and which may run to
£2^000,000 ; and all it says about finding the money
is that the cash should come out of the Consoli-
dated Revenue. Some futile suggestion has been
made to the effect that the thing is as broad as it
is long, because it is merely a question of changing
a State pension for a Federal one, but it isn’t; it
is a question of changing a pension system which
only affects two States for one which will affect
six. The Labor party is.solid for the Federal old
age pension, hut with its usual loose, way of: wink-
ing at the fiscal issue it doesn’t Seem to fyave
taken the trouble to become at all solid about
how to meet the cost of it. In fact,- some ’mem-
bers of the party, instead of raising extra revenue
to.meet the old age pension expenditure, .want to
burst up what revenue thifre is by taking off
Customs duties. Of course, w’ien the Braddon
clause expires, and the Commonwealth is. no
longer bound to return three-quarters of the tariff
• revenue to the States, the cost can be met easily
enough, so far as the Australian Treasury is con-
cerned, but that isn’t solving the financial
problem; it is merely passing it on to the
State Governments. And the more problems are
passed to the State Governments the more power-
ful the State Governments become, to the detri-
ment and weakening of the Federal authority.
This seems a good time for the Labor party to..get
a comprehensive financial policy. It plainly Oftfi't
get on much further without one.

The most humorous yarn told before the N.S.W.
Lands Commission is that of the official who
arrived at the Governor’s country residence in the
smallest hours of the morning, and wanted to
drag his Ex. out of bed that he might help to
defraud the country in his nightshirt. Under the
Act any State tenant. whose lease was signed
before 31st December, 1903, could get it re-
appraised, and, if he pulled the proper strings,
get the rent reduced. In other words, he could
keep the land and yet do the State a beautiful
light brown by backing out of part of the pay-
ment. This was a handy device for people who
knew how to work it; when Smith offered 6d.
rent in all good faith, Jones could get ahead of
him and offer Bd., and then, having secured the
land by offering Bd., he could get the rent reduced
to 3d. A batch of leases, on some of which a
very impudent dodge of this kind was to be
worked, was sent up to be signed by the
Governor on the last day of grace. The
messenger missed the train and when he
arrived distractedly at Moss Vale it was
one minute to midnight. Plainly he couldn’t
gallop three miles, drag the Governor out of bed,
explain things to him, and get his signature all in
sixty breathless seconds ; still he flew through the
darkness to the best of his ability, though flying
didn’t seem to be his strong point. About half-
past 12 he was tearing out his Ex’s door-bell by
the roots. About 1 o’clock he was explaining to
a bored secretary that the matter was urgent, and
making very little impression on the secretary.
Finally, the leases were signed sometime on
January 1, but they were made to falsely appear
as having been signed on December 31, and the
suffering country was thus made liable to fall in,
as usual. And it seems to have fallen in as usual.
During the past 12 months there have been
several healthy signs of a reaction against the
spirit of borrow and drift. Even the heavy old
Conservative journals are beginning to get the
notion that there is something wrong. A few
days ago the Australasian printed an article on
the subject in which it said •
The whole subject of the public debt of Australasia
demands a far more serious study than it is at present
receiving... The system (of refloat loans) can scarcely go
on for any considerable time longer, for it is evident that
the repayments will become so numerous and so heavy
as to be a constant source of anxiety, or even danger...
The position of the Treasurers who have the task of these
latter redemptions is not one to be envied... The policy
is one of drift...
And so on. Our public debt is some £230,000,000,
a truly enormous sum, and it comes due in huge
lumps of £15,000,000, £14,000,000, £32,000,000,
and so on. If a problem of this sort does not
“demand serious study” then nothing does.
But Carruthers is looking for a fresh capital site
so as not to have to look for Willis. Bent is recit-
ing cricket reminiscences, and Rason, whose State
is in the worst predicament of all, is sulking in a
corner, and won’t attend the Premiers’ conference
(where the debt will be, or ought to be, the prin-
cipal topic) because Carruthers put the stamp
on liis in vitation upside down.

Dave Richards writes
Have recently returned to Sydney, and cannot
help noticing tlie general usurpation of men’s billets
by the women and girls. Everywhere I go, where
formerly there were men there, is the skirted
person with the towsy head and the injured
expression hotels, restaurants, stores, curio shops,
libraries, agencies, everywhere. And everywhere, also,
is the man assisting the verandah or lamp-post to main-
an unintoxicated appearance. And yet there is the cry of
Freetrade, of a declining birth-rate, of more immigrants.
What common-sensible country wants other countries to
make its wares when its own workmen can find nothing
to do? And what self-respecting man will marry a girl
who earns the household toast and salad and ragout?
And what birth-rate can increase to any extent without
marriage? We want immigrants, although our own
people are now very much unemployed, and there is a
way of assassinating both chickens with one brick. Give
us a good hierh tariff not a poor, half-hearted,
knock-kneed, trembling thing, which doesn’t appear to
know whether it is Protection or an invitation to all and
sundry to dump their wares on our wharves and sell them
at ridiculously low prices, but a cheeky, well-made, un-
mistakable article that will represent the Alpha and
Omega of coming prosperity—and factories will spring up
“ like mushrooms, in the night,” and swallow all our own
unemployed and half of Europe’s. Australia, with its
present snivelling, doubtful, hybrid thing, is keeping
down, down, down, and will continue to do so till we
realise that we are men, and begin to supply our own
wants by supplying ourselves. Then it will go up, up,
up, and our “ great natural resources ” will be no longer
a butt for the world ; our markets and oilr harbor will be
full; our men will all be working with vigor and smiles,
and our women will be making babies’ clothes and boiling
the carrots.
The latest allegation of political patronage against
Yic. Premier Bent is too astounding to be
credited until it is proved in court. _ Rumor states
that the inquiry re the Sunday train accident on
the Gembrook line is to serve as an excuse for
Bent to provide another fat legal job for his old
friend Croker. The Railway Department
will be placed on trial, as it were —a
decidedly mock-looking trial—by the Go-
vernment, the Crown Solicitor appearing to
defend the Department, whilst Croker “instructs
counsel on behalf of the public.” This practically
means that “ the public,” who keep the State
railways going, must pay costs on both sides of
an inquiry in which a little third lot of parties—
the parties claiming damages for injuries received
—will also be represented. Where does the neces-
sity for Croker come in? All the damaging
facts that can be advanced against the Railways
Department will be brought forth by lawyer
Woolf appearing for a number of compensation-
seeking travellers. In representing the private
interests of these few citizens Woolf must neces-
sarily act on behalf of the general public. For that
matter he has already notified the Department of
his intention to attack the policy of narrow-gauge
lines. Will Mr. Croker, “on behalf of the pub-
lic,” also attack the Railway management, or will
he assist the Crown Solicitor in defending it?
What are the public to get from him in return for
his bill of costs ? The long-drawn-out Butter
Commission of last year has resulted thus far in
nothing of greater importance than Croker’s bill
for £6OOO. which has not yet been settled by
Bent. The re-employment of Croker suggests
that if his enormous claim lie taxed he is to find
consolation in the prompt settlement of another
big bill.
A recent advertisement in Sydney Telegraph
read :
ANTED two barmaids, £1 a week, for Ceylon.
It is up to the authorities to make inquiries into
such advertisements as this. The barmaid as we
know her is never heard of in Ceylon. Her place
is taken by the male nigger who goes about in
bare feet, wears a white petticoat and a jacket,
and has his long hair done up in a roll
behind with a circular comb stuck in it. (When
you first see him in front, the ends of the comb
look like two small horns, and you take him for
the devil.) But the nocturnal rickshaw man (the
only horse that turns round in the shafts and
argues with the passenger) takes you by night,
when you aren’t expecting anything of
the kind, to strange little dens in the
native quarter of Colombo, where you find
the white barmaid. Poor wretch ! She often
came fully expecting to be a barmaid, and not
having the money with which to get away, she
stayed and became something else. The house is
stuffy, ill-furnished and full of assorted humanity.
The almost naked horror in the shafts of the rick-
shaw (if you are out at night he always assumes
that the direction you gave him at the door of the
hotel was only intended to disguise your evil
purpose, and so he rushes you straight to the
stuffy emporium) turns round excitedly and
perspires at you and says “Nice gal!” Some-
times he amplifies it and says “French gal!
English gal ! Chinese gal ! Cingalee gal ! All
kind of gal! ” If the Australian steamer is just
in he suspects your origin and says “’Stralian
gal!” A baggy she-horror (generally some kind
of half-breed) comes to the door with something
like a slush lamp and urges you to come in. About
500 natives pour out from nowhere and encircle
you. And then, if you have any sense of smell
left, you smite hard on the nudity in the shafts
with your gamp, and drive him headlong on his
way, followed by the anguished yelp of the baggy,
elderly half-breed with the slush-lamp. “Wanted,
two Barmaids, £1 a week, for Ceylon ! ”

Japanese Count Hirosawa, who was educated at
Cambridge, has been re-visiting England. Says
he: —
There has been a great increase of luxury in the army
—some of the officers wear corsets —and the people seem
lighthearted over its condition. . . . With the purchase
of food from abroad, the concentration of the masses in
towns, and the absence of physical training or the teach-
ing of patriotism, the conditions are artificial. ... In
public life there are a great many men with axes to grind
in Parliament, in the army, arid on the press. ... The
Anglo-French entente indicates love of ease, rather than
strength of national character, and the suggestion that
Japanese troops might be used to protect the Indian
frontier could only come from a nation that knows its
decline has set in. . . . England is forsaking her maritime
habits—employing foreigners in her ships. Several
million people in England barely make a scanty living.
How could they be patriotic ?
Finally the Count expresses the opinion that
War with a first class, scientific Power would be disas-
trous to England.
When you consider that the Monkey has that
opinion about his ally, and then consider what his
opinion about himself is, it isn’t wonderful that he
curls his tail in the air and requests the exploded
lion to get a move on.
The photograph of Hongkong Chinese
merchant Chu Wun Man—who says that his
class must have equal privileges under the Aus-
tralian Immigration Restriction Act with Japs
and Hindus—is published in a Brisbane paper.
Chu Wun Man ! The name sounds cannibalistic.
“ Our highly-civilised ally,” the Jap, has
been demonstrating his value as an Australian
citizen. At Chillagoe (Q.), the other day, one
of him cut the throat of a white girl while
she was walking with a male friend, and
then suicided. The girl’s condition is critical.
And about the same day, over Fremantle (W.A.)
way, another of him cleft the skull of a constable
with a butcher’s cleaver, The constable’s con-
dition is also critical.
N.S.W.’s third Rhodes scholar, who
follows Barton and Rogers to Oxford, is Mungo
(not Mango) MacCallum, a clever son of
Mungo MacCullum, the diminutive Scotch pro-
fessor of English at Sydney University. The
deceased Rhodes laid great stress on the fact that
athletic prowess was to be considered in the selec-
tions just as much as erudition. MacCallum’s
title to fame in the field of Brawn and Muscle is
that he has occasionally been picked in the “ B ”
team of the ’Varsity Lawn Tennis Club, which
seems as feeble a title of that of C.M.G. Happily
Eing-pong is a bad dream of the past, or we might
ave found the runner-up in the P.P. Champion-
ship posing as a representative of Australian
athletics.
A campaign is now being carried on by the
Wolfe’s Schnapps people in Sydney against pub-
licans who sell other schnapps or gin when asked
for Wolfe’s. But the proprietors find their cam-
paign hampered by the refusal of the Board of
Health to give any help. What they ask is that
Board of Health inspectors should be allowed to
enforce the firm’s rights, provided that the firm
pays the. cost of their so doing. It is an
eminently reasonable request. Apart from
any question as to the merits of Wolfe’s Schnapps,
and its superiority or otherwise to Dog’s Bites or
the attacks of any other animal, the public
which asks for Wolfe’s Schnapps has a clear right
to get it, and nothing else ; and the Government
should help to see that the public does get what it
calls for, provided it calls before 11 p.m. and
doesn’t call on Sunday.

Certain recent “discoveries” in psychic
phenomena would lead us to believe that the
ghosts of the departed take an intelligent interest
in all that goes on on this planet. When the
name of a Dead and Gone is mentioned, he is
immediately on the spot to hear the said and see
the sayer. Consequently David Garrick’s spook
was no doubt at Sydney Palace Theatre on
Saturday night to witness the revival of the play
which has been pegged on to the distinguished
author-actor. As he is only dead some 150 years,
Garrick’s spook would be a quite young and highly
interesting wraith —quite a baby spook compared
to such old veterans as Cain and Goliath. Anyhow,
the ghostly Garrick could not feel affronted at
Julius Knight’s portrayal of him. In fact, one
(if one has a vagrant imagination) can imagine
ghost Garrick saying to his phantom wife (the
Garricks lived very happily), “Eva Maria, my
love, this young man does me very well. He
wears his brocades and satin small clothes and
ruffles superbly; and I’m not sure that his legs are
not better than mine were. Of course, upon no
consideration whatever, would I have made
such a dashed fool of myself as to sham
drunkenness to cure any whining girl of
her stage-struck love for me, but that’s
exactly how I would have treated yon saucy mer-
chant Gresham. Gadzooks, but was I ever like
that varlet when I was a wine merchant! I would
not be jealous, Eva Maria, if Mister Knight were
buried with me in Westminster Abbey. I might
spook the Dean and chapter with an idea to that
end.” And Eva Maria Violette, the celebrated
dancer, now Mrs. David Garrick, was doubtless
absorbed in watching Miss Unie Russell who
played the part of Violet Gresham, the girl who
loves the greatest actor and in the play
marries him. “As she is 150 years too
late, David, I don’t feel a bit jealous. But
I had more fire in me! Still I’m glad she
wasn’t earlier and in your company before we met.
That blue Juliet frock and her unsophisticated
eyes and fair hair had been rivals even to my
incomparable twirlings. Do you remember how
I used to spring off the point of one big toe ! ”
MADAME LYDIA YEAMANS-TITUS.
The rest of the company, being only imagi-
nary persons, would have no ghost-doubles to
watch them and criticise. But George Carey is
capital as the mullet-headed merchant who
tells Garrick that actors are rogues and
vagabonds by Act of Parliament, and Harry
Plimmer is horsey and dare-devil enough to be a
buckjumper or the rider of one. Also the other
members of the company do not shock the sensi-
bilities of the audience in any way. As a cur-
tain-raiser “ Comedy and Tragedy” comes on well
enough. Miss Maud Jeffries is most of the piece,
and raises the pulse of the audience to a state
of high fever with her tragic acting whilst her
stage husband (Plimmer) is fighting a duel with
the Regent of France. “ Wounded to the death,”
announces the gentleman, and all is happiness.
What would happen to an officer who slew the
Regent of France in a duel the dramatist sayeth
not.
* * * •
The co., which is received with great joy
by big audiences, will play “The Sign of the
Cross” on Thursday, and on Saturday night
“Beaucaire” will be revived.

At Sydney Boyal, ‘ ‘ La Fille du Tambour
Major ” runs more smoothly now for the week’s
practice the performers have had, and, as the
houses have been consistently good, they are
probably doomed to much more practice. There
is an unlimited demand in Sydney for light opera,
and any well-staged production can be sure of
good patronage.
“The Passport,” staged by the Brough-
Flemming Co., at Sydney Criterion, to fill a gap
whilst “Little Mary ” is being prepared, is a merry
farcical comedy hinging on the fact that Mr.
Sinclair, who is a widower, passes off Mrs. D’Arey,
who is a widow, as his wife, for passport purposes,
on the Russian frontier. It is generally under-
stood that almost anything may happen on the
Russian frontier, so that is a handy place at which
to locate an incident. In the fullness of time both
widow and widower marry again; and, on the
widower’s bridal morning, the lady who wasn’t his
wife, but who passed as such to defeat the bloated
Muscovite tyrant, turns up at the house. Here,
then, is a nice chance for complications. The re-
married widow is persuaded that her first husband
is still alive by folk who think Mr. Sinclair was
that first husband. The young bride is convinced
that her husband is a bigamist since the lady sup-
posed to be his first mate walks the earth. With
much comical explosive wrath the situations giggle
through three acts, and two minutes before cur-
tain-drop all is explained. A good deal of the
enjoyment gained by the average audience from a
farce of this description arises fromasense of rAental
superiority. The congregation knows that Mrs.
D’Arcy’s relations with Mr. Sinclair were quite
proper, and that Mr. D’Arcy is really dead, and
that Sinclair hasn’t committed the bigamy of
which he is darkly suspected ; but the people on
the stage blunder along wdthout a glimmer of the
intelligence that bulges out the foreheads of the
folk in the circle and stalls. And these folk feel
good over their superior cleverness, and have a
sensation of being akin to Sherlock Holmes.
* * * *
“The Passport” is briskly and cleverly
acted throughout. Miss Beatrice Day, on whom
the bulk of the burden falls, plays with a cheerful
gaiety that is very convincing. Admirable char-
acter sketches—so valuable in farce —are given by
Herbert Flemming and Edgar Payne, and Sin-
clair is a bright, natural character in the hands of
Carter Pickford. For Saturday next the play
about the pantry is announced.
** * *
The Shop Girl is coiling the last string
of imitation pearls about her neck, and pinning
the final item in bells and rolled-gold elephants on
her frock, and scurrying round to find “The
Boiled Bloater ; being a sequel to a Prince in
Disguise,” to read while going in per tram. The
Bulletin hasn’t any settled idea as to whether
J. C. Williamson’s Shop Girl, who will toil in the
Universal Providery at Her Majesty’s for the first
time next Saturday night, is this Sydney sort of
shop-girl; it makes the foregoing remarks with
an open mind, and in view of the necessity to say
something by way of introducing the new musical
comedy, in which, so it is promised, the whole
strength of the Comic Opera Co. will be em-
ployed.
** * *
Barnard’s Marionettes fill the biggest hole
made in the Sydney Tivoli bill by the withdrawals
at the end of last week, and fill it with laughter.
Afterwards, of course, you’ll swear that it was the
easily-amused ass sitting next you who was
laughing and spluttering—that what interested
you was the mechanical ingenuity of the business.
The Scotch Meisters supply a mostly new pro-
gramme of song, and the same distressingly old
programme of jokes. Apparently this first-class

quartette is trying to make Australia believe that,
after all, it is true that the Scotchman jokes with
difficulty. Fred. Bluett makes a warmly wel-
comed reappearance, and Mdlle. Cerito sings
three songs, mostly with the help of an immense
quantity of limelight. They are rather good
songs, but Mdlle. Cerito hasn’t the smallest idea
what to do with them.

“The Prodigal Son,” pruned of a little
verbiage and relieved of little Cissy’s painful song
since the first night, should have a prosperous
four weeks run at Melbourne Royal. Hall Caine’s
drama is in two respects an admirable change
from previous Drury Lane melodramas. The
interest is confined to the story, and the little group
of woe-stricken characters get into trouble with-
out outraging the audience’s sense of probability.


The argument as to Shakspeare’s brand of
politics and religion occurs at irregular intervals.
In regard to the modern genius the query is what
is his brand of whisky ? And the evidence shows
that genius as well as common sense plumps for
D.C.L. Shakspeare lived too soon or he would
certainly have plumped for D.C.L. whisky.*

“Sindbadthe Sailor,” late of Sydney, is
coming to the Princess’s on Saturday, with the
modest intention of staying for twelve nights only.
The same panto., or something similar under the
same title, was staged by Anderson at the Loyal
last year, since when Melbourne has probably
acquired an appetite for a brief revival thereof.
The panto, habit dies hard.

At Melbourne Town Hall, which is rarely
in evidence of late, a concert will be given by
Mis. Palmer on Wednesday (7th) next week.
The avowed object of the concert is Mrs. Gilbert
Wilson, and it seems that the lady, who is pre-
sumably a singer, will make her only appearance
on this promised occasion.

At the Opera House a pair of tambourine
spinners are the latest arrivals, and their short
turn is sufficiently amazing. The Primrose bri-
gade appear as Eight Pierrots some of the time
when they are not putting their best legs upper-
most. Also the abbreviated Crimmins and the
prolonged Miss Gore have changed the subject
matter of their duologue, and Miss Raymond is
devoting her brass instruments to other melodies
than she blew aforetime, and the rest of the pro-
gramme has introduced, some variations.
** & *
Adelaide theatres are beginning to wake
from their summer sleep. The Royal is showing
a brief season of “ Theatrescope,” with miles of
Wins that are for the most part obviously—‘though
most ingeniously- 11 faked. ” The Town Hall has
in a similar show that relied mostly on London
scenes. The artist who supplied the realistic

A N.S.W. LAND TRANSACTION.
Fancy picture of a Governor dragged out of bed to sign undated improvement leases in the
small hours of the morning. (Observe the perspiring messenger and the outraged flunkey.)
AT POVERTY POINT.
Francie Adler, the plump little Melbourne girl who last
appeared in Australia as a singer in the Rickards Co.,
when her warble was merely “ tricky,” is engaged for the
next grand opera season at Covent Garden. She shares
the distinction with three previous Australian artistes—
Melba, Lalia Miranda, and Frances Saville. Adler, now
known as Madame Alda, appears to be a further proof of
Marchesi’s capacity for bringing her pupils along if the
artistic feeling is born in them. The old lady must have
found Francie Adler a pretty tough subject at first. Her
grandmother, the accomplished Madame Simonsen
(mother of Frances Saville), could do very little with the
rebellious damsel, and she eventually refused to give her
any more lessons. The voice was there. Madame and
her husband, poor old Martin Simonsen, had a profound
admiration for their grandchild’s vocal organ. It had a
unique quality, they said, but the peculiarity of tone made
it more difficult to train than an ordinary sweet voice. And
Francie wouldn’t stick to her lessons. She went her own
wilful way, and when she presently got an opening in
Williamson’s chorus, and was given chances to come to
the front, her incisive voice often sounded out of harmony
with the others. On the Rickards stage she sang cheap
ballads, and put in her own variations. Front Europe,
she was first heard of as singing at concert halls in Ger-
many or somewhere. Then she devoted herself to serious
study under Marchesi before getting an engagement as
prima donna in Brussels. Two years work at the Brussels
theatre led to her London engagement. Adler, or Alda,
is presumably a lyric rather than a dramatic soprano,
more likely to appear as Michaela in “ Carmen,” and
Lola in “ Cavalleria Rusticana,” than to undertake a
heavy character with a woe-stricken top-note.
Marie Narelle’s success as a ballad singer in America is
evidenced by the fact that reserved seat tickets for her
farewell recital at the Astor Gallery, New York, were
issued at two dollars each. The farewell was fixed for
February loth, and the advt. stated that it was “ prior to
Miss Marie Narelle’s departure for a concert tour through
England, Ireland and Australia.” The songstress has
been giving her entertainment with only two assistants—
a German ’cellist and the ex-Melbourne damsel, Gladys
Ure, as pianist. There was some talk of the beauteous
Gladys travelling with Miss Narelle, but it seems that
she is now established in New York on her own. Two of
the songs she composed last year are highly spoken of,
and she is to appear in opera shortly.
“Melb.”: We are plunged into six weeks of A.N.A.
competitions for the sucking elocutionists, singers, piano
punchers and what not. A boundless propagation of
conceit, and a mischievous thing.
A new version of an old trick is played in the Islington
(London) current pantomime. Comedian sings a topical
song ; one verse of it reflects on the police. Constable
stands up in the stalls, and says that no reference to the
Force can be permitted. Manager comes on to the stage
to tell the bobby he has no right to be there, and there is
a proper time and place to object to anything that may
be wrong. Constable is very sorry, must do his duty, has
orders from the Inspector. He makes bis way on to the
stage. House on its feet, wildly excited. “ What shall
you do if I continue to sing the verse ? ” asks the comedian.
“ Sing it with you ! ” says the man in blue, and dances
furiously, and throws his helmet into the middle of next
week. Audience, which at first thought Magna Charta
had been burst up and that the “ liberty of the subject ”
was in 1 danger, sinks sadly into its seat and feels sold.
Armes Beaumont, the popular tenor, is now* 65 years of
age. His public musical experiences date from ’55, when
he made his debut in the Brunswick-street (Fitzroy)
Wesleyan Chapel. His first appearance in opera was in
“ The Bohemian Girl,” in Sydney, November ’63.
“The Modern Milo” writes from W.A. re “Poverty
Point ” par (B. 18/l/’06): “I thank you for your graceful
compliments, but wish to correct your correspondent’s
statement that I appeared in a recent panto, show at
Melbourne Cyclorama. My name, Pansy Montague, was
appropriated by some person, and dodgered (without my
permission) by a reckless management. Further, I was
not educated at Fitzroy, as stated. I schooled at Haw-
thorn, but that is by the way.”
Things from San Fran, papers about Nellie Stewart’s
first American appearance:—“Miss Stewart is Nell
Gwynne” . . . “ Better than Miss'Stewart’s Nell need not
be asked . . . Come to stay” . . . “ These Australians act
too much ” (it is Ashton Stevens who is writing this bit).
“ There is in their work an excess of elocution, facialism,
posture and gesture. This elaborate, finical, over-wrought
scheme of acting is at strange variance with the crisp
modernity of stage garniture and stage management.
And yet this acting, for what it is, is splendidly balanced.
The only question is this: Will Americans accept the
kind of acting ?... American curiosity will make a
success for the friends from Australia.” “A dream of
beauty . . . simply great... a darling ”... “ She co-
quetted with her audience until they were bound hand
and foot—hers without a struggle ” . . . “ She acts now,
not in the light key of the plausible, but in that peculiar
surcharged one of comic opera. Her voice is attuned to
an unnatural richness that at times becomes positively
sticky."
“0.P.” : Americans are more Australian than Aus-
tralians. Nellie Stewart was received with a eooee
chorus at the San Francisco theatre. I never beard a
cooee in an Australian one. The first cooee heard in
America is said to have been at a big meeting in Cinci-
natti. A Yank on the platform said to an Australian,
“ Any of your country people here ? ” The reply was, “ I
don’t know, but I’ll soon find out.” With that he gave a
cooee, which was echoed by a lady at the back of the hall.
The audience was greatly tickled, and kept on cooeeing.
Strauss’new opera, “Salome,” is said to break every
known rule in musical composition, and consequently
threatens to be the most marvellous production of the
age. But Strauss’ music is always of the surprising and
unconventional order. In “ Salome,” when 0 major is
sounded, he thinks nothing of letting a trombone play A
flat or D' sharp or any other old note that happens to
strike him ; and in one place he has five old Jews, singing ‘
$, $ and 4-4 time simultaneously. This is no doubt the
eccentricity of genius.
Said the San Francisco Bulletin, protesting against
them calling Sweet Nell “the Bernhardt of Australia”
(which is poor description, anyhow): “ This young woman
does not need to be tagged the anything of anywhere.
She is too complete a personality to want to be anybody
else.” To want to be, yes. But if that critic only knew,
Sweet Nell spends hours and hours fighting the born
mimic within her.
Septuagenarian George R. Ireland is to have a benefit
night at Melbourne Princess’s in the middle of April, and
if he can get a good assortment of items into the bill a
big audience should result. The veteran actor himself
will appear in one or more Shakspeare scenes with Miss
Chariot te Hemming, and probably his handsome daughter,
Miss Harrie, will be able to take part in the performance,
seeing that the Bland Holt Co. will just then be only
preparing for its re-appearance in Sydney.
The Wirths, when last heard of, were cursing the Perth
(W.A.) Railway service in language of the deepest dye.
The circus arrived at Freemantle t’other Sunday after-
noon, and was due to open at Perth on the Monday (bank
holiday) night. It could have walked the intervening 13
miles, and the steamship people, it seems, advised George
Wirth that he had better go on the march at once if he
wanted to make sure of a show thirty hours later. But
he trusted to the W.A. Railway Department and was
eventually left blaspheming. The menagerie and every-
thing appertaining to the circus was ready to b«
entrained by 11 o’clock on the Monday morning,
and then the trouble began. There was no engine
provided to do the shunting of the loaded trucks
until the elephants had done most of it, and the
scornful onlookers had jeered an engine-driver into action.
Then the “ narked ” Railway obstructionists protested
that the stuff was not securely loaded by the “ amateur”
circus hands, and the train was delayed until the job had
been subjected to red-tape requirements. Anyone who
has seen a bijj circus staff handling the luggage will
appreciate the bitterness of George Wirth’s shriek—“ Our
lashings not good enough ! Not blanky good enough !
I suppose our men have lashed more circus pro-
perty on to railway trucks than ever Westralian men
have seen." It goes without saying that the Wirthians
gave their local tormentors a choicer assortment of
tongue-lashings than they had ever previously heard.
Died, the other day, W. H. Vernon, who came to Aus-
tralia with Genevieve Ward, and supported that impress
sive actress in “ Forget-Me-not,” “ The Queen’s Favorite,”
and other plays. Vernon was broken up, mentally, for
Some time before his death, and the statement that his
age was only 71can be taken witha grain of salt. Vernon
narrowly escaped celebrity as an actor. He had a light,
buoyant style in any sort of comedy, farcical or senti-
mental, and he could put plenty of force and weight into
a display of passion or stern command. He wasn’t phy-
sically cut out for heroic characters. As a modern man
of the world he acted better than many a capable Ham-
let, but his figure and profile disqualified him for the
picturesque parts in classic drama.
“ Edyson”: Seeing “Utopia Limited,” one wonders that
it has been kept from the Australian stage for so long, and
wonders more at its failure in England and elsewhere.
That it has been a comparative failure is proved by the
infrequency of its re-appearances since the original Lon-
don production—if there has been any re-appearance-
Yet managerial enthusiasm enlarges upon the “ tremen-
dous initial success.” In truth, the fate of the opera in
London led to its being boxed in Australia for all these
years ; yet it has practically all the popular elements of
the big G. and S. money-makers, “Mikado” and “ Gon-
doliers.” There are even more numerous opportunities
for comedians than usual, and brightly acted by a good
set of musical merry men and a few engaging dancers,
“ Utopia ” would be a delicious entertainment. On this
Melbourne occasion it gets the treatment it deserves only
in a few features, and those comparatively minot;
“Falk”: Grieve to see “T.D.” (B. 4/l/’O6) joining'the
gang who throw petulant bricks at Richard Strauss. We
know the tone of them so well in the English papers, the
same old gang who howled at Wagner and jeered at Mere-
dith (they find it convenient to forget that now), and are
just wondering whether it isn’t time to save their face
about Ibsen. What gives away the anti-Straussians is the
white-hot fury that burns in them ; why not -stay away
and be happy? I can only give my feelings as against
“ T.D’s.” The first time I heard the “ Domestica,” I had
to stand. I can only take music, as a rule, in a long
chair, with a pipe and a tankard. This time, I was
further weighted with a big dinner and two pints o
Burton ale. I stood on my ten toes for 55 minutes (there
are no breaks between the movements), and nothing
moved but my hair. I couldn’t do more for the music
of the Spheres.
“ Melb.” : I never saw a play cut so deep into an audi-
ence as “ The Prodigal Son,” on the first night. “ The un-
foreseen only happens ” is a term that might be truly
applied. A Bland Holt drama without Bland is a trifle
like Hamlet without the Melancholy One: That was a
surprise. His clever acting, combined with managerial
prestige, upsets the equilibrium of a play. When Miss
Ross died in the second act, the Bland Ilolters, especially
the femininity, said, “We know all about that." Of
course she couldn’t be dead, with two acts more to come,
but she was, and a tong-bell kept up its dirge to remind
us. Baker really led .me to think of what G. V. Brooke
must have been like. How striking that scene where he
rushes out with his gun, to shoot his profligate brother.
He bursts into the room where the brother’s wife, his own
love, lies dead, and exclaims “Thank God ! Thank God ! ”
Why ? Two reasons. Because he is turned from his pur-
pose, and because she has gone where the wicked eease
from troubling and the weary are at rest.
“Two Seas”: I notice that a recent
arrival from Ceylon, named Beed, or Feed, is so
awfully concerned about the sufferings of the poor
white fireman on the mail boats that he wants to
tear him forcibly from his job and put a nigger in
his place. This newly-arrived person, named
Weed, or Greed, appears to imagine that the
white fireman is artfully snared in a trap, and
kept at his work by armed sentries. But he isn’t,
really; he actually applies for the billet, and if
Mr. Treed or Speed tears his job from him, and
doesn’t provide him with another one, he will
probably murder Mr. Bleed.
The recently-assaulted “Pastor*’ Abbott,
of Melbourne, palmist, phrenologist, faith-healer,
and successor to John Alexander Dowie, is a
suave, soft-voiced, white-haired, white-mous-
tached, and white-handed gentleman. For years
he acted as the parson at Holt’s matrimonial
agency, and makes it his boast that he has
married more people than any other person in the
country. He omits to mention that the Divorce
Court has probably unmarried more marriages of
his than of any other man.
It is announced that Nicholas Willis
quitted Australia in such a hurry that he left an
unpaid tailor’s bill of £5 7s. 6d. behind him. Still,
tailor Price, of King-street, Sydney, who is the
man at the other end of the bill, says he has no
fear about the money, and probably he is right.
Willis is a man who “ thinks in continents”—at
least he has thought about a pretty large section
of this continent—and he doesn’t go after small
game. Tailor Price doesn’t ascribe his bankruptcy
to Willis, but to “helping people in a friendly
way,” and to people helping themselves to goods
out of his shop in an unfriendly way. A drink
cure, run by a “ boy politician,” was one of his un-
lucky investments.
British politician T. Gibson Bowles, now
standing against Balfour for a London seat, on
Chamberlain, during the recent elections: “To
betray successively two leaders, to break up two
parties, this is a depth of political infamy not
hitherto sounded. In comparison with this the
Thugs of India are faithful friends, and Judas
Iscariot himself entitled to a crown of glory.”
Also, Lord Hugh Cecil : “Mr. Chamberlain did
not like people to disagree with him anywhere,
but to do so in Birmingham was something more
than wicked: it was profane—something like
brawling in church.” But, alas ! Chamberlain
got in by a big majority, while Cecil and Bowles
were defeated—the latter by a narrow seven
votes.
During 1905 the Commonwealth gained
2216 people by excess of arrivals over departures—
that is to say, it gained 4502 white people, and
lost 2286 colored people, leaving a total of 2216 to
the credit of White Australia. New South Wales
is still the greatest gainer by Federation ; it
gathered in a surplus of 9925 immigrants in 1905 !
the other States lost 7709. The surest test of a
country’s prosperity is the extent to which it
attracts immigrants, and this is N.S.W.’s record
in recent years :
1900 .. .. Loss, 1518 1903 .. .. Gain, 4539
1901 6913 1904 6597
1902 .. .. Gain, 6901 1905 9925
N.S.W. hasn’t struck such a patch of luck since
1891 —in fact, the whole period from 1892 to 1901
was a time of loss. It was only when N.S.W.
began to be ruined by Federation that things
looked up.
The Minister of Defence has bumped up
against the alleged fact that, if a small arms and
ammunition factory were established in Australia,
it would have to work on such a small scale, by
reason of the small population and the small local
demand, that the rifles it turned out would be
absurdly expensive. Well, let them be absurdly
expensive. It would be 20 times more expensive
if war came, and our supply of imported arms was
cut off, and this country, for want of that factory,
had to face the invader with road metal. Why
not reduce the expense by putting a prohibitive
duty on imported weapons, and catering for the
local private trade ? Also, why not try to work
up an export trade in shooting material ?
“E.W.” on a food matter :
Despite the cables confirming the comfortable condi-
tion of German workmen, recently published in the
Sydney Telegraph, that paper has discovered another
proof that Germany is being ruined owing to its high
tariff, for the other day it gravely informed its readers
that many of the wage-earners were “ driven to eat rye-
bread.” Now, all over northern Europe rye-bread is, and
always has been, the staple diet. It is eaten by rich and
poor, is found on every well-appointed table, is extremely
nutritious, building* up bone and muscle, and is largely
responsible for the splendid physique of the Teuton and
Slav races. The fine wheaten bread, the ordinary diet of
the Anglo-Saxon all the world over, is probably the cause
of that race steadily degenerating in stature, for the most
valuable portion of the grain has been carefully ex-
tracted. It fills and it may fatten, but it does not form
bone and sinew, and its consumption should be discour-
aged. Years ago it was common to meet bush families of
ten or a dozen children, all fine, burly fellows, standing,
when full-grown, 6ft. and over in their stockings. They
grew their own wheat, ground it in a kind of large coffee-
mill, and the product made excellent bread, containing
all that is necessary to build up a vigorous body. In
these days such families are the exception. At the age
of 14 or 16 the teeth begin to decay, and one rarely meets
a girl of 20 with a really sound set of molars. If part of
the price of Protection is to eat wholesome bread I, for
one, am willing.
From the first issue of Adelaide’s new
(and rather bright) weekly, The Gadfly ;
The Sydney people are fond of alluding to their country
as the “ Mother State,” but Great Grandmother would be
a ipord fitting prefix, judging by the senile outpourings
of Premier Joey Carruthers and his mob of stupid pro-
vinjcialists.; ’Tis a thousand pities that Australia never
made a complete job with a Bill for absolute unification
while the Federal enthusiasm was seething. Besides the
gre'at monetary saving by dispensing with twelve super-
fluous legislating machines and six toy Governors, the
suffering people would have been spared the incessant
blithering of little State panjandrums, who fear that
their half-pint measure is being obscured by the federal
bushel. The Sydney Joey waxes frantic in his efforts to
keep the limelight on his. microscopic person. He has
long since ceased to be amusing, and throughout the
other States there has been formed, with a large and
increasing membership, a society for being tired of New
South “ Wails.” The Sydney gang howls unceasingly of
the wealth of the great grandma State ; they claim that
it is ihe whole body of the Commonwealth, the lesser
units being merely tentacles ; but if the Federal Go-
vernment contemplates spending about ninepence out-
side of the great rich State, the Sydney Town Hall is
engaged for a number of importers to give a public
exhibition of foaming at the mouth over the attempt
to ruin the before-mentioned old lady. Over the federal
capital site question Carruthers is like a dancing Der-
vish when an expected patron passes him by. He
claims that the decision of a body of irresponsible State
Premiers, committed to a scrap of waste paper, and until
recently lost, overrides the Constitution. According to
Joseph, it was decided that the Federal Parliament
should meet in Anthony Hordern’s new Sydney shop, or
something equally ridiculous, -c ihat the provision in the
Bill fixing the conditions for Dhe establishment of the
capital are null and void. A S.A. suburban model Par-
liament some time ago decided that Federation was a
Hass, and the union should be dissolved. This decision
was entered in red ink in a threepenny exercise-book,
which if produced will bust up this portion of the Empiah
—that is, according to the logic of St. Joseph.

From Sydney Telegraph cable notes, Feb.
23 (re disturbances in Natal): —
The murderers were, it appears, semi-civilised members
of ’Mveli’s tribe, in other words, na'i- cs rendered insolent
by missionary teachings.
A fine tribute to the “missionary effort” for
which the Australian small boy is always being
urged to contribute his trouser button.
“ Viking” : There is nothing much wrong
with the Webley revolver issued to the police,
except the short barrel. Also, Eley’s cartridges
are the very best. But the weapon issued is too
frequently left at home. The fact is that it is
rather heavy, and so the intelligent officer spends
7s. 6d. of his pay on a cheap and ineffective pistol
with a l|-inch barrel. This is light and is not a
burden when worn. It will not shoot straight,
nor seriously hurt anyone that it does hit, but
that doesn’t seem to concern “ the force.” When
used with cheap ammunition it has no more effect
than a catapult. I have used every make of large
calibre Webley issued since 1872, and they were
all first-class weapons. I have an old ‘442 now,
and after 30 years’ use and neglect it is powerful
enough to stop the rush of a hill tribe. The shot
that didn’t hurt that kanaka who exploded in
Sydney may have come from a Webley, but a
bad Webley is so rare that any revolver-using man
will have his doubts about it.
* * * *
“OK.” complaineth with justifiable
bitterness :
The Australian Exhibition in Melbourne (which I hope
to see further popularised next year under the catchy
phrase of “ The Industries ”) struck me as being least
attractive in the one department where it might have
shone brilliantly. Among local town industries there is
none so popular with the noil-patriotic class as afternoon
tea, and this would naturally be expected to show its
highest form of development at an Australian Exhibition,
intended to impress the unpatriotic. Hut the afternoon
tea exhibit was utterly unworthy of Melbourne. I speak
from experience. Ordering refreshment for self and
ladv, I was served at a bare, uninviting table, with two
large thick cups of tea that had been previously
“ milked ”at the counter. The incidental sugar supply
harmonised with the tea service. Refreshments
were supplied on the typical Sunday-school picnic scale
of magnificence. A rather aggressive waitress, whose
appearance was in keeping with the cups and saucers,
brought a couple of small, dry scones, with some dabs of
yellow butter sandwiched in the middle of them, and
made a peremptory demand for a shilling as she banged
the ugly delf plates on the table. I thought of what
might have been at this exhibition of Australian products
and manufactures : Artistic tables and chairs, repre-
senting local work in various kinds of Australian
timber. Pretty specimens of Australian glassware for
flowers and sugar—the best Queensland sugar—and
milk, and if there is not yet any fine pottery
work in this country [But there is.—Ed. B.], the local
tinware might have been fetchingly represented by
glorified pannikins and embossed platters. Also, the tea
might have been served in smart little teapots by charm-
ing Australian girls. The best products of local industry
in any country are the girls, and they should be repre-
sented by choice specimens at a national exhibition.
* * * *
At Mackay (Q.), recently, one Mundeen, a
gentleman of color, joined matrimonial hands with
a Danish girl. Before they breathed as one the
coffee-colored sugar-planter put £lOO into the bank
to the credit of his prospective bride. In addition
he gave her mother and all the members of her
family complete outfits of clothing, and promised
his wife a White Australian pony and a set of
silver-plated harness. The marriage festivities
lasted three days, and were attended by all the
Butta Singhs within a radius of many miles.
Mundeen and his girl were married by a parson, as
is usual in such cases. There is badly wanted in
White Australia a law against “ mixed marriages ”
of this sort. Some years ago an aborigine turned
up at Singleton (N.S.W.) with a young white girl,
and asked the local Registrar to be the Voice that
Breathed o’er Eden in their case. The officer
refused to Breathe. Straightway they went to
the study of a local Wesleyan parson, and he per-
petrated the official deed.
* * * •
Richard Seddon, junior, of Maoriland,
one time a captain in the Boer unpleasantness,
was lately presented at Wellington with an
address and £7OO in cash. The affair took place
at a smoke concert at which the old man’s
brother diggers and political supporters gathered
in great force, but the farm-burners who went to
Africa with the hero of the evening were a very
thin gathering. The ex-looters feel bitter about
young man Seddon. Some of them are minus a
limb, some are minus other things, one is a
paralytic with a damaged spine, another is trying

these vigorous old thieves and combustibles think

SHE HAD HIS MEASURE.
“Go on; do you think I could deceive my own wife?"
“ Georye, darling, I know you couldn't; but I believe you are foolish enough to try it.”
The latest method of child-slaughter, as
explained by Coroner Candler, of Melbourne, is
by alcohol poisoning. A woman, whose husband
was “found drowned ” in the Victoria Dock last
September, took to drink. She took to it so suc-
cessfully that she stayed drunk, and didn’t even
bother to get sober on Jan. 5, when her child was
born. The poor infant was supplied with nutri-
ment that was mostly alcohol, and developed two
or three hyphenated diseases, of which it died.
Coroner Candler, after hearing the evidence at the
inquest, took 24 hours to think, and then fell on
the woman like a shipload of Nemesis. After
explaining what he thought of her, he committed
her for trial for manslaughter, and fixed a bail
that was virtually prohibitive. She will probably
be acquitted by a jury ; but the coroner has at
least set a new precedent going.

NORTHERN WAIROA HOSPITAL.
We, the undersigned Medical Staff of the Northern
Wairoa hospital, beg to intimate to intending appli-
cants for the positions of Medical Superintendent and
Assistant Medical Officer to the above Institution,
that we have resigned our positions owing to the
regulations and bylaws adopted by the Board of Trus-
tees. We also beg to notify intending applicants that
we are already settled in practice in the district, and
any gentleman accepting the position under exist-
ing circumstances will not be entitled to any assist-
ance from us. Intending applicants are invited to
communicate with one of us.
JAMES C. COLLINS,
WILLIAM 11. HORTON,
Medical Staff.
This effort on the part of two medical trades-
unionists to “picket” their old job and intimi-
date blacklegs from coming to it probably deserves
sympathy (The Bulletin doesn’t know the facts
of the case, but gives the trades-unionists the
benefit of the assumption that their case is a
good one). But supposing these two gentlemen
were coal-mihers and not doctors, what wouldn’t
the Herald have shrieked about conspiracy and
intimidation ?
A Victorian P.M. —one Burrowes —lately
exposed the fallacy that the Law “ regards every
man as innocent until he is proved guilty.” An
accused person at Warracknabeal, who, being on
hail, had twice come all the way from Melbourne
to answer a charge, only to have the case adjourned
because the Government analyst was too busy to
test certain matters, mildly asked if he had any
claim for expenses. Burrowes said he had “ never
had such a request made to him, before and
accused must have forgotten himself ” ; but to the
ordinary citizen it looks as if the Law had forgotten
itself and forgotten the accused as well. In fact, it
apparently forgot everything but the prosecution;
that was looked after all right. Suppose, the next
time the unpunctual Law calls on that man to
come all the way from Melbourne to Warrackna-
beal on a fool’s errand he hasn’t the money to pay
his fare —what then ?

EASTER CAMPS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ The Commonwealth Military Board has approved of
the following-Easter camps of training.”— The Age, 6/2<’o6.
There’s a stir up at the barracks, which for months have
been half dumb,
For the summertime is passin’ and the autumn’s nearly
come ;
There’s rustlin’ in the offices, and bustlin’ on the square,
And officers and others wear a hurried, flurried air.
The Commandant’s a-callin’ for returns, and maps, and
plans;
The Stores Department’s checking stores from guns to
billy-cans ;
And the D.A.A.G.’s cussin’; and the O.C.A. fair ramps—
All the dazzlin’ staff’s a-fussin’—gettin’ ready for the
comps.
Yes ; the Easter camps are cornin’,
That’s the reason things are hummin’,
We are goin’ to play at soldiers—goin’ to have our annual
treat;
It’s our regular occasion
For “ preparin’ for invasion,”
And we’re goin’ to learn to paralyse the blokes we’ve got
to meet.
Every mornin’ there’s a lev6e from some bold militia
corps,
Chiefly adjutants and colonels, waitin’ at the general’s
door ;
They are showe'd in, lookin’ solemn, and they find him in
his chair,
And they line up in a column with a highly martial air.
And they listen most respectful while he tells them of his
plan
To “ reorganise the army ” and knock feathers off Japan !
And he asks them “What’s your muster? Is your
corps a doin’ well ? ”
And they answer in a fluster “ they can hardly rightly
tell.”
Or perhaps they mention sadly
That the corps’s been treated badly ;
That it hasn’t got equipment nor a proper place to drill ;
That they need four hundred rifles,
And a thousand other trifles,
And practical encouragement before their ranks ’ll fill.
Then the Commandant dismisses ’em, and tells ’em with
a snort,
That “the wisest thing that they can do is send in a re-
port”;
And adds that they should “ send it to the D.A-Q-M.G.,
Who will read and recommend it, and then forward it to
ME.”
And so the deputations—and the mornings—pass away,
Full of plans and consultations for our cornin’ Easter
fray;
And the whole Defence Department and the staffs dig
and explore,
And examine—to discover what they always knew be-
fore !
That we’re short of ammunition—
What we have’s not in condition —
That we need ten thousand saddles, and twelve batteries
of guns ;
That we cannot make a cartridge,
Or a gun to shoot a partridge,
Let alone to arm Australia’s hundred thousand fighting
sons 1
And out upon the barracks square they’re marchin
round and round ;
You can tell they’re gravel-crushin’, half-a-mile off, by the
sound;
And it’s “ As you were ! ” and “ ’Tenshun ! ” and “ Form
fours ! ” and “ Squad, salute ! ”
(There’s an officer a-passin’)—but that don’t teach men to
shoot! ”
And it’s “ Dress there, Number Twenty ! ” and it’s “ Stand
up, Seventeen !”
“ Ceremonial drill ” there’s plenty—and thank God for the
canteen
When it’s hundreds in the shade—and there ain’t no
shade ouside !
Ah, they teach them all about parade, but never how to
ride 1
Yes; the sergeant-major drills ’em,
And the captain nearly kills ’em,
Till they get set up so stiff and straight you’d think
they’d got the cramps !
But their marchin’s simply splendid,
“ Open order ” or “ extended ; ”
Ho 1 we take the bun for marchin’ at our pleasant Easter
Camps 1
Then last —but no means least—of all, our gallant volun-
teers
Are “ gettin’ ready for the camps,” as they have done for
years.
They’re packin’ up their uniforms, their beds, their
kitchen gear,
With demijohns of whisky and a cask or two of beer ;
They’re takin’ patent-leather boots, pyjamas and cigars,
And mandolins and violins to serenade the stars ;
They’re takin’ hymn and prayer books in the more re-
ligious corps,
For a hymn-book is so handy when you're goin’ to train
for war 1
With all this par’phernalia
They’re goin’ to save Australia—
Goin’ to learn to paralyse the Germans, Japs, and other
scamps.
Heaven help the hapless foeman,
Chow or Russian, Greek or Roman,
Who dares to face our army when it’s holdin’ Easter
camps.
VICTORIA.
O. C. CABOT.

The young lady with the_improper story,
who has been quiescent for a while, bobbed up
once more in Melbourne last week. The epi-
sode occurred at Macedon, and the bay and girl
concerned—he was 18 and she 15J—-sang in a local
church choir, and philandered on the way home.
That was her tale, though she made no mention of
the circumstance until some months later, when it
became urgently necessary to make an explana-
tion of some sort. The youth denied the charge ;
it was practically his word against hers, and hers
would probably have stood but for a severe cross-
examination. Therein the blessed damozel
admitted that, when previously in service at Mal-
vern, “she on one occasion thought she was walk-
ing out with a, man, but she had since thought it
was imagination from over-strained nerves.” The
interesting child further said she had told a
fellow-servant at Macedon that she had been
married while in Malvern (cetat 14 !), mentioned
her husband’s name and showed a wedding ring
—she thought now that she had imagined this like-
wise. Also, she had kissed various boys at sundry
times and seasons and had smoked cigars “to
cure her nervousness.’’ Altogether she appeared
such an irresponsible, imaginative, hazy person
that the jury acquitted the youth. If that girl
can write as well as she can talk she would make
a desirable acquisition to the Stinking Fish party,
which lives by imagining things of one kind and
another.

The outcome of a bogus marriage, at Wel-
lington (M.L.), a while back, was a civil
ease, in which damages were claimed by
the woman, a simple-minded creature
who couldn’t read or write, or tell
the time, or the months of the
year. She was induced to
marry the defendant, a fire-
man, at an evening party ;
and, after the ceremony,
she parted up coin
for beer, the crowd
irrigating at her
expense. Nothing
further seems to
have happened as
the result of the
“marriage.” De-
fendant said the
party was having
a spree when the
marriage was sug-
gested. They
couldn’t get any-
one present to act
as parson, so the
pubbery adjoining
was raided, and an
old derelict with
venerable whiskers was
bagged and carted
along. A copy of Zola
was placed in his hand,
and a towel thrown across his arm.
He said, “Will you ’ave this bloke
for a ’usband ? ” She said “Yairs,”
and the ceremony was concluded.
The “parson” had a drink, and
everybody else had a drink. And
then the poor girl wanted the beer-
money back, with something else for
disappointment, when she found out
it wasn’t legal. The case was dis-
missed, of course'; nevertheless, the
sort of party which would play that
sort of trick on that sort of girl de-
serves to be hit collectively with
something hard and often.
“ Group of children assisted by the Bene-
volent Society of aST.S.W.” was one of Sydney
Telegraph’s latest exhibitions of pictorial enter-
prise. The group consisted of twelve youngsters,
mostly barefooted, and a dog. It is a low-down
thing to publish the portraits of the kiddies and
practically label them as paupers, arid it isn’t
professed that the dog gets aid from the Bene-
volent Society.
• * * *
Chief Justice Darley lately complained of
books from the N.S.W. Supreme Court Library
being taken out of the building. One volume, badly
wanted, was discovered in a solicitor’s office.
Which recalls a Melbourne incident of long ago.
In a matter before the Full Court, in which the
leading lights appeared, a case, alleged to be in
the books, was quoted as being on all fours with
the one being argued. The book was sent for, but
the librarian could not find it. For a breathless
hour or two the earth was ransacked for that
book. A well-know Q.C. was in the case and
pooh-poohed the idea of there being any such case
as alleged, and in the end the search was aban-
doned and judgment went for his clients. Then
the book was found. The Q.C. had been sitting
on it.

“ Hiphen ”: I possess
an athletic brother, who
goes in for physical cul-
ture, and looks for
trouble. On Christmas
eve he went to bed full
of good cheer and other
drinks, and was just
dropping to sleep, at
about 1.30, when he
heard burglars. Jump-
ing up, he went
through his exercises
to see that his muscles
were in good working
order, then crept to
the front door with
the stealth of a law-
yer crawling up to
a fee. On the mat
was a man —not a
very big one—so
my brother gave
him some ju-jitsu
and physical cul-
ture and jumped
on him often, and
hit him frequently,
and shook him at
intervals, till the
man lay down and
rested. Then my
brother put a few
grips that he had
been wanting to
practise for a long
time on him, and
dragged him inside
and lit the gas. To
our great disap-
pointment he was
not a thief—at least
not the sort my
brothcutook him for
• —only a Sal v ’army
specimen. We Could
hear his friends up the
street making noises and
Christmas carols on tin in-
struments, and taking up the
usual collection. My brother was
disgusted at wasting so'much physi-
cal culture on a mere Salv’armyist. He told me
that he was so surprised that if the man had asked
him to apologise he’d have done it. However, the
man was too weary to ask him, so we put him on the
pavement to be collected, then went to bed. We
found a collection box on the mat next morning.
We suppose it was left there as an atonement for
disturbing us.
* * * n
A case weighing 4|- tons, and alleged to
contain the bronze statue of the late Victoria, has
been dumped in Brisbane, and is waiting unopened
till they can scrape up a pedestal somehow. As
the result of much beating of the loyal tom-tom,
this pedestal will be provided, but it will be a
pretty mean affair. But, “Ah, she was a great
Queen!” they say as they drag out a reluctant
sixpence. Yet ask that man what year died this
great Queen for whom he has all this personal
affection, and 19 times out of 20 he can’t tell you.
What a hollow mockery the grief for dead
monarchs is, after all!

“Melb.”: Marcus Clarke’s room at the
Public Library, where he acted as sub-librarian,
has just been demolished and obliterated. It was
on the east side of the south Sculpture Gallery.
Cronies yet remember dropping in for a chat with
Marcus at his green table. Perchance one might
find Shillinglaw, Walstab, M'Crae, or Carrington
there, while Redmond Barry had been finding
some dry-as-dust job for poor Marcus.

has t’ have his eyes wide open t’ see what not t’

“Hexham ” : Hignoranfc of hany but Hing-
lish ’istory, as usual. Sydney Telegraph can’t
find any parallel to the forcible dissolution of the:
Hungarian Parliament nearer than Cromwell’s
time. One, much more recent and infinitely more
momentous m results, was the expulsion of the
French Council of Five Hundred by Bonaparte at
the point of the bayonet, which act virtually
abolished the First French Republic. The same
paper turned on its historical authority last week
to weep about the policy of “ruthless ex-
termination’’ of the natives “practised by
the Spaniards in Mexico.” There are 14,000,000'
Mexicans—3B per cent. Indians (presumably
“exterminated ’’), 43 per emit, mixed, and 19 per
cent, people of pure Spanish descent; and the
Indian has had equal privileges with the white
for the last 82 years, and nowadays is mostly
able to put his candidate into the Presidency.
Juarez and Diaz, two Indians, between them
filled the Presidential chair for the best part of 40
years. Why doesn’t the ’Graph hire somebodv
who knows history when he sees it ?
* * * *
“ 1.P.” on a church matter :
Re Socialism and ritualistic parsons (B. 15/2/’O6). Rev.
E. S. Hughes, whose incense-burning-and green chasubles
have caused so much controversy in Melbourne recently,
is editor of a church paper which The BraLETiN once
described as “the only genuinely religious paper in
Australia.” It has ventured publicly to defend a White
Australia, to proclaim the Labor party as the best factor
in Australian politics, and to incur the reputation of
being a pro-Boer by denouncing Jingo-imperialism. Also,
it barracks straight out for Socialism. The only church
in Sydney where similar “ ritualistic” services are held is
Christ Church, near the railway station ; and it is worth
noticing that the great bulk of its congregation is drawn
from the workers. Moreover, the church officials include
several energetic trades-unionists, among them a former
colleague of J. C. Watson as an office-bearer of the
Typographical Society. “ Brother Charles,” whose sen.
sible straight talk The Bulletin' has several times quoted
with approval, is another “ ritualist,” and wears chasu-
bles—-don't know what color. While these men fearlessly
hold radical opinions and live up to them, in welcome
contrast to the usual attitude of “ the churches ” on such
subjects, no Australian democrat will be inclined to join
in the “ anti-ritualistic ” campaign of parson Digby Berry
and the Argus.
* * * *
The S.A. local option carousal resulted in
a victory for the temperance-folk in every district
except Adelaide, the one place that could
afford the loss of a few pubs. At the end of
12 months, therefore, one-third of the present
hotel licenses will be refused! in Port Adelaide
and the suburbs, and any amount of property will
be left dry on the owners’ hands. Yet, in the
matter of pubs and drunks, S.A. is a compara-
tively holy and virtuous State. Statistics show
that its population of 372,000 averages only three
drunks a day, and that, while population has
increased in ten years by 52,000, the pubs have
decreased by 126.

DIANA’S VENGEANCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At a meeting of the Albert Park Ladies’ Swimming
Club, Miss Smith dived from the spring-board and struck
a youth who had concealed himself in the water. He is
suffering .from concussion of the brain. —News item.
"Watching where he didn’t oughter,
Paddled long a foolish wight;
" Chin-deep, hidden in the water,
Fascinated by the sight
* Of the costumes that they wore so
Daintily in girly swims,
•Clasping close the swelling torso,
Leaving bare the russet limbs— *
3 But he had to reckon with
Miss Smith.
Had he dreamed of a collision,
Welcome would the thought have been.
When an extra-charming vision
On the diving-board was seen,
With his eyes and mouth extended,
He was fairly overcome ;
And there suddenly descended
On his hollow cranium—
From the ether’s blue zenith —
Miss Smith.
Now the youths who’d study nature
Stay without the shark-proof laths,
Sudden penalties await sure
Those who trespass ih the baths.
Safer are the swelling lenses
Of the opera-glass instead ;
So they modify their frenzies
Lest there fall upon their head,
Like some old avenging myth—
Miss Smith.

Weak Heart for Years.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
“•One day I fell down 'in 'a''dead faint,” said
Mrs. Harry North, 55 Lauiiin-street, Cook’s Hill,
Newcastle. “I was living at Broken Hill at the
itime. My sister thoughtd'was dying, and rushed
•away for help. By the‘ time the doctor reached
the house I had come round a little—but I could
not speak. He said my'heart was so weak that it
was almost at a standstill. He warned me to be
very careful of myself, and told me never to do
anything that would excite me at all. Another
faint might mesvmm'y death. From that out, till
Hr. Williams’ Pink Pills cured me, I didn’t know
what good health was. Now, thank goodness, I
am never a-day sick. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
built me up dill I was stronger than ever. It’s
going onifewr years since they cured me—but my
ihear.t has mover given me a minute’s anxiety all
.that 'time. To-day my health could not be
better.
“ As a-girl, I was very healthy,” Mrs North went
•cm, “ but soon after I was twenty-one my health
began do break down. I worked too hard, and
•didn’it dake my meals regularly. This soon told on
ime. I grew weak and thin. Some days I don't
knowhow I got through my housework. I had
hardly 'the strength to raise a hand. My fingers
all turned yellow, and my nails went blue. As for
any face, I was afraid to look in the glass. There
wasn’t a particle of color about me. My eyes sank
•away back in my head, and my cheeks fell in.
Ifou never saw anyone look so ill and wretched.
“ Sometimes I could not feel my heart beating,
(even when I pressed my hand hard over it. At
•other times, it jumped and fluttered till I could
hardly draw my breath. These attacks came cn
without any warning. They might last for afe w
hours only—but very often I was laid up for
weeks. During these bad turns I was fit for no]
thing. I always had a feeling as if I was going to
•die. Many a night I went to bed feeling cert -.in
that I would never see morning.
“ The doctor told me my heart had hardly the
strength to keep beating,” Mrs. North added.
■“ A horrible smothering feeling came over me, and
I felt as if I couldn’t get enough air. I would have
to have sips of brandy and water every few minutes
to keep myself from fainting. That was the only
thing that kept my heart going at all. After the
•day that I fainted in Broken Hill, my life was
just hanging by a thread. My sister and I were
washing. Both of us were having a good laugh
when, all of a sudden, a sharp pain shot through
my heart—and it stopped dead. My body went
cold and rigid. I would have fallen headlong if
my sisterihad not caught me. She said that my
face turned deathly white, and that my eyes got
fixed. From that time, I never knew what hour
would benny last. There was always a chance of
me dropping off in a faint, and never coming out
of it allive.
This was my terrible state when I started Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People. I bought
•some at Hart and Firkin’s, the Newcastle chemists.
They suited me from the very first. They gave
me an appetite—a thing I hadn’t had for years.
Gradually they filled my heart with rich, [strong,
healthy blood, so that it worked regularly and
well. Then they put new strength into my
stomach, so that I was able to get all the
good out of everything I ate. My nerves
grew steady, and I slept better. To-day,
I am a strong and healthy woman. My heart
gives me no trouble now. I tell everyone that Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills saved my life.”
Now Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills build up the
strength in just one way—they actually make new
blood. That is all they do, but they do it well.
They don’t act on the bowels. They don’t bother
with mere symptoms. They won’t cure any
disease that isn’t caused by bad blood. But, then,
nearly all common diseases spring from that one
cause—anaemia, indigestion, biliousness, head-
aches, backaches, kidney trouble, lumbago, rheu-
matism, sciatica, neuralgia, nervousness, general
weakness and the special secret ailments that
growing girls and women do not like to talk about,
even to their doctors. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills,
by striking straight at the cause, cure all these
adments just as surely as they cured Mrs. North.

Williams’ Pink Pills. If your local retailer can.
