The WILD CAT
COLUMN
I wo of the three big American insurance offices
were run on the mutual principle, the same as the A.M.P.
and other Australian concerns ; and there has been just
as much scandal over them as over the one proprietary
office, the Equitable. The whole trouble has been that
they have been mutual offices in name only ; and tinally,
the directors, relying upon this, have been ■ able to do
things which other people are calling robbery
and spoliation. There is a lesson here for Austra-
lia. In connection with this movement to pur-
sue the business of the A.M.P. Outside Austral-
asia, the one cry on the directors’ side is
“ Trust the management! ” You produce figures to show
that the English expense rate is higher, the English
death-rate is higher, and the English interest rate lower ;
you produce incontestable figures to show that the result
of doing foreign business—if you get a large enough foreign
business to be worth mentioning—must be to reduce the
bonus paid to Australian policy-holders, who of course
have to share and share alike with the foreign policy-
holders ; you produce, after that, figures which cannot
be twisted or gainsaid showing that the Scottish Widows,
the greatest of the British offices, pays a 245. bonus
where the A.M.P. pays 40s. by confining itself to
Australasia. You make the chain of argument abso-
lutely complete ; and to commend the argument you
submit the names of men who have tested it; and found
it sound, and who therefore accept the conclusions —such
men as Chief Justice Parley, of N.S. Wales ; E. W. Knox,
manager of Australia’s greatest industrial concern, the
Colonial Sugar Refining Co. ; Peter Proctor, for a quarter
of a century financial editor of the S.M. Hurald. And to
all this comes the one set of replies—“ Trust the directors
or “ The management should know best ” ; or “ Won’t
you accept Mr. Teece’s assurance before that of irre-
sponsible critics ? ” Never a figure in disproof; never an
argument. The directors put forward a statement at
first; then general manager Teeee put one forward ; and
the two were in hopeless conflict. After that, never a word.
Just “ Trust the directors.” Arguments were put forward
against the proposal at the meeting in January. There
were some who went there with an open mind. “ Answer
the arguments against the extension,” pleaded a N.S. W.
ex-Minister. But they didn’t answer them. They did
not attempt to answer them. It was just “Trust the
directors 1” again.
It was just “Trust the directors!” in the
American offices, too.
What A.M.P. policy-holders are asked to do
by this parrot-cry is to give up the mutual principle, to
all intents and purposes. Instead of thinking the matter
out for themselves, and governing their own affairs,
they are urged to do no thinking for themselves, to have
no mind of their own, but to just blindly follow wherever
the directors may lead. And more than follow ; they are
urged to actually authorise the directors to go in a cer-
tain direction, and still not use their own judgment as to
whether that direction is the right one or not. “Trust
the directors.” Abrogate the mutual principle, and
destroy the very essence of the system of self-govern-
ment. That is what it means. And in America it
meant—well, there is no need to go into the things it has
meant. Anyway, only this week, the papers that have
been printing day after day the parrot-cry, “ Trust the
directors ! ” printed also a cablegram to the effect that
the joint committee of the U.S. Congress “denounced
the past administration of mutual offices,” and “ recom-
mended drastic reforms.” The beginning of it all was
when policy-holders yielded to just such stupid cries as
this “Trust the directors”; when they yielded to the
argument that the directors knew more about the matter
than they did, and so handed over control to the directors ;
when at every twist and turn they strengthened the
hands of the management and weakened their own.
To escape what has come of this prostitution of
the mutual principle in America every A.M.P. policy-
holder should form his own opinion in the matter, and
then act. And the last chance he will have of acting will
be on March 9. On that date the confirmatory meeting
will be held. Meantime those who have not sent in
proxies may do so. If they believe in extension, they
should send their votes to chairman A. W. Meeks, at the
A.M.P. office ; if they don’t believe in it, then the proxies
should go to Gerald R. Campbell, Denman Chambers,
Pliillip-street, Sydney.
* • * *
Concerning the expenses of some of the smaller
Australian insurance offices, the amalgamation of which
was advocated (B. l/2/’O6). From the last N.S.W. official
figures the Metropolitan’s ordinary' premium income was
quoted as £4552, and its expenses for the mixed business
(ordinary and industrial) as £13,436. This is, no doubt,
the best the “Statistical Register” can make of the
society’s returns ; and if the figures are misleading it
is the society’s own fault for not separating the ex-
penses of the ordinary and industrial business.
Bunching all its figures, as it does itself in its revenue
account, the Metropolitan’s premium income was last
year £17,337, and its expenses w'ere £12,412. But if the
people who insured in the Metropolitan got their insur-
ance at the same cost (for management) as the A.M.P..
National Mutual, and Citizens’ average, they would pay
£2730 instead of £12,412, and the other £9682 would be
available either for the reduction of premiums or the
increase of bonuses.
* * * *
Shipping people in Sydney—in Australia for
that matter—are looking forward with some curiosity to
March, when the new contracts for sea carriage of Broken
Hill ore come into force. This business, the most im-
portant after the Newcastle coal trade in the Common-
wealth, has hitherto been given to members of the
Steamship Owners’ Federation, but this year it has been
captured by Messrs. Scott Fell and Co. The surprised
and angry' companies which have lost the job are loud in
their opinion that Fells will be unable to carry on with
the fleet at their disposal unless they charter boats from
outside Australia. It is insinuated that the new' con-
tractors intend to run the service W'ith foreign-owned
steamers, relying on the fact that the strict requirements
of the local shipping Acts, as well as the Merchant Ship-
ping Act, cannot be applied to foreign boats. The firm
in question makes a specialty of running foreign-owned
tramp steamers, and already has had a tussle with the
Sydney authorities in the case of a vessel, the Tiberius.
Should they run German and Norwegian steamers in the
inter-State trade, their opponents hope to kick up a fine
row. Scott Fell and Co. are at present dumb as oysters,
and their intentions are waited with interest.
» * * »
Private enterprise is being squeezed by a ship-
ping combine, which has a monopoly of trade to Japan
and China. The regular traders have established a system
of rebates on freight to which all shippers are entitled,
with a proviso that such rebates are only' paid to the
shippers at the end of each year. At present there is a large
demand for Australian produce for export to Asiatic ports
—so large that the available tonnage is not equal to carrying
it. Yet shippers are practically' prevented from sending
cargo by steamers other than the combine’s, because all
accrued rebates are forfeited if at any' time consignors
send their goods by' other yessels ; so shippers have to re-
fuse orders after the combine’s ships are full. Yet we
maintain commercial agents in Japan.

This is rather ancient history ; but the Union directors
are a very gradual body—as the whole staff is pre-
pared to swear in the matter of that percentage
reduction of salary made some years ago, and not since
restored, in spite of the fact that the dividend has mean-
time been doubled. The Union is a fine bank in most
respects, well and conservatively managed ; and it is a
pity that (it may be because of the ignorance of its
English directors regarding Australian conditions) it
should be putting up the reputation of having
about the worst-treated and, consequently, most
dissatisfied staff in the Commonwealth. Though its pro-
fits are not what they were two or three years ago, when
banks made big earnings at the cost of very big risks
which fortunately turned out all right, the Union’s
figures continue remarkably good. Shareholders’ total
funds earned £7 16s. Id. p.c. per annum—a capital re-
turn which makes the mouth of almost every “ bloated
manufacturer ”in Australia water. He, pampered man,
has to be as little dissatisfied as he can on 2£ or 3 per
cent. —and often not that. While other banks have
had great difficulty in maintaining their advances,
the Union’s total at August was £12,600,929, agaibst
£12,497,141 in 1904, and £11,968,017 in 1963 ; and its
specie, &c., and cash balances amount to £3,878,819
against £3,800,914. Certainly it has had a considerable,
though not overwhelming, increase in deposits ; but the
balance has been lent out on short call in London—a
pretty poor use to put Australian money to, yet the sort
of thing that is bound to happen till Australia has a tariff
high enough to promote great manufacturing industries
which will call for the help of the financier. On Aug. 31st,
the Union alone had £1,125,000 at call and short notice in
London ; at its last balance, the Bank of N.S.W. had
£990,000 ; the Bank of Australasia in April had £2,693,000.
Australian money this, in nearly every case—£4,BoB,ooo
of it, averaging perhaps 2 per cent. And the Australia
to whom this belongs is paying 4 per cent.; and the same
J. Bull, to whom it lends for 2 per cent., is the man who
holds the mortgage.

Capital has been increased during the year, the result of
the acquisition of the Victoria brewery. That concern
now nominally belongs to a separate co., but the Carlton
is really the separate co. ; 75,000 new contributing
shares (paid to 25.) have been issued to provide
the capital for the new co. ; and on the Carlton’s assets
side appears “Shares in other co.’s, at cost, £6064.”
Profits show a serious shrinkage. Further, whereas in
former years the amount provided to cover bad and
doubtful debts was specified, this year there is only
silence on that point. It is possible, therefore, that, if
the same provision had been made as in previous years,
profits would show up even more poorly by comparison.
On the other hand, note that the sundry debtors item is
now less than usual; and that is good, for a bird in the
hand is worth two bills in the bush. Despite the bad
turn, progress is still being made in freeing the co. from
its awful load of boom trouble ; mortgage, debenture, and
other debts have been reduced by no less than £21,909
during the year. A doubtful feature is the continued
grow th of the new “ real estate ” item ; doubtful, because
it was into that pit that the co. fell before. Goodwill still
stands at £154,000 in the books ; and £154,000 for the
goodwill of a concern which couldn’t pay any div. for
years, and now only pays 3 per cent, on a’capital about
one-fifth the size of the goodwill, is a humorous item. The
old real estate has been wu-itten down bv over half, and
that accounts for £355,129 of the dead horse which the
energetic co. has to drag along. It is no use asking when
the co. intends to issue a clean balance-sheet. There are
only two ways in which that could be done, and either
way would involve the,writing down of the debenture
debt ; and the debenture-holders prefer to hang on and
wipe the froth off out of profit. So the balance-sheet
will probably continue as it is in spite of its humorous
features.

Six months ago the directors reported that “ the acqui-
sition of the business of the late George Adams will help
to increase the profits for the current period.” Evidently
something has gone wrong with the works, for there is
no trace of the business having been taken over. There
are two weak points in the situation. (1) The debt is
nearly one-and-a-quarter times greater than the capital.
Interest called last year for £6260. This is very much the
same as if the shareholders were ordinary shareholders
for £lOB,OOO, with £125,000 of 5 per cent, cumulative pref.
capital to mop up the profits in case of a slump of any
sort. (2) There is grave,doubt whether sufficient, or in
fact anything, is being written off for wear and tear of
plant, premises, hotels, etc. All that is shown as
charged is a small amount for repairs. On the other
hand, the co. has a fine estate of 4668 acres, “situate
just outside the town boundary,” as the balance-sheet
has been mentioning lately. And the inwardness of that
item will presently be seen when the co. offers the land
for sale in sub-division. What effect that will have on
the balance-sheet remains to be seen, for the co. doesn’t
mention the figure at which the estate stands in its
books.
* * *
It looks as though something must happen
among the ruins of the M’Cracken brewery (Melb.) before
the end of next month. As mentioned last week, the co.
has to pay 41 per cent, on £550,000 of debentures, and
for five years past it has only averaged enough profits to
pay £3 7s. ; so the other £1 3s. has had to come out of
capital. That sort of thing must end sooner or later, and
the directors say it must end next month, when it will
be impossible to send along the interest. An effort is
being made to hurriedly arrange things with the deben-
ture-holders, whose name is mostly J. Bull; but J. Bull is
at present argumentative.
* * * *
Howard Smith and Co., Ltd., an inter-State
ship-owning concern, which began about five years ago.
with a goodwill item of £40,000 :

The matter isn’t put as plainly as it might be in the
accounts. There was an original goodwill item—a paper
asset—of £40,000. Gradually this was reduced until, in
1904, it stood at £32,000. Then, last year, the
N.S. W. Government paid the co. £27,181 more for a wharf
than the co. had valued it at in its books ; and, in order to
completely wipe out the paper item, £4BlB was lifted out
of profits. But there is no trace of this special charge in
the profit and loss account ; there is an item, “ Earnings
of steamers, wharves, and investments, profit on coal
sales, less provision for contingencies”; and apparently
“ provision for contingencies ” covers the big slice which
has been bitten out. To that extent, therefore, things
are better than they look in the accounts. Whenever a
coastal shipping combine is mentioned, there is the reply
that, anyhow, the co.’s don’t pay outrageous dividends,
so that the monopoly can’t be very galling ; and to that
the retort is that the co.’s are hiding profits in all sorts
of corners. And here is something to suggest that
this last charge isn’t wholly unjustified. Besides the
amount shown above as written off there is, then, this
“ provision for contingencies.” In previous years this
sort of inner reserve was bundled up with sundry
creditors, in much the same way that some banks bundle
up “ bills payable and other liabilities,” “ other liabilities”
being the bit put aside to take off the rough edges of a
run of bad bargains when that experience happens along.
This year Howard Smiths adopt a rather better plan ;
they show ships and plant at cost (£413,457) on one side,
and on the other the total of all the depreciation, insur-
ance, and contingency funds (£219,731); debts due by the
co. are then kept separate. This means that in the co.’s
five years it has wiped off out of profits £40,000 goodwill,
accumulated £16,992 of ordinary reserves, and provided
£219,731 to cover five years’depreciation of steamers,
and their insurance. What “contingencies” may be
is not clear ; but apparently steamers, &c., could be
written down from £413,457't0 £193,726, or by 53 per
cent., and the co. could start off with no goodwill dead-
horse, but with £16,992 of solid reserves to help along
capital. Granted that this and most other inter-State
shipping co.’s have a heavy proportion of awful hulks on
their hands, this co. appears to have done remarkably fine
work in the way of preparing to write them down to nil.
In four years, besides paying the div., the co. has reduced
its debts by £20,834, accumulated £30,000 of fixed de-
posits, £42,119 of debentures, and added £40,494 to its
holdings in other co.’s, besides adding about £171,000
worth of ships to its own fleet. On the other hand, the
co. carries £202,823 of 5 per cent, cumulative pref. capi-
tal, and it is in a business liable to vigorous attack from
within or without almost any' moment. Such things
are calculated to give the ordinary-share holder some rest-
less moments.

But neither interest nor bonus is paid to shareholders till
their shares mature ; and £6937, in addition to the present
bonus, has been accumulated to meet these claims. This
to a slight extent is a set-off against the poverty of the
reserves ; anyway, it means that the society has the
use of this extra money until more capital is sup-
plied. But, of course, it doesn’t alter the fact
that all that the society has put aside to cover possible
losses on £39,932 of advances is £s9o—just 14 per cent, of
the total risk. If the advances went back on the society
to the extent of If per cent, (some Melb. societies were
struck to the extent of about 40 per cent.), it could only
pay the promised bonus by taking it out of capital
(because everything the society has is out on loan) ; and
that would make it a tougher job to earn the same
interest in the future. Under the system adopted by this
society, the 124 per cent, isn’t quite as gaudy a return as
it looks, because it isn’t paid for eight years, and acci-
dents may happen in eight years. Nevertheless the
results during the past year have been surprising ; £lO Bs.
a year paid in by weekly instalments during the past
eight years has resulted in an average draw in 1905 of
£144 13s.—the £B3 4s. paid in by instalments, and £6O 9s.
by way of interest and bonus. There are not many Aus-
tralian investments that have given that average for the
past eight years and then returned capital as good
as new. But Perth has had its rush of population ; and
the society will have to prepare now for a possible re-
action in property values. And it is just at this point
that the size of the reserves may cause anxiety.

June, 1903 .. 2778 15 ~ ~ 2574 204 47,060
Dec., 1903 .. 3280 15 „ „ 2969 311 47,371
June, 1904 .. 3309 15 „ „ 3264 45 47,416
Dec., 1904 .. 3630 15 „ „ 3312 318 47,734
Share premiums and suspense items 3309 51,043
June, 1905 .. 3835 15 p.c. = 3375 460 51,503
Dec., 1905 .. 3119 15 „ „ 3375 51,247
The reduction in net profits is due to a charge of £833
having been made upon the meter reserve fund. Other-
wise the figures are well sustained, the revenue
being £13,905 against £12,938 for the corresponding
half of 1904. The reserves are largely nominal ;
£29,175 is, for instance, set against “ renewal plant,
mains, &c.” Apparently this money has been spent;
and, as when you take out a worn-out main and put in a
new one you still have only one main that is worth any-
thing, this amount seemingly ought to be written off.
There is no depreciation reserve apart from this renewal
reserve ; and it is doubtful whether any renewal fund
(that is, the total spent on renewals) is equal to the actual
depreciation—which is only another way of saying that
old works, no matter how well patched, are never as good
as new works. If this co.’s renewal fund consists of
money actually spent on renewals, then the contributions
to that fund (about £5OO per half-year) ought to come off
the profits shown abovein order to arrive at the true profits;
and if that is done the dividend will not have been
earned. The position, however, is very uncertain—as
the positioned most gas co.’s is, chiefly because so much
of the assets are under the ground, and the extent of the
depreciation can only be guessed at. Gas shares may be
very good holding; but most times you want to know a
great deal more than the balance-sheet tells you before
plunging. In this case, until the other thing is proved,
it may be accepted that part of the div. is being now paid
out of capital accumulations. Note also (as collateral
evidence that the assets are over-valued and that the
reserves are not real) that in 1901 the assets earned over
30 per cent, gross, whereas now they earn only 26 per
cent, gross.

Launceston is blessed with a water supply which can be
harnessed up to make cheap electric light and power ;
and, as the municipality has taken that business in hand,
the gas co. has a hard row to hoe. For a couple of
it looked as though incandescent lighting might
put the gas co. in a stronger position, but recent figures
show that it is losing ground again. In the half-year
to June it wrote off £5OO ; in the past half-year it has
written nothing right off, and has only added £55 to con-
tingent fund. The latter, now amounting to £lBB3, is
included in reserves, though its true character is doubt-
ful. Besides paid capital of £50,000 the co. has now
£35,205 of debentures, an increase of £lBOO in the 12
months, but the overdraft has been reduced by £1244
{now £1806). Altogether, from the shareholders’ point
of view, the outlook is very drab.

Broken Hill South S.M. Co., whose shares
are supposed to have earned more com. for Australian
brokers during the past 12 months than any other stock
on the lists :
Written Re-
Profit. Dividends. off. serves.
Dec., 1903 £21,296 £lO,OOO £4,002 £51,345
June, 1904 41,283 20,000 20,300 52,328
Dec., 1904 51,119 20,000 6,937 76,510
June, 1905 53,596 35,000 6,205 88,901
Dec., 1905 71,149 35,000 19,081 105,969
There are 200,000 shares in the co., so a Is. div. means
£lO,OOO. Of the shares, 78,503 are still liable for 9s. 6d.
But the figures as to profits given above, and bound-
ing along from £21,296 to £71,149 within a short
two-and-a-half years, are liable to lead people into trouble
unless they pick at them and get under the surface. In
the first place, some unknown amount of the past half-
year’s profit, but supposed to be either £17,500 or a trifle
more, has been got from the sale of accumulated zinc-
bearing tailings. The directors bound themselves not to
disclose what the Zinc Corporation is paying the co. for
its tailings ; but it is pretty well known that the amount
is £35,000 a year for 200,000 tons a year until the present
lot (about 750,000 tons) is used up. Also it is known that
one lot of 100,000 tons has been paid for.' Deduct, then,
£17,500 from £71,149, and you get a fair comparison of
what the South did in the past half-year with what
it did in the half-years before that. This would give
£53,649 as the half-year’s ordinary profit, and that , is
only £53 more than was made in the preceding half. Yet
lead averaged £2 Is. 7d. per ton more and silver lfd. per
oz. more ; and on the strength of those rises the price of
the stock has been boomed till the mine was given a value
of just upon £900,000. The South turns out in its
concentrates over 11,000 tons of lead and 300,000 oz. silver
in six months. On that output those rises would
meant something handsome ; and, if there hadn’t been
anything else to consider, the jump in the share-values
would have been justified. But there evidently is some-
thing else to consider, since, instead of the profit from
silver and lead being £20,000 or £30,000 more than it was
in the previous half-year, it was only a beggarly £53
more.
What has become of all the money which that
rise in metals represents? The explanation is double-
barrelled. (1) The co. didn’t get a good part of it, in con-
sequence of the terms of its contract for the sale of silver-
lead concentrates. (2) What the co. did get extra was
mopped up by extra expenses or was neutralised by poorer
recoveries. Mining costs were 12s. o'Bd. per ton in the
past half against 10s. 10’2d. in the previous half (and that
means about £5600 less profit on an output of 95,269 tons),
milling costs were higher, less/jre was handled, and that
and the somewhat poorer extraction resulted in an output
of 11,197 tons lead against 11,896 tons, and 313,8450 z.
silver against 382,4210 z. To produce a better concentrate
cost £4 2s. 2-9 d. per ton in the first half of 1905 ;to pro-
duce a poorer one cost £4 13s. B'6d. per ton in the second
half. So between one thing and another the co. did no
better with the high prices than it did with the lower
prices of the previous half. But the market boomed
because prices were high, for all that.
• • • •
B.H. South’s ore reserves are estimated at
1,400,000 tons ; this is above the present bottom level.
Last half-year’s gross profit (apart from zinc) was 11s. Bd.
per ton. At that rate there is £816,600 of gross profit in
the mine—that is, profit without allowance for wear and
tear of plant. Add £122,500 for zinc tailings (700,000
tons) already produced ; total, £939,100. As at present
prices the market value is just under £700,000, that looks
all right. But two and a half years ago, when.the market
was a good way from its bottom, the gross profit was
only 7s. 2d. per ton ; and at that rate the gross profit in
the mine so far as developed (apart from zinc) would fall
to £501,650. In the half-year to Dec., 1902, the profit
was only 2s. sd. per ton ; total for 1,400,000 tons,
£172,500. Practically no improvements have been made
in silver-lead concentration meantime ; the lead recovery
is now a trifle better, and the silver recovery is a trifle
poorer. Almost the whole difference lies in the better
markets. Those markets may go back to where they
were ; they are, anyhow, always moving up and down.
But the market is valuing the mine as though the prices
were going to stay, just about up where they are till all

From advices to hand we learn that the Mount
Morgan Gold Mining Co. who lately installed five Hornsby
“Upright” Water Tube Boilers representing 2500 h.p.
have their new plant under steam and a phenomena!
saving is confidently expected in their coal bill and cost on
maintenance. It is gratifying to note that the
“Hornsby” boilers are fulfilling all that the makers
claimed, and with the excellent results obtained, com-
petent engineers have no hesitation in recommending
their general adoption. The firm have already created
one engineering record with their well-known patent
safety oil engine, carrying, as it has, everything before it
in all parts of the world, and we have no doubt they are
on the eve of creating a second record with their
“Upright” boiler. R. Hornsby and Sons, Ltd., Hay-
market, Sydney; Spencer-street, Melbourne.**

Important Notice to Mine Managers and
Users of Concentrators. —In the recent action for In-
fringement Wilfley v Card the decision of the Supreme
Court of Victoria did not merely refer to a Card top
which was altered without authority, but it “*d refer to a
Card Concentrator manufactured and worked at the New
Chum Consolidated Mine, Bendigo, under the supervision
and control of N. Guthridge Limited, and to a Card Con-
centrator exhibited at Long’s works, Footscray, which
was officially pointed out as the true Card construction
during the trial, and which defendant used tor exhibiting
to customers and making tests, and on which they gave
exhibitions to witnesses for the purposes of this trial. Ine
Judge specifically decided that the substance of the
Footscray table and of the No. 38 (New Chum Consolidated
Table) was the same as that of the plaintiff Wilfley and
were infringements. N. Guthridge Limited were ordered
to pay damages and costs, the costs were taxed and the
Sheriff seized the goods of N. Guthridge Limited in Mel-
bourne and Sydney, but as they were all claimed by the
receiver for the debenture holders the execution was re-
turned unsatisfied. Bankruptcy proceedings have been
instituted in the Supreme Court of N.S. Wales to wind up
N. Guthridge Limited. As N. Guthridge Limited are still
offering to indemnify purchasers, take notice that all
users of Card tables which infringe the Wilfley patent will
be proceeded against: The user is liable to an action as
well as the manufacturer. *

Wilfley Ore Concentrating Tables. Important
notice to mine managers and users of Concentrators.
The Supreme Court of Victoria has decided in the action
brought against N. Guthridge Limited that the well-
known Wilfley patent is valid, and has been infringed by
N. Guthridge Limited by the manufacture and sale of
tables which they call the Card Concentrating table.
The Court granted a certificate of the validity of the
Wilflev patent and ordered N. Guthridge Limited to pay
damages and costs of this action and granted an
iniunction restraining N. Guthridge Limited from in-
fringing the Wilfley patent. This is to give notice
that the Wilfley Ore Concentrator Syndicate Limited will
enfore its rights against all persons buying or selling or
otherwise dealing in or using such infringing Concentrat-
ing machines. Edward J. Rigby, Attorney for the \\ ilfley
Ore Concentrator Syndicate Limited, care of Austral
Otis Engineering Co., South Melbourne.*

, The printed Explanation is that the Broken
Bill mirte fires arose from “ shots.” Charges; it is ex-
plained, were fired, probably neglected, and timber thiis
set alight. Perhaps. It is the usual thing, anyhow, to
blame the workmen. Curious, nevertheless, that year
after year should pass without a fire, and that two should
then start within a day or two—one in the best place to
affect the group of mines at the North end ; the other to
affect the South end. Also, it is strange that they should
start just on the eve of a visit from a London-Melbourne
group of speculators, which visit, it was pretty well
known, was going to be followed by another “ boost ”
in the share market, principally in North, Junction
North, British, B.H. Prop., and Block 10. There has been
heavy Overselling in most of these stocks, and a further
advance meant ruin to some of the bears. The fires put
an immediate advance out of the question.
• * » *
Old Lewis Lloyd’s luck wasn’t passed on to
the English co. which took over his Burraga mine
(N.S.W.i in 1899; for it hasn’t got within sight of
a dividend since. For the best part of a year nego-
tiations have been going on between the co. and
the owners of Hockey’s unworked M.C.P., next
door, out of which the co. might at once have got
enough to keep the pot boiling ; but the Hockey trustees,
acting, it is said, on a report made by G. H. Blakemore,
of Cobar, opened their mouths too wide. The idea for a
long time has been that Hockey’s could only be worked
profitably from Lloyd’s ; so Lloyd’s people asked —
“ What’s the use of your property to anybody but us ? ”
That was when Lloyd’s Co. was feeling good and big.
But things have been so bad in the past six months (and
they were bad enough before) that it is Hockey’s turn to
ask now—“ What’s the use of what you’ve got without
what we’ve got?” Lloyd’s haven’t" quite got to that
depth of despair yet; but they are busy looking for
another mine somewhere else.
* * * *
Golden Fleece, Wyalong (N.S.W.), from two
reefs at the 500 ft., is raising soz. stuff in fair quantities,
and a record yield is expected this year. Co. is thinking
about a mill of its own. And it is time. First the stone
is carted to a public battery, 1J co 2 miles avvay ; then tail-
ings and concentrates go to the highest bidder, and have
mostly to be carted another long distance. Junction co.
is also tired of being without a crushing' plant. A pro-
perty, known as Hildebrand’s, that not long since went
begging, now owned by Phillips and party, is getting into
the first flight. Stone, which is being raised from a XOin.
reef at the 300 ft., compares favorably with any oxidised
ore ever raised on the field. Some Outside attention is
being paid to the Homeward Bound ; but there is nothing
to warrant a rush for those shares. The reef, though
good, is small and patchy, and the country is hard.
Lock, stock, and barrel, eight (8) men are now
employed on the whole Ooppabella (N.S. W.) silver field.

Following Westralian mines worked during
January at a loss: North Feather, producing 7810 z., loss
£300; Merton’s Reward, 8440 z., £300; Princess Royal,
6540 z., £6OO ; Craiggiemore, 2250 z., £7OO ; East Murchison
United, 13320 z., £4BO ; Golden Ridge, 4000 z., £4OO. Chief
features of the month are the continued drop in the
grade and profit of Great Fingall, Oroya, and Golden
Pole ; while Merton’s Reward and North Feather worked
at a loss. Kalgurli and Tower Hill have improved their
position, and expect to do still better.
* * * *
Tower Hill, Leonora (W.A.), joined the list of
producers in December, when 3360 tons were treated at a
profit of £730 ; while, during January, 4760 tons were
crushed for a profit of £2OOO. The 're is low grade, worth
only about 28s. a ton ; but the lode is very large, and is
being stoped out for a width of 35ft. Above the first
level reserves are estimated at 70,000 tons of 7dwt. ore,
or enough to last for twelve months. Co. is a reconstruc-
tion of the defunct Standard Exploration, and is in
1,000,000 shares, of which 930,000 are issued. Mine is
equipped with the old Paddington Consols’ 40-head mill,
thoroughly overhauled, and supplied With labor-saving
devices, and costs pan out at under 20s. It is expected
that 6000 tons will be treated monthly once the plant runs
smoothly. The large number of shares is against the con-
cern.
* * * *
Kalgurli (W.A.) continues to open up splen-
didly, and profits will shortly amount to £lB,OOO monthly,
or well over £200,000 a year. During the last three
months profits have been £16,200, £16,700, and £17,000.
It is expected they will average £lB,OOO monthly when
plant is properly going.- There is five years’ supply of ore,
and this should be greatly augmented when the 1150ft-
level is opened up. From 1140 ft. to 1160 ft. levels the ore
is worth over 60s. per ton, and at 1180 ft. can be taken as
worth 70s. A level is being opened up at 1150 ft, and
looks like largely increasing the reserves. Everything
points to the mine being one of the soundest in the State,
and it should easily pav 15s. a share.
* * * *
Boulder Main Reef recently sold its twice-
treated tailings to a local syndicate for a substantial
£6OOO, and smiled. Two filter presses were imported
from Germany, and a large cyanide plant of corrugated
iron was erected, at a cost of £4OOO. After two trial runs
the syndicate concluded that someone had blundered, as
the result was practically nil. In all some £ll,OOO or
£12,000 was spent by tbe syndicate. A similar case
occurred when the Queen Margaret (Bulong) tailings were
sold for £l5OO to an enthusiastic assayer. Moral: Don’t
buy tailing's on one man’s advice and assays, no matter
how good and honest the man.
Hannan’s Star, Kalgoorlie, is still struggling
along in the vain hope of finding something payable.
After running for months at a loss on ore worth 255. to
265., directors closed the mill at the end of October. The
“general managers” in London recommended doubling
the mill —what for they may know ; writer doesn’t. One
member of the firm is on the spot trying to discover how
to make bread out of stone, or gold out of Kalgoorlie
dust, Why not try Broken Hill, like Lake View Consols,
or Cobar, like Chaffers ; or, if co, wishes to foster W.A.
industry, why hot have a go at Phillips River, which is
the fashion at present, and will be much more so in the
near future ?
* * * *
Haiiiiaii’s Main Reef, Niagara (W.A.), seems to
have been as Unfortunate in its new property as in its old.
After keeping holders waiting for three years on various
pleas, crushing was started in November, when 1620 z.
materialised, Great things were predicted for December,
but only 26902. were won from 900 tons, and the profit
was £125 only, although costs panned out as low as 20s,
Id. per ton/ This poor result settled its fate, Really
good mines are just about as difficult to find in W.A. as
elsewhere ; and, as directors, engineers and brokers must
live, any hole in the ground is christened a mine so long
as the public will stand the racket.
* * * *
Queen Margaret, Bulong (W.A.), is again look-
ing sick, the rich dab below the 700 ft. level having been
rooted out. This dab yielded 1170 oz. in Nov., 7390 z. in
Dec., and only 198 oz. iii Jan. The mine is pretty well
pig-rooted above 700 ft., and no development can be done
below that unless a new shaft is sunk. Co.’s South Gipps-
land leases are nearly worked out, the last four returns
being 4110 z., 2770 z., 1790 z. and 109 oz. As mine didn’t
pay last year on better averages, it must be working at a
dead loss now. Whenever funds in hand give out, co.
must collapse, unless a fresh rich patch is come upon in
the old workings of the Margaret leases.
* * * *
Queensland Menzies (W.A.) has practically
decided to shift its plant to its new acquisition, the
Goodenough, at Picton. This mine has produced a lot of
gold, and was at one time under option to Boulder
Hamilton on behalf of Boulder No. 1; but the deal fell
through. Since then tributors have been making- much
money out of it. Mine is to be thoroughly sampled and
assayed by E. K. Beaumont, and it looks as if Queensland
Menzies mav have a new' lease of life.
* * *
Many years ago the Alick Forrest exploring
party, when crossing from the Fitzroy River into the
Northern Territory, found traces of gold close to the
border line, but the explorers were more concerned about
their next supply of water than about dry-blowing. The
country was a desert and they had tQ push on for their
lives. Since then very little has been done about the
find, but recently some prospectors pushed out from
Hall’s Creek, where gold was discovered more than 20
years ago, and they found several gold-bearing reefs,
specimens from which are distinctly good. Rain rarely
falls in those regions, but the season when it is least im-
probable is approaching, and numerous parties are get-
ting ready for a start. A Kimberley squatter, who was
one of the first to take up country there, considers the
prospects of the new field are excellent, but grimly pre-
dicts that a good many men will leave their bones behind
before the first battery is erected. The locality is known
as Tanana, and the Westralian Government is being asked
to proclaim it a goldfield. Natives are another obstacle
to the development of the country.
♦ * * *
Half-yearly meeting of No. 2 South Great
Eastern, Gympie (Q-), elected the same, old directors.
For a proposition with big potentialities, the directors
report is a pitiable inanition. When the specimen stone
vanished, and the amalgam fake blew out, the Board,
under pressure from shareholders, looked as if it would
work the show on the large scale it merited. Vigorous
development and economy in costs were all that were
required, for the gold is undoubtedly in the mine.
Present position is-Fixed deposit, £4OOO ; at credit of
current account, £4457 ; total, £8457. “ Working charges,
greatly reduced, now' stand at—mine 15s. 6Jd., battery
2s. 7Jd. ; equal to less than sJdwt. per ton.” Then this,
as the apex of the directors’ economies “ The past year
has been the first during which the trial of combining
secretarial duties with those of storekeeper has been
adopted.” Heavens, what an inspiration ! Now let The
Bulletin suggest combining the duties of braceman and,
say, chairman of directors.
Recent development in Mills United, Charters
Towers (Q.), shot the prop from under the D.D. School
Reserve, and there w'as a rush to get out. The rise going
up towards the School Reserve from No. 6 in Mills’ started
on 4ft. of stone. When up 50ft. the reef pinched to a
few inches on the foot and hanging walls, with diorite
between. Intrusion may be only a floater or “ blob,” but
where you find one you generally find several ; and
School Reserve cannot afford to have many of them, as,
even if it were all fit for the mill, the quantity of stone in
the corner of its lease will hardly spell dividends.

Carrington’s P.C., near Charters Towers, is
now going full time. Fortnightly clean-up, 325 tons,
gave £1125, with sands to come. Development in lower
ground has been suspended. Operations are confined to
where the stone is easily got, so as to keep the mill going
and liquidate the overdraft, about £3OOO. Last returns,
£3 9s. 3d. a ton, should pay. But what a drop from the
£26-a-ton stuff that the snow was boomed with 1
* * * *
Brilliant Extended, Charters Towers, should
shortly pay a div. In four weeks 3000 tons w'ere treated
for £8236, residues and concentrates included. On last
half-year’s costs (£2 os. 3d.) this leaves a profit of £2200 for
the month, or 14s. Bd. per ton. Wet season has disturbed
the cyanide work ; 950 tons were treated for £470. Not
much profit in these small lots, as costs on even a 14,000-
ton parcel run to ss. The improved methods of handling
residues that we heard so much of a while back are very
slow at putting in an appearance. The drying-ground, a
pretty good water-basin, has from 3ft. to 4ft. of tailings,
representing some thousands of tons, stacked on it. Wet
slimes and sands are carted in drays from the pits, and
tipped out again a quarter of a mile away, at the cyanide
works, on top of the partially dry stuff. It is tedious
work drying residues in the middle of a wet season ; so
the second pit of 2210 tons of tailings has been sold for
£4G6. No 12 east level is out 290 ft. ; in the last cut the
reef suddenly pinched from 4ft. to a few inches. No. 11
east, out 303 ft., is in formation. Is this the end of the
shoot of stone ? Why do these things happen when the
Brilliant North (Columbia) is unwatering its shaft to look
for the reef ?
* * * *
Golden Alexandra, Charters Towers, for the
half-year made a profit of £10,986 from 966 tons, and paid
£8750 in divs. (Is. 9d. per share). The 629 ft of develop-
ment work done proves that the reef is of the in-and-out
sort, but, still, is worth chasing, as the 966 tons averaged
£l6 17s. Bd. Costs run to £5 Bs. ; it needs to be a gold
mine to stand that. No. 4 S.E. level (the deepest) is out
130 ft. ; it has improved considerably of late. For a
stretch of 112 ft., 15in. of stone shows; it was classed as
poor, but goes about an ounce to the ton. Present end
shows Ift. of respectable-looking mineral stone, but it .
I may cut out as suddenly as it came in. Stone throughout
the mine varies from soz. to £oz. per ton ; but no esti-
mate can be made of quantity. The mine depends upon
these rich patches for dividends ; practically there is no
stone in reserve. Co. has 140 tons hauled towards the
next crushing, and more divs. will come, as there is
£5270 in hand.

threatens to overcome the water difficulty this time. By

1 .ok for several ounces to the ton in the bottom of the

Kidston’s ideas are not always Morgan’s, so the
new Queensland Government intends to use about £40,000
worth of the railway material lying at Croydon in the
Richmond-Cloncurry railway extension. Tenders have
been called for carting the rails. Treasurer Kidston can
see that those, rusting rails will keep almost as well on
the track as they will at Croydon, and, what to hinv is of
greater account, their use for the time being eases the
strain on the State’s purse. Croydon (N.Q.) people threat-
ened to slay Murphy, their M.L. A., for voting in favor of
the Etheridge railway resolutions. Now they promise to
kill Kidston for daring to use the rails stored at Croydon
for any other than a Croydon line. The “ indignation ”
meeting urged those rails should be used in
giving Croydon connection with a deep-water port.
Alternatively it was urged they should be used
for connecting with Georgetown, and so eventually
with an eastern port per the Etheridge line. Croydon
wants—(l) Deep-water port at Normanton. And Mature
says it can’t get one there. (2) Rail communication wit
Georgetown. Kidston shifts the rails. (3) The Etheridge
line, with Cairns for a port. Good ; but its call to the
east is late. (4) Murphy’s scalp for daring to support the
Etheridge line. Murphy is equally determined to keep
his own scalp. It’s a poor game being member tor
Croydon, and Murphy publicly expresses his willingness
to pass the job on to somebody else. Croydon s cry tor
rail connection with the east, when it already has a line
to the Gulf “port” of Normanton, is interesting, it
bears out a Bulletin contention that Gulf tratnc must
come east if it wants a live port. And yet the Queens-
land Silver Lead Mines proposes to dump its naii-a-
million scheme on the sand flats that shelter Burketown.
Well, it will be sorry.
•***■*
Graphite deposits of Mt. Bopple (Q.) are attract-
ing some notice, also a little venturesome capital, the
expenditure of which should be carefully watched.
Graphite beds are situated between Mt. Bopple and
Beacon Peak, Maryborough district, and certainly yielded
graphite of a class. Samples assay variously in fixed
carbon from 25 per cent, to 90 per cent., and in ash from
5 per cent, to 70 per cent., so the business is purely a
gamble. So far the Mt. Bopple graphite has been pro-
nounced by experts to be suited to use in foundry facings,
which is a similar report to that obtained on the graphite
mines at Underwood Station (N.S.W.). Geologists express
the opinion that better quality seams will be discovered
at Bopple as prospecting advances. Better stun will
certainly need to be picked up if promoters are to do any
good. The foundry-facing market is a poor and limited
one at best. „
* * * *
New Loch Fyne, Matlock (Vic.), lives on the
memory of a splendid past; and it isn’t finding that a
pleasant occupation. Staff has had to be shortened and
co. is putting through trial lots from different shoots of
ore to find out their real-worth. What has tied
the show up has been the putting in of
a long tunnel to really test the shoots in the
diorite dyke at depth. Whether it would not have been
cheaper to sink a shaft is not clear ; but the board seems
certain that the gold is underfoot. To raise cash to get
it the co. is going to take out the quartz left overhead.
There was talk about taking the whole of the dyke and
sending it through the battery, but the formation is just
below the possible grade. Besides, diorite is bad to
crush. As matters stand, New Loch Fyne seems to be in
the position reached long since by the Wood s 1 oint
mines which have worked the dykes. The shoots of gold
cut out at shallow depths, and are richest near the
junction of the dyke with the country rock.
* • * * *
Cassilis (Vic.) shareholders are a sad lot. Mine
has gone back, six months’ work having cut out all
the profits (£1600) brought forward, and landed the
banking account on the wrong side of the ledger to the
tune of £2OO. But that is not all. The co. had a clerk with
a taste for horses, and £374 besides has to be made up.
Still, the Board put the cart before the horse in
explaining affairs. The real trouble is the shortage of
gold ; what the co. needs forthwith is pluck and capital.
Every manager has wanted some new form of treatment,
or has set out to re-arrange tbe works; and the place is
skinned of timber for firewood. Cootes’ electric scheme
seems the only way to get out of the mess. De-
velopment work has been hampered by the inade-
quacy of the steam pressure, so the footage is down
to 1100 ft. against 1580 ft. two years ago. As the ore occurs
in lenses, some of which are very short, any falling o fl * n
development is fatal. “ Belt ahead ! ” must be tbemotto
as much as ever it was at the Lucknow mines ; but Cassilis
shareholders, from the Board down, seem shy about put-
ting the dibs on the table for progressive operations.
They have done well out of the mine, but only in a small
way ; whereas if they had subscribed working capital
liberally, they would have been reaping liberal returns
now instead of wailing.
•x * * *
The second advent of Glenfine South, Pitfield
(Vic.), recalls the sensationalism of the first advent. It
had been a languishing sort of an alluvial co. when
Chisholm cut quartz in his reef drive. As stone
showed gold, a big bulk sample was sent to Ballarat.
Shares had jigged a bit in anticipation, and the
speckers made merry for the feast. As the day
for the clean-up arrived, it began to dawn on the
shorts that there was a very strong contingent in the
market picking up scrip. Some of the speckers held to
their guns ; others funked and overbought, and so
saved their bacon. In connection with the first clean-
up from the deep ground last week, events were
repeated in all respects save one. There was the
same preliminary fluttering, and the same preliminary
hardening of prices. But, instead of a jump in values on
clean-up day, as on the first occasion, there was a rush to
sell. That took place on Thursday afternoon, and the
yield did not get to the public until Friday morning. The
yield was good, so what was the reason of the early
scuttle ? Looking at the mine in comparison with the
Great Southern, Rutherglen, there never was the least
cause why shares should rank equal witn those of that co.
Next, there isn’t enough wash proved to pay share value
in divs. Further, the ground is patchy and the bottom
irregular. Lastly, even if the mine was the best in the
State, experience of last week demonstrated that the boys
of the Ballarat brigade hold the fort, and it is wise to re-
member that they pick bones clean.
* * * *
Mutual Hills is a show near Derby (Tas.)
which it is said the Briseis ought to have nibbled in.
Perhaps so ; but the general opinion is that what Lindesay
Clark leaves the other fellow is welcome to. The crowd
which figured so largely in the Briseis ExtenUed-
Weldbrook floats is doing the pioneering. It was said
in the Argus that there was much noise and little result.
The gibe may have been deserved, but it is rather late in
the day to cast stones. The Briseis Extended was
allowed to pass; ditto the Weldbrook. Why then trouble
about the Mutual Hills? There is a time to sipite,
and that is when the public is on the feed.
But the public isn’t feeding now, and isn’t likely to until
the Weldbrook and the Briseis Extended show up as
mines. As the day when that will happen cannot
be computed, Mutual Hills can be left to flutter to-and-fro
in the little game of battledore and shuttlecock, played so
deftly by the talent of the Stock Exchanges.

“ Yanco” : Carruthers is going to have a surplus
of £500,000. I shouldn’t wonder. Besides selling Crown
lands, he has annexed the accumulated interest on the
deposits in School Savings Banks. These banks were
instituted some years ago, and, as no interest has been
available for depositors, the accumulated interest has
become a sort of a burden. This accrued interest, if it
doesn’t belong to the depositors whose money produced
it, would appear at any rate to belong to the schools
whence the money was banked ; but Joseph has set .ill
doubts as to its ownership on one side by collecting the
money and paying it into the consolidated revenue. Aiiy
man Who would do that sort of thing- in this sort Of
year ought to have a surplus.

“Arab” : Re remarks by “ Wyung ” (B.
28/12/’O5) on eels. The kind he mentions are
common in the Hunter River, at Morpeth (N. S. W.).
Have seen a big old-man eel with a mane down his
back like a horse. Used to catch them, years ago,
with a hand-line (they will take any bait). Used
to alsp catch them in a drum-net. Was rather
fond of a piece of eel nicely salted and boiled, and
used often to give one to a policeman who was
stationed at Morpeth at that time. He was jolly
fond of eel. Was at the old steam punt at the
Hinton crossing, one day, and saw Bob Brooks,
the boatman, land a tremendous one (6ft. long).
When Bob opened him, he found six kittens in his
interior. I suddenly lost all fondness for boiled
eel, and, after that, used to give all I caught to
my friend, the policeman. These eels have been
found travelling overland, through the lucerne
crops, on moonlight nights, when a heavy dew
was falling. Don’t know what they left the river
for, but think it was to look up a Federal capital
site.

S. A. Board of Agriculture has a bad
opinion of the travelling sand along the Coorong.
At a meeting in Adelaide last week it was pro-
phesied that the drift, unless checked, will fill up
the Coorong, and then attack the big stations on
the other side. Wholesale planting with marram
grass, regardless of expense (as done effectively on
a small scale —9 miles by 2 —at Fort Fairy) is
suggested. ,
“ G. ” : In a recent horse article, London
Daily Telegraph refers to the “Australian
whaler,” the writer evidently thinking of the
Murrumbidgee whaler, a speedy beast, as all real
Australians know, which is capable of carrying a
heavy burden, in what is technically known as a
bluey, as far as five miles between sunrise and
sundown. The whaler is so called because it is a
whale for work, and can subsist for as long as
three hours without a drink.
“ Homo ” : “Snag” (B. 8/2/’O6) says “The
fact that sore-teated or vicious cows are generally
called ‘ snags ’ may have something to do with the
title ‘ snagger ’.” So it has, combined with the
additional fact that the newly-arrived teat-puller
is generally so adroitly manoeuvred by older hands
that he gets hold of all the ill-tempered beasts—
those suffering from some real or imaginary griev-
ance, and meagrely endowed with the milk of
bovine kindness. Consequently he frequently
runs against a snag—some of them, indeed, are
“fair cows” to deal with —and, as every cowjuice
extractor lias to be initiated some time or other,
hence the designation. This is not merely a hasty
conclusion based on my own extensive knowledge.
In order to get evidence conclusive and complete,
I formed myself into a deputation and waited on
the highest cow authority in the State—Mr. Bent.
He received me courteously when he found my
visit was in no way connected with the vacant
Judgeship, and said “Rybuck. I was a bloomin’
‘ snagger ’ meself once, about 40 years ago, ’n’ can
bloomin’ well tell you all about it.” And he did.
He supplied me with numerous interesting remi-
niscences while so doing, but as they were mostly
irrelevant to the point at issue, it may be as well
to omit them here.

“Dave H. B.” : The action of various
deform N.S.W. personages in leaving the ter-
minus of the Narrabri-Walgett railway for so long
at Cryon has had disastrous results. There is
scarcely a blade of grass along the stock route to
the terminus. So stockowners have had to sit and
watch splendid bargains vanish, while the State
has lost hundreds of pounds in truck money,
whereas if the line had been carried on to
Walgett, the authorities could scarcely have met
the demand for trucks. The earthworks were
long since constructed to Walgett, apd every
week is seeing them washed or blown away, ■or
trodden down by stock. In the same way the
rails were laid to Cry on fully 12 months before
the line was opened, missing the whole of one
season’s wool clip. So is money wasted, while
the settler who has decided to combat the whirl-
wind, the drought, and the rabbit, looks in vain
for a quick, cheap, reliable way to the markets.

“ Werrimee ” : Ducks have cen-
tral depots, or headquarters, generally
a lake, lagoon, or large swamp. Hun-
dreds leave these waters at night, and
travel many miles over the surround-
ing country, dropping into little pools
and creeks, into tanks and dams, gilghi
holes and claypans—places where man
is the disturbing element by day.
They return to headquarters at dawn
or sunrise. Thus it comes that the
traveller often finds a duck on his
tent-line in the morning, and the
settler secures a brace in his waterhole
by taking a gun with him when going
for his matutinal bucket of water.
Other nocturnal visitors are the nan-
keen crane and the black swan. The
former appears between sundown and
dark; the latter on starlit nights.
Many a summer’s night I have lain
out on the soft grass, listening to the
shrill cries of the swans and the
whistling ducks, the whirr of wings,
the “flop-flop” of the flying-fox
mingling with the low, quick “ quack-
quack ”of the black duck. And many
a gun waits at flight-time, with
ominous muzzle pointing starwards,
ready to discharge a hail of lead into
the flying squad. Now and again you
hear a thud and a clatter on the
galvanised roof, as the deluded birds,
mistaking the silvery sheen of it for
water, swoop down upon it. A favorite
sport with travellers, when camping at
a waterhole, is catching black duck
with fishing lines. The line is thrown
across a selvage of weeds, watercress
or lily-leaves, so that the hook, baited
with grasshopper, cricket, or shrimp, hangs j ust
over the outer edge. The hook must be small, so
that the bird can swallow it, and the line light
and strong. Very often a strong pin acts as well
as an ordinary hook. The cocky’s tame ducks,
frequenting the adjacent waterhole, sometimes
get hooked by mistake and disappear mysteri-
ously.

“Hix” (Buenos Aires): —
Is there never a song of Australia ? In the back parlor
of a pub. in the Gaucholand Pampa, after other songs
were done, a Oriolla girl sang the Argentine anthem and
an all-nations chorus joined to yell “ Libertad, Libertad,
Liber tad.” We had the “ Marseillaise ” and “ Gor’save ”
and the “Star-Spangled Banner.” Then an Australian
harvester expert was called upon for the anthem of his
country. He hailed from Wagga, and he sang the “ Wild
Colonial Boy ” ! Give us something with music to carry
abroad. Verse is no use to us, exiled. We want some-
thing tuneful to croon on the Pampa when the planted
gum-tree ’minds us of home. Squatter Staughton came
here for ten days last year, and this year we had an
equally lengthy visit from George D. Meudell. Both went
home and wrote nasty things about this country. Trouble
is that in this country nobody is particularly interested
m kow-towing to wealthy Australians, and no one weeps
when they tell how Australia is going to the dogs. There .
is no audience for them and no prospective title. So they
quit and curse Argentina.

“ Nap ” : The amount of injury caused by
bush-fires, provided the homestead is saved, is
often nothing like what it is cracked up to be.
Know a man in Gippsland who admits that he
owes his success in life to the substantial compen-
sation he received from a public fund because his
densely-scrubbed land was cleared for him by an
accidental bush-fire, which saved him large expen-
diture.

“ Dandalup’s ” yarn : Away out in the
Westralian bush lives Paddy, the cooper, in
a bark shanty, nestling on the bank of
a little stream known as Tumble-down Greek.
Here he lives year after year, and he
is so old that lie never seems to get
any older. His only companions are an old tabby
cat with a stumpy tail, a black snake, abob-tailed
gohanna, and a shaggy old grey dog, a cross
between a collie and a dingo. Periodically the
old hermit accompanied by his companions,
the snake in one pocket, the “bobby ” in-
the other, and followed by the cat and
dog, takes a trip up to the little town
of Manjurah, situated on the Murray
River. Then Paddy is in great re-
quest, and many a good “ dollar ”
finds a resting place in his
capacious pocket; for, as
everybody knows, he is the
champion “ egg setter ” of the
district. Late one afternoon
I went down to a cocky
friend’s place to see the old
fellow do some setting. It was
rather late when he started,
and the sun was sinking in a
sea of blood-red gold, which
made Paddy’s performance
look weirder than it really
was. He first tied a fantastic
arrangement of straw on to
the top of his head ; this de-
coration stood upright and
looked like the head-dress of
a South Sea Island cannibal.
Then lie took three eggs in
each hand and went down to
the bottom of the fowl-yard,
and started to prance about
and make faces at “Broody,”
the hen. Gradually he ap-
proached her, until he got right
up to her nest, by which time she
was hypnotised. Then, crooning a
sort of aboriginal chant, he placed
the eggs under “Broody”; then
kneeling down in front of her he
chanted and bobbed his strange head-dress up
and down for fully half-an-hour. Then, with
the remark of, “Damn it, she’ll do,” Paddy took
his departure. I went down to see those chickens
when they were hatched, and, as sure as chickens
are chickens, each one had a beautiful feathery
top-knot on the roof of its head. If ruffles
round the neck or legs are wanted, Paddy
is always sent for, for he was never known to
miss. And for all this his charge was only a
modest “ half a dollar.”

Lately at Te Kiri, Opunaki (M.L.), a cow
dropped a heifer calf. Nine weeks afterwards she
dropped a fine, healthy bull calf. Then she
dropped the habit.

IN OUR BACKYARD.
OUR ANGEL BOY : “ Wile yabbit-oh!”
P.Q.F. : Not generally known that
wallabies, when the grass is green, can live
without water. But in dry times they flock to
water, not so much to drink as to eat the moist
roots near the margin. The koala, too, can
dispense with water, but the ’possum must have a
“ wet ” every night.
A COVE WITH A GUN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was summer, and something impelled me
To speed to my mother, the Bush ;
So I gathered my traps and I held me
Away to the vistas of hush.
To my birthplace I journeyed all eager,
And I wandered there under the trees ;
There I laughed at adversity’s leaguer
As I quaffed the rich wine of the breeze.
I was happy, and laid me there thinking
As I watched the great going-down sun,
Till I saw a bent figure go slinking—
’Twas a Cove with a Gun !
I knew him the moment I spied him—
I knew that I might not escape ;
Say, why did the devil provide him,
This fiend in humanity’s shape ?
He saw me and turned his steps hither,
And I saw he was burdened with birds ;
Then I felt all my soul sear and wither—
I was wholly divested of words !
In my dumbness I signed with my fingers.
And I prayed he might go at a ran ;
But he stands there—great heavens 1 he lingers—
The Cove with a Gun !
His face was the face of a mammal
Far down in the vertebrate grade ;
A blend of gohanna and camel
His pestilent features displayed.
His jaw was the jaw of a lizard,
His brow was concealed in his hat;
How I yearned for the power of a wizard
To shift him away out of that!
For his form interfered with my sunse' ,
And I moaned to myself, “ I’m undme 1—
Aye, my holiday’s spoiled by the ons:t
Of a Cove with a Gun ! ”
He stood there some time and surveyed me,
Slouching off when he heard a bird screech ;
Then Providence came and arrayed me
Once more with the blessings of speech.
“Praise the gods, he is gone ! ” I was saying
When a sudden explosion occurred ;
’Twas that lizard-faced miscreant siaying
Some other unfortunate bird.
“ Now he’s twisting its neck,” I reflected,
Its innocent life is all done—
Aye, its fluttering soul’s been dissected
By the Cove with a Gun !

“L.E.” : The bush fire lias sometimes a fine,
moral influence, and turneth away wrath. At the
hack o’ Neerim the Gip [island fires struck two
cocky families. These families had adjoining
selectidrls, and their ttext neighbors Were 19 miles
ftway. For nine years the rival cookies never
Spoke; Though fitr from society and the
Mechanics’ hall dnd tlie churCh aiid the pub. ;
thdugh eaten out by loneliness and caterpillars and
ted ldries; they glowered Over a family feud that
itfdse through a saucepan Mrs. Smith had once
littrrdwbd and returned to Mrs. Jones some-
what tlie worse fdr wear. The row _ be-
hartib aS severely dignified ds an international
dispute: The Jones’ brats were never allowed
to filaV Wihli the Smith spawn. Smith never
Had a pipe with Jones, or borrowed a plough from
iiirri ; and Jones never leaned over his fence and
took an intelligent interest in Smith’s crops. Mrs;
Jones had her own opinion regarding Mrs. Smith,
and delivered it freely to her hens and family.
Mrs. Smith bashed Mrs. Jones’ pigs with a paling
every time they broke through her fence. Even
when the saucepan had become a pigs’ bucket the
war was still waged on general principles. Then
the fires burst out. Both huts were burnt to
the ground. Mrs. Jones’ pigs arid fowls were
now fried eggs and bacon; Smith’s delaying
crops came up sudden, like cigarette smoke. Jones
had no fencing left not to lean over. Both cookies
were absolutely burnt out. The belligerent fami-
lies sought refuge at the creek. Both clans
camped round the one spot. They lay with their
noses on the ground, letting the fire pass over
them. Trees crashed and fell over the creek.
Smith would look up and pray, and Jones look up
and curse. The women kept pouring water over
themselves, but still they didn’t speak. They
silently made a kind of mi-mi and went to roost,
all huddled together, helpless and ruined, and yet
they didn’t speak. Next morning they were
hungry and had no food. The men went fossick-
ing, and Jones found one of Smith’s cows that had
escaped' in the scrub. He brought it into camp.
He said nothing, but—at last the ice broke, oh
rather the fire cooled down. Mrs. Smith, who was
Jones’ sister, threw her arms round him and
kissed him. “ Gome an’ ’ave breakfast,” she
Cried joyously to Mrs. J ones’ brats. The elder MisS
Jones milked the cow; and When both families
had rioted in milk, the selectors yarned and formed
plans, and the kids started fighting, and the
women retailed all the gossip they had stored up
for nine years. The saucepan incident was buried,
and all was peace and good will. And though the
cookies were burnt out, and hadn’t a bean - or a
grass-seed or a second pair of pants, and the fires
still burnt sullenly, and they Were nearly blind
with smoke, and they had to start all over again,
they reckoned in their hearts this was the best
day they’d struck since they quarrelled over
nothing in 1897.
RUSHED.
TOWNIE (misinterpreting frantic signals): “Bi
Jove, them country girls fairly rush a
fellow! ”
A remarkable case of equine homing in-
stinct comes from Goulburn (N.S.W.). Nine
horses, out of a mob sent by Messrs. M‘Callum
and M‘ln tosh to Condobolin from Binda, re-
turned recently to the latter place after six months
—a distance of over 360 miles. Country traversed
is well enclosed, and it’s a mystery how the mokes
negotiated the hundreds of gates, fences, and
other obstacles en route, and arrived in good con-
dition, minus only a foal of their original number.
Probably a case of one highly-gifted animal acting
leader to the rest. And none of the nine un-
attached nags got entangled in a halter in the
whole 360-mile browse. They must be honest
folk out there.

“ Alfran ” : Be magpies cutting fencing-
wire. Some years ago, when I was living near
Burwood (Vic.), one of my neighbors complained
that the wires in his fencing were being continu-
ally cut and short pieces abstracted. Some
months afterwards a bough of a tree near the
fencing in question fell to the ground. Attached
to the bough was a magpie’s nest, the frame-work
of which was made of fencing-wire, which the
magpies had stolen from the fence.
“ Sandybug ” : Some months ft'fo 1 1 was
sent to mark out certain mineral leases Oft 1 the
head of Little Snowy Creek, a tributary of the
Mitta River, in the roughest part of Victoria. I
got to the prospectors’ camp by a fluke, for my
directions were all wrong, as usual, and I saw m uch
Scenery by the way, A few days after my arrival
the boss of the party remarked at breakfast:
“ We’re out of dinny. One of yens will have to
go to Mitta for it.” .faefe Something said he
Would gO; 1 said I Would go With him, as I had
sdmb notices to gire to the Warden, We arrived
at the ruin little tillage of Mitta after a tramp
down the range that loosened every joint m my
body. I made for the' nearest pub.—a collection
of sacks and saplings—and dfaAk a bottle or two
of the best beer I have tasted in my life—Beech-
worth brand. After an hour or so, I spied Jack,
coming towards me mounted on a stout horse and
leading another one. “ Come on ; jump up ! We
must make camp afore dark,” he said. Where’s
youf dynamite?” I asked. “ Here !” opening
his shirt; stnd there, packed over a tightened
belt, WaS a, large supply of dynamite in plugs.
Over his saddle ill fi-Ont dangled two empty kero-
sene tins, fOr cooking purposes—Jack was cook.
Everything went well until the steep pinches, and
then the excitement began. I Wanted to give
Jack a wide berth, but I had to keep him in sight
or get lost, as the country to a newcomer was
absolutely hopeless. The jangling of the empty
kerosene tins, after a stumble, frightened Jack’s
horse, which shied, stood on its hind legs, and
finally threw its rider, practically at my feet, as
my neddy would keep up to the other horse. I
died several deaths of sheer fright on that journey
to the camp, and my demonstrations of horror,
loudly punctuated by some bad words, only
seemed to amuse Jack. We arrived at camp,
greatly to my surprise. I was completely shat-
tered with fright, fatigue and relaxed throat from
screaming, cursing, etc., etc. That night, in my
dreams, I was several times blown into a spray,
surrounded by a nimbus of kerosene tins, horse
entrails, etc., and I am still subject to awful
visions. Money would not tempt me again on an
Upper Mitta trip—at least, nothing under a
million would do it.

“ W.P.8.” : Saw what appeared to me at
first to be a new brand of insect the other day
My first shock of surprise over, I examined the
object. Couldn’t make it out even then, so put
my specs on to get a clearer view. The object
was slate-colored and yellow. Round that what
looked a cross-bred article was a large hornet and
a tarantula locked in a deadly embrace. The
former was moving backwards, and the spider
seemed to be pushing it that way. Observed
closer still, and found the spider was dead, and
the hornet was dragging it backwards to its
dinner-table. Seized a bamboo rod and aimed at
the hornet. There was a ghastly, crushing sound,
and then a whirr of wings. I had killed the
spider deader than ever, but the hornet had risen
with murder in its eye. Dropped the bamboo,
and fled like a cab-horse for safety. Came back
as soon as things had quieted down a bit, and
spotted the hornet some yards away, still dragging
his dinner. Picked up the rod again, and, taking
aim, smote with all my might. There was a
death-scream, and then all was still. Wonder
was the spider killed in a heated argument, or
was he already dead when the hornet met him ?
What horse, is it advisable to put £1 on when a
hornet and a tarantula begin to scrap ?

Sole Representatives Tor Australasia: ROBERT H. BARBER & CO., LTD., GEORGE STREET, SYDNEY.
PERSONAL ITEMS.
George H. Reid, K.C., M.P., has turned the
corner into his 62nd year. In 1879 he was called
to the Bar, and entered Parliament in 1880.
Joseph Capel Smyth, who animates a
bench in the Queensland Chamber of Fossils, has
not been known to speak during his 22 years’
membership.
G. H. Reid has been retained to defend
W. P. Crick, at Sydney Central Criminal Court,
on the charge of meeting Peter Close and Willis
too frequently.
A witness with a neat turn of phraseology
transpired at a Melbourne court lately. Speaking
of a husband, in trouble for assault and battery, he
observed that the gentleman in question was “a
staunch beer-eater.”
Bishop Streteh, the fourth C.E. Bishop of
Newcastle, is Australian born, a native of Geelong.
His predecessors in the gaiters of the coaly city—
Tyrrell, Pearson, and Stanton —were born in
London or thereabouts.
W. L. Edwards, N.S.W. Usher of Black
Bod, retires after a year’s service. His pre-
decessor, S. M. Mowle, held the billet for 30
years. The silly job is now tacked on to a Legis-
lative Council clerkship.
On his return to Ireland genial Dan
O’Connor will find the house in which he was
born about 60 years ago, and where his father
carried on a butcher’s shop, still standing on the
Spittle—something Dan O’Connorish in the name
—a market division of the town of Tipperary.
President Chaffey Baker denies that he
intends to stand down from the next Senate con-
test in S.A. It is said, however, that Josh.
Symon, K.C., finds the drain on his time too
great to justify him in-seeking re-election, espe-
cially as there is no present prospect of a Federal
judgeship falling into his hands.
A Sydney parson, who has lately travelled
the world, relates how he climbed up Mount Sinai
and read the Ten Commandments from the top.
That is the type of man who, after Gabriel has
blown his trump, will, if it isn’t hung up out of
his reach, bray into it.
A drop. N.S.W. ex-Governor Beauchamp,
of “birthstain” memory, was mentioned for the
Lord Lieutenancy of Ireland in the Campbell-
Bannerman Ministry, but the post finally assigned
to the ex-Gov. is that of Captain of the Corps of
Gentlemen-at-Arms ! Salary, £l2OO.
Artist Percy Spence, after a good soaking
in Australian sunshine, returns to London next
month. Spence did excellent black-and-white
work in London during his recent stay ; this time
he intends to tackle color-work, and has already
some commissions for portraits awaiting him in
the Big Smoke,
Dead recently at North Sydney, Miss Edith
Hanson, third daughter of the late William Hanson,
at one time N.S.W. Government Printer, and of
the Empire proprietary. Miss Hanson occupied
a position as clerk in the Customs. She rendered
her father large help in the production of his
“ Geographical Encyclopedia of N. S. Wales.’’
Colonel Waddell, chairman of the Board
inquiring into the Major Hawker affair, has
been squatter, banker, and politician, as well as
soldier. The three brothers—Premier, Parson,
and Warrior —grew up on a patch of dry sheep
country on the skirts of Lake George—the lake
that only needs water to be as wet as any other
lake-
A NATURAL QUESTION.
“ Father , dear, why doesn't the Government let
Mr. Willis come hack? He must be very
homesick."
“ Melb.” : J. M. Davies, brother of
Matthew of the Boom, has been much under the
limelight. J.M. also got knocked out over the
Boom, but emerged in purest samite. His ambi-
tion was to build the finest house in Melbourne
—and there it is, a commentary on the text that
the great ones of the earth build desolate places
for themselves. Bent tried to make Davies a
Judge at the age of 66. The storm that arose
made the old man decline the honor. If he had
taken it he would have been the first solicitor to
climb on the Victorian Bench.
Apparently Mr. Willis, though he has
eliminated all traces of compulsion from the
process by defeating the extradition law, is not
going to return to N.S.W. just yet to deal it out
to his calumniators. There is nothing to be done
in the land agency business in S’Africa—the Boers
are “off ” all agents, having struck the fathers of
the tribe of Willis in the days of the emancipation,
when agents collected the compensation for the
liberated slaves for the pore, guileless farmers.
The Boers have never
forgotten those agents.
So Willis, it is an-
nounced, has gone
into the business of
selling horses and
ponies, which Mrs.
Willis is shipping to
him from Australia.
Last week the Full
Court decided that
fugitives from the law
were not entitled to
have their evidence
taken on commission,
and that Mr. Willis’s
presence is urgently
required in N.S.W.
in the interests of
justice. Surely, now
that he has the oppor-
tunity to remove all
suspicion that he was
fighting to get clear
away, Mr. Willis will
return “in the inter-
ests of justice.”
Box, the new Vic.
County Court Judge,
puts in all his spare
time on the Gippsland
Lakes. He has a cosy
place overlooking
Bancroft Bay, and
owns a fast boat of
funereal hue, with
boatman to match.
Box is a funny-looking
mariner when he goes
down to sea in his
ship. He sports tight
knee breeches, a black
jersey and a faded and
battered ruin of an
old hat jammed on
his legal knowledge like an extinguisher. There
seems to be very little of his Honor in this rig ;
and it is said the crew has been known to reach
for his Honor suddenly, mistaking him for a be-
laying-pin.
B. R. Wise has definitely thrown up the
Cobden Club. That body wanted explanations of
his new views; he tried to let a little light into
its mind, failed and resigned. Interesting to
note, in reference to this, that Canadian Prime
Minister Laurier, who enforces in his country a
tariff twice as high as that of Australia, is a
Cobden Club member.
John Warrington Rogers, newly deceased
in England, aged 84, was appointed by the
Imperial authorities to the Solicitor-Generalship
of Tasmania before Constitutional Government
was granted. After serving a term as M.P. in
the slow island, he acted for 20 years as County
Court Judge in Victoria. He then resigned and
practised at the bar because the Ministry of the
day refused to make the tenure of office for the
County Court bench the same as that for the
Supreme Court. In ’93 Rogers left for the Cold
Country, where he dreamed away the last 13
years of his life.
Em of Battenberg’s mother, Beatrice,
was always sat-on by the rest of the family, and
for the sin o'f being the youngest childshe was made
to attend to the crotchetty Royal mamma.
Nowadays, that task over, Beatrice of
Battenberg (usually attended by Ena) spends
much time opening the Distressed Dogs’
Home, or the Lost Cats’ Shelter, or the
Refuge for Damaged Ducklings. So
King Ned makes even Beatrice a
useful hack at the business of propping
up a throne. Probably she gets some
return in the way of gifts that eke
out the £6OOO a year she receives
from the public purse. Now the
aged Cinderella’s pretty daughter
lays hands on the finest matrimonial
fish in the Royal market, for whom
Connaught (with a marriageable
daughter, Patricia) angled in vain.
Alfonso’s “civil list” gives him an
income of £280,000 —a great deal
more than his distressed country
can afford.
Brief extract from Maoriland
Liberal Herald (Premier Seddon’s
monthly magazine):—“ln the late
Prince Albert, and His Majesty
King Edward, and the Right Hon.
Mr. Seddon, we see three great
natural leaders of men.”
Ex-Victorian Governor “ Chinny ” Clarke,
who was among the batch of people selected by
Balfour at the eleventh political hour for lifts in
social rank, probably deserved recognition more
than any of the others, most of whom seem to
have had baubles thrust upon them because they
gave money to party funds. Clarke has proved
himself such a power at the War Office that when
he was recently laid up for a few days with a sore
throat or something, half the British Cabinet
found it necessary to rush round to his private
house and consult him about the things that
matter. Ex-Excellency Clarke has about the
clearest brain of the whole British War staff, and
if he had plenty of money to back up his brains
he would probably be at the very head of the
whole concern. As it is, he is just a “plainbrown
man ” of the kind George Turner used to be.
AN ILLUSTRATED CHESTNUT.
