Acting-professorship of Classics at Ade-
laide University goes to H. S. Dettmann, a master
at Sydney Grammar School. Born about 30 years-
ago, in N.S.W., where his father was inspector in
the Education Department, Mr. Dettmann is
entirely a product of the State school system.
Mr. G. C. Henderson, another Sydney product,
formerly a State school teacher in N.S.W., is also
a professor in Adelaide, his chair being history.
Two sons of members of Melbourne’s
oldest legal firm, Malleson, England and Stewart,
passed away last Monday week—Frank England
and Gordon Stewart. Of the original firm there
now only remains J. O. Stewart, who is over 70,
and intends soon to retire. Then the big practice
will pass to Willie Stawell (son of Vic.’s ex-C.J.),
Hugh Nankivell M'Eacharn (a nephew of
Malcolm’s), and Osborne, all strangers to the
original three partners.
Attorney-General Davies’ refusal of Vic.
Supreme Court Judgeship leaves Bent a chance to
offer the position to the man whom all the Bar
say should have it—Leo Oussen. He is admitted
universally to be the strongest man at the Vic.
Bar, and from being a railway clerk at a pound a
week has worked himself up to a practice which,
even in these dead times, is worth £5OOO a year.
But Cussen may possibly refuse. He has still
political aspirations.
“ Melb.” : Premier Bent is a member of
the Moorabbin Shire Council, and delights to go
down there and squabble with ehawbacons.
However, Councillor Ferdinando (what a name !)
swabbed the floor with him the other night—
—metaphorically and allegorically. But that is
all in the day’s work. Bent is never happier than
when wrangling over a drain or something, even
though the hayseeds are uncivil to him at times
and Councillor Ferdinando ; becomes almost
violent.
The will of the late Frederick William
Elliott (of Elliott Brothers, Sydney, chemists)
divides £50,000 pretty evenly among his people.
He provides, however, that if any of his daughters
joins any religious body, or becomes any kind of
“sister” or nun, she shall forfeit all interest in
the estate. The three daughters are all married,
and married women seldom rush headlong into
the sister business, or the nun industry, but, as
Frederick used to say about almost everything
when he was alive, “one never knows.”
Old Samuel Davenport, of Adelaide, who
was a member of the State Executive in the pre-
constitutional days of over 60 years ago, will com-
plete his 88th year on March 5. Although his tall
figure is now very much bent, he is often to be
seen about Adelaide streets, and transacts his
business much as he has been doing for the last 20
years. Although he introduced the olive into the
State and had much to do with the inauguration
of the wine industry, he is not at all a wealthy
man.
Died (22/2/’O6), aged 80 years, Ralph
Moore, fourth son of Captain Joseph Moore, of
Moore’s Wharf, Miller’s Point, Sydney. Mr.
Moore was a native of Sydney, born in Cumber-
land-street ; the old structure in which he first
saw the light still stands. The Moores made
money in the ancient whaling days. A brother,
Henry Moore, M.L.C., was the first Sydney agent
for the P. and O. Steamship Company. Another,
David Moore, M.L.C., also a Sydney native, was
a member of the second Haines Ministry (Vic.),
from April, 1857, to March, 1858, and was one of
the four members for Melbourne in the first
Legislative Assembly, in 1856.
Ernest Cliitty, a totally blind Auckland
(M.L.) student, took his B.A. degree at the local
University, recently. His subjects included
mathematics, constitutional history, political
economy, mental science, and jurisprudence. He
absorbed his knowledge viva voce , and success-
fully gave it out, for examination purposes, to a
typewriter. Williams, a Sydney undergrad.,
took his degree under similar circumstances, a
couple of years ago.
One of Sydney’s oldest identities, Michael
Chapman, joined the innumerable caravan of the
dead last week, at the age of 85 years. If not a
native, Mr. Chapman was a very old colonist.
Nearly GO years ago he was described as “ painter,
etc.,” in Crown-street, Woolloomooloo. In the
fifties he entered into partnership with Ebenezer
Beaumont as oil and color men at 470 George-
street, and the business was continued by Mr.
Chapman for many years. From 1866 to 1900 he
was an alderman of Sydney, and was mayor in
1871 and 1872. When George Wigram Allen
died he was elected to the Legislative Assembly
for Glebe, and held the seat from 1883 until 1891.
He was a useful alderman, but as an M.L. A. he
was never more than a voting machine.
Some discursive remarks by “ Wurrym ” :
Kielley and Duggan, the last of the Fenians, were
smoke-socialled at Perth (W.A.) last week though the
latter was absent owing to ill-health. Kielley, like J. B.
O’Reilly, was a soldier. He was one of the chief plotters
in the scheme to seize the armory at Clonmell, and under-
took to make the necessary skeleton keys. He was
granted a license in 1878, and a free pardon about a
month ago. Speaking of J. B. O’Reilly and his novel
“ Moondyne,” the original of this work used to bemoan
to me his hard luck in being so misrepresented. Moon-
dyne Joe was originally sent out for poaching. He was a
gipsy, and had the gipsy’s unconquerable love for free-
dom and the open air. That was all the trouble there was
between the gaol authorities and Joe. On one occasion
Joe was missing from the gaol, where for weeks he had been
employed breaking a large heap of stones. There was no
supervision. The officials knew he could not vet over the
wall without some aid, and then would certainly be seen.
Still, he was absent when wanted. On searching outside,
a small tunnel, going under the wall foundation, was
found ; this was traced to a chamber formed under the
stone Joe was supposed to be breaking. In this chamber
Joe had sunk and tunnelled at odd times. Other times
he stuck to his stone-breaking job. After an absence of
some weeks Joe was recaptured down the coast, and was
being brought back by two constables, to one of whom
he was handcuffed. The party slept one night in a hut
somewhere near Rockingham, about 14 miles from Fre-
mantle. Next morning there was no prisoner. One
bobby went back'to look for him ; the other went on to
Fremantle to report. In that town he found Joe had only
slipped the handcuffs out of cussed nes?, as he had walked
on and reported himself at the gaol. Moondyne’s frequent
escapes got to be too much for the gaol people, so an iron
cage was made for keeping him at home. When it was
completed a Government House party, headed by
Governor Weld, went to inspect Joe in his cage, and
Weld promised the gipsy that if he got out again he
would get his ticket of leave. There were in the party
the son and daughter of a high official —the one a high
official himself now and the other the wife of another
exalted public servant. Later on in the day they went
back to the gaol as visitors to the governor and his
family. Regulations regarding the governor’s visitors
were not very stringent, so the two youngsters found
it easy to go and have a yarn with Moondyne, and
incidentally leave him a file or two. Next morning, while
Weld was breakfasting, a servant announced that a man
wanted to see him. It was Moondyne Joe. “Good
Lord,” said Weld, and he wrote out the ticket, of leave.
Thereafter Moondyne Joe lived in peace. He went to his
rest some five years or so ago. In his last days he was an
inmate of the depot for old men who have blazed the
track.

There is talk among Adelaide Democrats
of returning Kingston for his old district this
year, even if he is too ill to take his seat, in order
to mark-public appreciation of his splendid ser-
vices. But a seat which he can’t occupy is a very
poor present, and the throwing away of a Demo-
cratic vote in the Reps, is a poor compliment to
the cause which Kingston has so much at heart.
Better to put in another sound Democrat, and
present Kingston with a £2OOO cash testimonial and
an assurance that his constituency will be waiting
for him whenever he is well enough to want it.
Kingston is never seen in the city now. He is the
Hermit of Marino.

The mayor of a village near Paris lately
arrested himself on the charge of murdering his
wife. The poor woman, suffering tortures from
cancer, had implored her husband to put her out
of her hell-on-earth, and at last he responded to
her prayers with a hatchet. Then he made his
deposition, and left the law to decide how much
he was guilty of murder. Seems to The Bulletin
that the worthy mayor might have raised the
interesting law point with something less unsym-
pathetic than a hatchet. There are more ways
than one of giving a wife a merciful quietus, and
she would probably prefer to have an artery
opened with a sharp penknife, for instance, than
to be hit across the head with ironmongery.
Human nature doesn’t like to be treated like a
mere piece of wood.
“ A.G.” gives a veracious account of the
“horrors,” as he knew them :
X was returning to the station, after knocking down
mv cheque, when a touch of “the horrors” came on.
The bottle of whisky the lamber-down gave me, to put
me right, did not last the 60 miles by coach. Rummaging
about, I found a bottle of rum, and polished it off. A
passenger got off, later on, and missed his flask ; I had
accounted for it. 1 can recollect the scenes that passed
through my mind as well as if I had witnessed them at
the Mutoscope. They were mostly pleasant, horsey, and
impossible. The sight of an object started my mind
wandering. For instance, a concertina suggested music.
A boxing-glove was the means of my witnessing the most
bloodthirsty encounter that any man could wish for. One
horse would be multiplied by six on the least provocation.
Suddenly a circus came with flags flying and band play ing.
They pitched their tent and the performance started. The
man on the white horse jumped clean over the store
(70 x 30). Another followed him riding a saddled emu.
An imp tied red cotton around one of my fingers and
pulled. I hung like a monkey to my seat, and the cotton
snapped. It was the signal for the band to play. After
listening to it for an hour or so I stepped up to shake
hands with the conductor when I got scalded. Aight
time I suffered the most. It took me the whole of the
first night removing firewood from my bunk back to the
wood heap. I was giving the station hands a speech on
the benefits of Freetrade (I w«s mad), when one of the
biggest cats that I have seen came in the side door back-
wards. Its eyes were lumps of silver. When its tail
moved it left a fiery streak in the air. I distinctly re-
member the cook frying chops on its ribs. Past the hut
there is a bore flowing. With the aid of a laddei I tried
to get up to it. I could see the fish moving about through
the bottom. It was an overhead stream. That liignt
the hut collapsed. I held it up as long as I could, borne
of the hands rushed out in their shirts when I raised the
alarm, and said, “ All clear, let her fall.” I jumped back.
The noise of the falling timber and iron seemed to say,
“ You did it, clear out.” Away I ran and picked up with
a carrier. His wife and kids were on top of the loading.
He had a novel contrivance for getting up and down, it
was a large wheel, with steps on it. You simply stood on
a step, and, as the waggon moved on, the wheel revolved.
When at the top you stepped off. I offered hunagood
price for the plan, but he refused. We had a row and
parted, because he would not stop his dog, a green ana
yellow our, from following me. A stockman on the
neighboring run found me, after being away foi five days,
40 miles from home. And he says my watch was wound
up, and correct as to time. It took four weeks
straighten me up. Some who see snakes with le„s „et
right in two weeks. I fancy the worse the visions the
speedier the recovery. One tries harder to get away
from them.

Some unemployed member of the N.S.W.
Legislative Assembly might do a useful bit of
work by calling for a return of the number, names,
and injuries of the people who have been privately-
compensated by the Government for being acci-
dentally shot by the police in the execution of
their duty. Almost every other day the papers
tell of midnight or early morning chases, of re-
volver shots being fired by the pursuing police,
but seldom is there mention of anyone being hurt.
But strange rumors float round, and a story is told
of a cabman who died from wounds received while
sitting on his cab when the police were bombarding
a vanishing figure in Hyde Park.

Public Notice.
Department of Mines and Agriculture,
Sydney, 22nd February, 1906.
IT is hereby notified for public information that
in all eases where an unstamped transfer o
interest in any Mining Claim or Lease 13
for registration the transferee must forward to
this Department an Agreement for Sale where
such has been entered into. If no such A | ree
exists, the tranferee must forward a Statutory
Declaration setting forth that no Agreement for
Sale has been made in reference to the transfer,
and stating the amount of consideration money
for such transfer. It is, however, preferable in
the interests of both transferee and the Depart-
ment, that every such transfer should be stamped
at the Stamp Duties Office before being lodged for
registration, but in tlie first instance an inquiry
should be made at this Department as to the state
of any lease or leases to be transferred, failure
to adopt one or other of these courses will cause
delay in the registration of the transfers for which
the Department canno^be^repionsiWm^^
Under Secretary for Mines and Agriculture.

The beauty of Valaze is that its use is
such a very simple matter. Just before retiring
it should be rubbed well into the skin and allowed
to remain on during the night. “It works its
wonders while you sleep.” It is a pure natural
herbal food, goes straight into the skin, feeding
and nurturing the delicate tissues. It eradicates

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, February 27, 1906.
My Dear Arini,—
Madame Browneyes sent a business card inti-
mating that her new millinery models would be
on private view at her showroom on a certain day
last week. The Girl Who Doesn’t Mind the
Weather got the card at Bowral. A private view
of Aphrodite herself wouldn’t have brought her
down from there, she explained, unless Aphrodite
wore a new season’s toque. “My curiosity on the
subject of the New Hat is unappeasable. If ever
Satan gets my soul he’ll have to fish with a hat-
baited hook. If it looks a stylish bait I’ll bite—•
for certain. If hats were worn in Heaven I’d
make greater efforts to get there; but haloes
and nimbuses seem to be the only head gear,
and the fashion of these hasn’t altered in two
thousand years—or whatever age Heaven is. I’d
go niad if I couldn t vary the angle of wearing my
halo, and turn it up at the back with amaranths,
and put a cherub winglet or pair of seraph quills
through its crownless brim. Am not sure that I
wouldn’t put in a crown of some celestial fur
I here are lambs in heaven aren’t there ? I’d have
a baby-lamb-skin crown.”
It was 97 in the shade when I met her at Red-
fern, and when I showed her the figures, she only
looked at the thermometer contemplatively and
f :ud J \fy hasn’t a good shady hat to keep the
neat oft. At the showroom she started to send
up the temperature, and madame’s pulse, with
commentary and question. “Say”—the Girl
Who Doesn t Mind the W r eather once travelled
as far as Honolulu in an American liner, and ever
since has nourished a few American mannerisms
like “ bav Say, I hope you won’t mind alter-
ing it i buy ? And when you are trying on, don’t
put it on one side. Yes, I know ever so many
wear them on one side, and it’s right enough if
a 6 jt eCt 1S Co( l uettls h, but with me it’s rakish.
And I never yet met a woman who 'wanted to look
rakish. Wliat swarms of bee-colored millinery
you ve got, madame ! O, yes, I know brown’s
tn and no mistake, and bees are a beauti-
ful bright shade of brown, but we haven’t
all got brown eyes, or red hair and sardonyx
eyes. Gimme a queen-bee hat then.” They put
on her fair head a toque made of twisted brown
tulle, the upturned brim wrought with layer upon
layer of brown, dead leaves and brown violets.
1 atches of lichen, mould, and fungus were on the
leaves and flowei petals. It had the disastrous
beauty of dead floral things, and after the girl
had worn it for 38 seconds she said. “Take it
away. H disturbs me. Reminds me of Proser-
pine. There’s a name for you, madame. Christen
it the Proserpine toque.” Another brownie was
tried To its rich, decaying, autumnal tints
and foliage were added two pea-hen’s wings
and the marchioness who assisted madame
remarked that these wings were a guinea a pair
Ihe client said that these sere and yellow hats’
with artistic discoloration and elegant blight’
made her feel like an extinct volcano, or a dead
moon. Her nervous organisation was so fine that
she was often what her clothes suggested
Madame feared, in zero tones, that the client was
merely prejudiced against brown in all its shades
and materials, and the client, who had been
reading a current novel, “Soprano,” on her rail
tpp down, admitted the accusation, and quoted
“ Prejudices are moral corns—anything that
touches one fnakes it ache more or less.”
A hat with a crown of Volga fur rather took her
fancy. Ihe fur, of which neither of us had ever
heard, is snow-white, short, close, and the softest
of all soft things. “ Soft as a horse’s nose,” said
the client, bable crowns, mink crowns, with tails
are “in,” and hats with grey kid lining. Also
green hats—a color like a green flame—blue-grev
like skies, and soft, like sighs. A big
with a regular turban of violets, was greatly de-
sired by the client, especially when she learnt
it had been sold and no replica allowed A
navy-blue hat with a “ gourah ” (a ’ fan-
shaped fragile feather ornament) and velvet
lemons was also on trial for 20 seconds, and then
came a cross between the torpedo and the boat-
shape, trimmed with dead violets, faded chrysan
themums and a long grey shaded feather curlino-
from forehead to nape of neck with a grace note of
pink over one ear. ‘‘ It suits my profile ” said the
girl juggling between two long mirrors, with a
.hand glass. “You will observe, Madame, that
I ve got a long pointed nose and the arrangement
of this bushy feather rather takes the eye off mv
Wilson’s Promontory facial outline. But it doesn’l
suit my disposition. I feel like a Blighted Bein°-
I feel as if the dark man that the fortune teller
promised me had come into my life and then left
me for ever. It must be the effect of all
this dead and dying garden spoil. Why is
the new millinery of a mortuary kind?”
They brought her a radiant thing in powder blue
and cinder black, with a bright-eyed bird holding
up one side of the brim. “If las much as tried it
on, she said, “I’d want to goto the Union Club
and blackmail my father’s oldest friend.” She
took up a glove.-stretcher and fanned herself
violently with it. I came away. But next day I
met the girl in a tea-room, and she told me she had
bought three models without trying them on at
all.
Last Thursday, the hottest day of the season
there were some 5000 persons surf bathing at
Manly between 6 and 7 o’clock, and about another
3000 were looking on or stealing the 5000’s
trousseau. At least a score of persons had their
wardrobe stolen. Many a woman who goes to the
beach with a mackintosh over her bathing dress
leaves, with childlike confidence, mackintosh and
sandals on the beach, unguarded. Owners and
property are parted for ever. Scarcely a
really picturesque costume is to be seen at
any but the Rose Bay baths. In the
latter, Mrs. Jim Fairfax takes the apple with
her pale green costume, with a lot of little frills on
the sleeves, and a hat tied under the chin with
green ribbons, and she looks a lorelei. But report
says she only wets her toes. All the other women
agree that it would be inartistic to spoil such a
lovely water frock with water. Two attractions
at another baths are sisters, very long and lean,
clad in tight black combinations and wearing
picture-hats. They don’t swim—but just go in
and bob up and down, never varying their
standing room, but always bobbing with.exactly
the same amount of bob in exactly the same spot.
Manly this year is crowded with visitors from
the sunstruck and droughty West of this State,
and they are almost as loath to come in out of the
wet as are the fish.
Departers of yesterday by big ships include the
Jarmans. He was the metallurgy specialist at
Sydney Uni., and has gone to Maoriland to do the
like work at its Uni. Mrs. J., a dark, auburn
lady, .was a very picturesque figure when she came
here a few years ago. Her name is Corisande,
arid you couldn’t be so named and not
live up to it. She was one of the few who
wore the lonely curl known as the Alexandra.
Allan Shaw, the coin juggler, went; so did Dr.
Rougier, Consul for Russia. Next Saturday the
H. Llewellyn Joneses leave for America for a six
months’ visit. Mr. Jones is American Vice-
Consul in Sydney, and is a C.E. representing the
Steel Trust.
Captain Greville, the Northcotes’ military secre-
tary, has wired that Lady Northcote gives her
patronage to the theatrical benefit in aid of the
Victor Daley fund. Benefit occurs on March 30.
From a Sydney woman writer’s page :
The servant girl (in Maoriland) is not a difflcultv—she
does not exist. Housework has to be faced in all its
details by the members of the family. Miss Seddon and
Miss Ward are no exceptions. They adapt themselves to
the situation, and do the work just like any other girls
in advanced Maoriland.
Someone has been fairy-taleing the writer. There
is no more difficulty in getting domestic servants
in Maoriland than here. There is difficulty
everywhere in getting competent generals , but
fairly-skilled cooks, housemaids, and laundresses
may always be secured in M.L. Wages are about
the same as in Australia. Servants’ registry offices
are in plenty in all the towns, and there is a steady,
if small, exodus of domestic women workers from
Australia to M.L. To my personal knowledge
three left last week. They are going to work in a
hotel if possible, at one of the health resorts, and,
as one of them explained, “ It is much easier over
there to get someone to walk out with you. As
for Miss Ward “doing the work,” the Wards
have a well-staffed household.
Madame Fallieres, vvife of the new President of
the French Republic, is described as “an accom-
plished hostess, whose natural refinement has
oeen polished by a long period of official life in
Pans. Yet hitherto she has accompanied her
cook to market to get the day’s supplies.

subjects ; music, painting, drawing, singing
plain and fancy needlework ; take full charge of reli-
gious instructions ; must have knowledge of dress-
making' ; sal., £26. Apply, with references, to .
The “ usual subjects,” plus four accomplishments,
some theology, and a working knowledge of a
trade—all for ten shillings a week ! ! Holy
frost! Advertiser is not likely to get the pearl
she wants, for a young woman who can do plain
needlework and dressmaking only can get a billet
in the country any time at more ‘than that wage ;
and if she is artistic she can get enough time off
to paint a water-hole, with a cow and a gum in
the foreground, for her own amusement. As for
the “usual subjects,” she can afford to cast her
shoe over them.

regattas:— /
The Prince and Princess embarked on a golden barge
towed by rowers, who chaunted a wild song, while girls
danced on the prow. In the races with long boats the
rowers kept time with clashing cymbals. The Shans
showed their arms, which they dispensed with in rowing
Winding their legs round the oars, they propelled them
light skiffs at incredible speed.
From the royal tour in India. A Sydney crew

from practising, read of the success of women

and some 50 of them in New York), what kind of
things do they say ? Mrs. Quaekenboss, an
American Lady One—l suppose that is the
r»cu lllline Oentleman One— makes on an average
£BOOO a year, and does much work free for desti-
tute clients. Several lawveresses in the land of
Aunt Sam are in partnership with their husbands
but the most remarkable of the tribe is a Miss King,
wbo, 15 years ago, was a domestic servant —a
Merely Mary Ann. This genius, three years ago
stood up in argument to the most brilliant lawyers
in New York on a highly technical dispute over a
patent. She won her case, and put out of business
a great corporation with a capital of a whole
dazzling million pounds.
A friend writes me this after a visit to Maori-
land :
I drifted down the Wanganui River—the most wonder-
ful river in my experience. You start early—beastlv
l' a 1 r y ./ r °n' Taumurunui, a little wooden town where the
inhabitants use the name of the place to fence their pad-
docks with, or else they chop off a syllable or two and
use it for a gate. If the river is low, you do the first stage
in a Prohibition canoe. It holds about fifteen, and each
traveller is limited to 561 b. of dunnage. Of course the
usual female brings a Saratoga trunk. The Prohibition
canoe is urged on its wild career by a petrol engine, and
tor nearly half a day it shoots a rapid about every half
nnle or oftener. Towards noon, after shooting dozens of
rapids and dodging 16 mountains, you reach the house-
boat and have lunch. It is a two-storey boat—sleeping
rooms, baths dining-room, music-room and sundries
Here you feed luxuriously with a mountain on each side,
the river flying: past into the great g'org-e ahead, and the
small, brown, dusty Maori kid gaping at you from the
shore, and you feel like an American king bolting his
hash m a palace. Possibly you feel so much that way
that you stay a week and fish.
Then you get into a steamboat which isn’t Prohibition
and shoot rapids most of the afternoon. You are in the
gorge. A wall of rock" rises on each side, in most places
square and straight as the ends of a cathedral. It is
loaded with vivid green moss, so thick that often hardly
an inch of bare rock appears. High up dense masses of
vivid green tree-ferns overhang the moss. The moss and
"^ en J 8 are re^ec t f -'d in the green transparent river
that flies along—where it isn’t having a rapid—like a
silent green looking-glass in a tremendous hurry. It
” e a g'reat green glass animal hastening along
with its mouth full of moss and a tree-fern in each hand.
And as the afternoon wears on, you grow weary of the
loveliness of it all, and full of undigested magnificence
It ought to be served up in six lots, not thrown at you
all at once. You spend the night at Pipiriki listening to
the passing river, and you are chased by green tree-ferns
in your sleep.
MISS NELLIE STEWART as a drummer boy
in one of her finest parts.

Maoriland wants an army and navy of its own. If it
doesn t provide itself with them an enemy will come
along some day and demand £20,000,000 indemnity, and
when the money isn’t forthcoming he will- go up the
Wanganui Elver, and scrape the moss off 50 miles of
consecutive gorge, and then one of the wonders of the
world will be no more. If I had a gorge like that I should
at least have it insured.
Mr. Itrnest Watt, of Sydney, has been makin o '
some fame for himself by a translation of Prosper
Mernmee s “ Lettres a une Inconnue,” which he
Englishes with the title of “Love Letters of a
b-enius. There is no doubt as to the merits of
the translation ; it’s excellent work, with much of
the flavor of the French stylist; but in my experi-
ence Mernmee does not translate well. His
charm is more in the style than the matter, and
style _ cannot be translated. But these love-
letters in English are at least a monument of
learned labor; and so many of our “gilded
youths” pass aimless existences.
Merrimee s love letters do not kindle. They
seem to me prim—an unpardonable fault in a love-
letter. they are the outpourings of a more
than middle-aged man, with a note alwavs
ot querulousness. How much fine literary gold is
lost m the world’s buried or burned love-letters
(probably the best love-letters are burned because
the circumstances which prompt them make their
burning necessary) ! The love-letters that are
preserved arid printed never seem like the real
thing ‘ with fine frenzy rolling if a love-letter
ever does roll. ,
How well he blims ! ” is now a common remark
among society hostesses in London. So, at least
says a society paper. They mean that he is a

I*ibt long ago, hostesses wanted people whose talk

under a new name ?
Shop-lifting, it seems, is epidemic this year
amongst some of the well-dressed women of
Maoriland cities. The other day, three comfort-
ably-attired femininities appeared before a shocked
magistrate, at the Wellington police court, for
pilfering from a big drapery establishment, at a
bargain sale (those sales !), and were severely
admonished and let out, weeping bitterly. Their
names were considerately withheld from publica-
tion by the kind-hearted press, so that possibly
their husbands don’t know yet what distressed
their httie wives so much that day. But some
kind fnend wdl teil them later on. The leading
Wellington drapery firms were interviewed, and
each set down its losses from thieving
women at many hundreds of pounds annually.
„, te b too » the big rag-shops are badly
had ' by the pretty demure girlies in their em-
ploy whom they expect to dress “ like ladies ” on
a lowly wage. A dozen or so hands found pick-
ing and thieving were unceremoniously “ sacked ”
in one establishment lately. In another, two very
nice girls were recently subjected to an igno-
minious search before leaving their work, and
were found to be e..oh wearing twelve pairs of
expensive lace frills purloined from the firm’s
stock. They are now taking a holiday. Which
sets one thinking when one sees extra plump-
Jookmg girls walking haughtily from the drapery
shops at knock-off time. Base-minded people
can t help wondering whether the plumpness is
quite natural.
Hardly an English or American paper one
takes up but has something to add to the
enormous mass of literature about man’s rela-
tion to woman, her relation to him, the
psychology of marriage, how woman ought to
treat her mate when he begins to growl after the
purring period of the honeymoon, and if he pre-
fers a perambulating clothes-rack to a housewifely
slave. In one of Marion Crawford’s earlier works,
“ The Three Fates,” a clever mother talks to her
daughter:—
Men have an immense body of tradition to sustain •
and they do it by keeping up appearances as well’
as they can. All men are supposed to be brave, strong
enduring, honorable and generous. They are supposed
never to feel hot when we do, nor catch cold when we
should. It is part of their stage character never to be
afraid of anything, and many of them are far more timid
tham we are ... It is not true that if you run away men
will follow you. They are far too lazy for that.' You
must come to them, but not too often. What they most
want is amusement . . . Never ask- a man where he has
been, what he has seen, or heard. If he has anythin l ' to
tell, he will tell you ; if he has not, you only humiliate
him by discovering the emptiness of his thoughts
Always ask his opinion. If he has none himself he
always knows someone who has. The difference between
men and women is very simple, my dear ; women look
greater fools than they are, and men are greater fools
than they look.

The Warringah Bowling Club, which is day-
after to-morrow in its enterprise, has in-
stalled the radiance known as the Lux light on
its verdant green, so that it may gambol on the
grass long after set of sun. Seems a sane and
artistic proceeding in this hot country. Mem-
bers of other clubs (jealous perhaps) say that
the new light and new playtime will make
the Warringah unpopular with the wives of its
bowlers. They will be ’ticed away from home by
the charms of bowling under the Lux and the stars.
Anyhow, some 300 guests rolled up to the open-
ing ceremony. President Denyer, “ Hop,”
and others made addresses of the “ lead
kindly light” description, and Miss Geraldine
Rivers, Miss Smith and Mr. Irving lifted up good
voices in good songs.

At the Palace on Saturday Maud Jeffries
appeared in a dawn-in-December pink gown,
raised in front with a cord and jewelled clasp, to
show a white brocade underskirt. Her hair was
done flat on top and curled on her neck. Never saw
her look mere bewitching. If her face and figure
as she appeared then couldn’t launch a thousand
ships and burn the topmost towers of Ilium they
would at least cause an up-to-date Leander to
launch a motor-boat on a stormy sea for her
safety. In the audience, a bonny debutante,
Miss Heather Keys, a cousin of Vinia De Loitte,
took the eye of the audience at interval time.
An Adelaide correspondent writes :
Adelaide crematorium is becoming quite a fashionable
roastery. Already there have been four cremations this
year, the latest being that of the widow of the late Judge
Andrews, whose remains were obliterated in 17 minutes—
the shortest time on record. Deceased used to drive
about Adelaide in an ancient chariot, painted bright
yellow sjnd black.
S.A. artist Christmas is touring the North Island of
Maoriland with an order from the M.L. Government to
paint several large landscapes depicting the great natural
resources of the country. He says he is going to paint
the land in its varying stages, first virgin bush, and sO on
up to ultra-cultivation point, after the manner of the
advertisements which show how you looked before and
after using Bull Beans. Christmas also intends to paint
on his own account a number of panels, showing the
different kinds of Maoriland wood, each panel to be made
from the tree it depicts. These attempts will be exhibited
at the Christchurch Exhibition, and afterwards sent
round the various country towns of America.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, February 27, 1906.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Society folk are feeling sour over the suggestion
of a Geelong vice-regal headquarters. Their rigid
Exes, are difficult enough of access even at Ston-
ington—but at Geelong ! People who don’t take
a cannibal interest in Government House feel,
however, that the proposal to move the State
Exes, to Geelong is a most happy one. This putting
of them in an out-of-the-way corner seems a step
towards a complete abolition of the useless office.
About half the Bent capital, in a wild way of
speaking, turned out to wave its hanky at the
multitudinous voyagers on the Moldavia’s decks
last Tuesday. Several official batches were there
to farewell the Austin Chapmans and Joseph
Wards, who, however, didn’t leave till the next
day, when they fled by express to the sainted
capital for embarkation. The Langtrees had a
fair portion of Toorak to murmur good wishes.
The Miss in particular attracted a gathering of
she-chums, and was garbed for the occasion in a
deck dress of the cutest cut. Her cabin had a
blockade from door to port-hole of floral contribu-
tions, and sufficient photographs to stuff a city
show-case. Beads of perspiration mingled with
the briny tear of sympathy, and one couldn’t tell
t’other from which in the blistering heat. What
added to the sorrowful tone of things was the
funereal get-up of the official farewellers. Also
aboard were squatter Thompson, his mate, and a
pair of six-footer twins. The only fun created at
the anchor-weighing was through the confusion
created by this conundrum pair of long girleens.
Farewellers went hugging the same twin twice
over, thinking it was the other. There isn’t an
eyelash of difference between the duplicates.
Two gay bachelors embarked, and had a swarm
of vivacious young she-folk to hum “For they
are jolly good fellows” as they started their
escape to Europe. A dirge would have better
expressed popular feeling.
Two Sydneyites, Mr. Owen Cox and Miss
Bonser, did the Joseph Ward family much frizzy
glory some days before the Maorilanders took up
their band-boxes and departed. On Tuesday
night the detachment took the two grand tier
boxes at the Royal to sample the Hall Caine
drama. It’s a long time since the Royal had such
a galaxy of starched linen area and blank chest in
its special pens.
Lady Northcote is giving her helpful support to
more local industries. This time it’s to the lace-
makers of Abbotsford. Last Monday she paid a
visit to the convent on the Yarra’s edge, and after
reviewing the oceans of point and duchesse, of
Irish crochet and other rare varieties of the skilled
stitch, she left an order for several sumptuous cas-
cades of the crochet. The M ‘Eacharn dame was
the first to flaunt a whole rig of Abbotsford lace
in a ballroom. Others followed.
The Seventh-Day Adventists, now in camp at
Camberwell, seem to be forgetting the teaching
of earlier camp-meetings, when fierce Yankee
preach-marms inveighed against corsets, carpets,
and civilisation generally, and exhorted their
hearers to “ git close to Nature’s heart.” Nowa-
days, floorcloths, sideboards, &c., abound in
Canvas-town. Apart from their “Seventh-Day”
theory, the Adventist's differ little from the
Methodist sects, except that they are vegetarians,
which implies that they worship the Goddess
Hygeia (without knowing it), and think and talk
of hardly anything but health, longevity, and
dry-as-dust patent foods, whose names all end in
“ose.”
In camp, t’other Sunday, a long, thin doctor
lectured on “ Health,” dwelling much on his own
“stomach,” as if haunted by misgivings that
hearers, misled by his contour, might suspect him
of struggling along without that necessary pro-
tuberance. In the intervals of stereotyped hymns
and prayers, it was rather comic to hear the lec-
turer describe his internal experiences, especially
certain “severe abdominal pains,” which periodi-
cally attacked him in his pre-Adventist and,
presumably, pre-matrimonial days, when he
“visited constantly” at a house where the
daughters (anaemic, though seemingly attractive)
were wont to undermine his digestion with strong
tea and bread and butter.
When Hopetoun (now Linlithgow) was Gover-
nor of Victoria, three of his best servants, all of
whom had lived with him in Scotland, went hope-
lessly to the bad through the drink craze. In
those days everybody at Melbourne Government
House had access to liquor ad lib., the annual
expenditure of the Governor amounting to £30,000.
Hopetoun did not care for beer or champagne
himself, and thought the stuff should be thrown
away on his servants and the “society” unem-
ployed who infested his premises.
Local Germans don’t fret much over the heavi-
ness of Melbourne’s “Presbyterian Sabbath,”
furnished, as they are, with a refuge in the shape
of a “ Bier Garten.” There they spend Sunday,
plaving cards, smoking, and sipping lager in a
favilion, or packed into a smoky theatre-room.
t’s enough to make one’s very flesh cry out to
think of those German plays—long, labored, do-
mestic melodramas, that go on and on and on, till
everyone not made in Germany goes home with a
sick headache, leaving the plot not half worked
out. It comes as a shock, that Beer Garden, to
untravelled Australians, who have always listened
meekly while German acquaintances scoffed at
Anglo-Saxons as tyros in the matter of
Sunday amusement, and described the happy
Vaterlanders sitting round little tables, in
sunshiny fresh air, with flowers blooming, birds
singing, and bands playing in every direction.
There is a bit of garden at the seventh-day haunt
of local Teutons, but the habitues never bother to
go into it, and it has grown dusty, hugger-mugger,
altogether hateful. Men, women, and children
prefer to perspire in the theatre hall, with its low
iron roof and miserable ventilation. Herren puff
clouds of tobacco-smoke, and on the stage a stout
frau, in the Heavy Mother’s part, sips coffee, knits
and says “ Bitte ” or “ Gewisz ” every ten minutes
or so, while the Heavy Father, in tow wig, rouged
cheeks, large waist and small clothes, twaddles
through a whole scene regarding his low-born but
innocent daughter, pursued by the perilous at ten-<
tions of a town-councillor’s son.
Miss Nancy O’Loghlen, one of the large batch
of daughters left by the late Bryan the Baronet,
has plighted her troth to a foreigner of birth and
cash in the Big Smoke. Miss Nancy was the
friend and companion of the Rupertswood dame
for a decade, and joined her in her Mayfair estab-
lishment. The beauty of the O’Loghlen family,
after two seasons of social life, passed into a
religious order, and is now a fully-fledged sister in
a Loretto convent. The widow of the Baronet
and the rest of the family keep some state, and
boast of spending less on clothes than most mem-
bers of their set.
Miss Vida Goldstein has a talent for blending
sentiment with business. Last week she embarked
for the sandy State, to add the family’s blessing
to a brother’s marriage and also resolved on a
brief season of platform talk on the general
inferiority of man. Friends who, in the old days,
saw Miss Vida serving a ball on an opponent in
the ’Varsity tennis court, declare they saw all the
successive platform battles she has waged since in
her determined little eye then.
Commander Colquhoun steps jauntily into the
shoes vacated by Naval Director Cresswell, and
in the stress of additional cares and responsibili-
ties is likely to lose the coquettish fringe that has
survived all the early crown-thatch. Up to now,
that small sprouting over the bump of individu-
ality has stood against the zealous assaults of
time.
A procession of naval and military experts is
moving week by week to the Cold Country. Every
cadet by this time has a notion of getting his turn
at the grand tour, on an inquiry as to the proper
military brand of moustache, or bear’s grease for
the hair, or something.
The theatrically-equipped figure in Holman
Hunt’s pious canvas wears a cloak that Mrs.
Bland Holt remarked on sotto voce in the Gallery
last week as being a covetable property. An
ancient buckle of dazzling brilliancy fastens it,
and this too leads to green-eyed longings. The
diffusive lamp suggests, even to my lay mind, a
theatrical prop that any Wilson Barrett would
provide to light a way to a stage Heaven. A
savage criticism of the canvas is that children
munch their chocolates as they look on it. They
are not found chewing before “Anguish,” or
“ The Crisis,” or “ Quatre Bras.”
The Argus Mackinnons arrive shortly with
a Kosciusko height of luggage. They have
been shooting on Scotch moors, and hunting in
swagger Scotch company. They return with the
spoils of the chase —as fat and prosperous-looking
as ever.
The George CJhirnsides have never become
reconciled to life in this land since their last
costly gad abroad. Just lately they tried a jaunt
to Maoriland, but returned to their South Yarra
mansion more bored than ever. Mr. Chirnside
slept through some of the finest scenery in Maori-
land, and was conveyed through the rest reading
a newspaper.
The marm who accompanies her fair chicks to
the seaside in search of pleasant and approved
masculine company, is one comic hot-season
object; but the mother who comes along to guard
her boys from the Harpies, the Gorgons, and the
Sirens of beach-haunts is funnier. One of her
materialised at Lome this scorching season, and
everywhere the sonnies went their ma was sure to
go. The recreating girl was in despair. At last
the Revolt of Boy happened, and thereafter that
Roman matron only met her offspring for a brief
feeding interval, when the table was surrounded
by grinning pals, and all private warning as to
the wiles of Girl was rendered impossible.
All the rest of the day the sonnies pursued Eve’s
daughters with cheery enthusiasm.
Bendigo has broken out into a matrimonial rash.
The corner in bachelors, so long the boast of the
Bendigonians, slowly but surely yields to the
inroads of ■'Cupid. Every time a Melbourne
charmer is responsible. Two wealthy sons of
Father Abraham have launched upon the troubled
sea of matrimony with Christian mates, to the dis-
turbance of two opposing sects. Later, one
obdurate bank-manager, long regarded as a hope-
less sticker, faced the rails with a dasher of Block
fame. Now two solicitors and a medico are on the
parson’s mat and Quartzopolis is rubbing its eyes
in wonder.
Napoleonic stationary is being much distributed
over this city. One lot is gold-lettered, and
another coroneted. One young person has written
to a weekly publication denying that she is the
other person, and signs herself L. L. Napolona
Van de Leur. “ L’Aiglon,” it seems, brought
forth congratulations from sundry local descen-
dants of Buonaparte, and now Miss Brune is
corrugating her industrious brow in an effort to
classify those Napoleonic nexts-of-kin who kindly
admired her impersonation of the consumptive
member of the family. Some portraits of de-
scendants of Napoleon have a place in the Tittell
Brune private' gallery. They represent two young
things in black frizzy hair, scarlet garb, and a
weird Egyptian solemnity of expression. Their
sense of family greatness is very obvious.
The marriage of Miss Oliver and Mr. Stuart
Murray is to provide the one Ash Wednesday
revel. High-Churchers are supposed to feel re-
luctant about the Voice breathing on such a grey
Church day, but the Olivers are Scotchbyterian,
and have no scruple. The illness of the bride-
elect broke in for some days upon a busy pro-
gramme of girly farewell sprees, and at one time
it was feared that even the Chant would have to
be postponed, but better days came and saved the
scheme.
Miss Lesly Madden, with the Vallanges, is
housed at Frankston dreaming away the idle
noons. Presently the lugubrious Captain Vallange
ships home to receive an extra stripe or gold braid
tab as an emblem of a fresh military honor.
The farewell spree accorded to a girl that’s
going to make a good match is a trial to the
seasoned spinster who has had to cheer dozens of
such brides-elect. The effort to look blissfully
gay and happy under such circumstances some-
times ends in a painful grimace. Only the iron-
nerved and the steel-hearted can work up the due
expression of debonair gratulation. _ But the
fledgling is genuine in her gush. She is only out
a brief season and knows no malice. In fact,
youth and health work for optimism, and she can
blithely cheer the bride in the full rosy hope that
her turn comes presently and the millionaire is
close at hand. If the feted one have any nice
feeling she’ll not rejoice too aggressively in her
good luck. But every She is at heart a brag,
and the chance to impart spasms of envy to her
darling chums is not an easily resisted temptation.
A few were forward with autumn riggings, and
a great many with fresh top-sails for Saturday’s
race meeting. The Caulfield rally went with
such uncertain skies that these cold-weather
appointments were in part excusable. Funny
how fashionables take headers into a fresh season !
While the shoe-gear is still of the summer sum-
mery, the hose en suite, and the under-frills not
more warm or substantial than spiders’ meshes,
the millinery proclaims the fall of the leaf. On
Saturday, Mrs. George Fairbairn, Mrs. I). Casey,
Mrs. Leslie Macdonald, and Miss Byron Moore
were a quartet (though not exactly birds of a
feather) m cornflower blue. Little Miss Todd
—one of the small heiresses of the State
—tripped, it in crimson lake from toque
to toe. Mrs. Herbert Power led a host in ruby
top-sails. Mrs. Jack Frisken sailed in laces of
rare value. As a head-mount, the high dame
flourished a brilliant cerise, with a dash of white
plumage up the side. A second figure in crimson
lake was Miss Rita Kiddle, and her millinery was
the newest and sauciest on the course. Round a
“coolie” crown ran a garniture of French pink-
red roses. Long floating gossamers marked the
fashion of yester-year. Tails no longer brand the
wearer high-grade. Now they either look redun-
dant or giddy.
A frail, pretty English visitor —Mrs. Mundell
(much accent on the “ Mun ”)—was trotted out to
the Caulfield course by a large Todd-Laidlaw
contingent On Saturday. The touring one wore a
French black muslin with introductions of con-
ciliatory white to reduce the sombreness of things.
A remarkable figure in all black was Mrs. C.
Buckley—the imported wife of the eldest of the
Mars Buckleys.
The Spook and Bump business must be paying.
One of its professors is located at a, leading
hostelry where he “receives” in luxurious state.
In the forenoon he struts in snowy linen swinging
a gold-headed cane. At night his shirt-front is
studded with fine pearls. And the women of the
land acclaim him great.
Mr. Rupert Clarke arrives back presently, all
trouble with the eyes being now a thing of the
past. From recent reports of Dame Clarke’s
movements between Mayfair and the Buck
country, it is gathered that she’s a stayer for the
nonce in England.
Once more the Reid family, of warehouse
wealth, are off on a gad. The beauty of the flock
tries a second trip for her health.
A young religious squatter who spent much
time converting (?) the local white she-heathen
(barmaids preferred), escaped matrimony and the
permanent clutch for a long time, till one experi-
enced Juno came along. She ’verted slowly but
surely and nailed the amateur missionary. They
are now married. It’s a dangerous thing to go
trifling with a girl’s religion. Her millinery or
her high heels are fit subjects for correction, but
not her faith.
Menzies’ tries a fresh novelty next week, when
the Wool Clan are about, and horse interests
uppermost. On Monday, Wednesday, and Fri-
day there’ll be a programme of music in the
Marble Court, where Cuyler Hastings used to
hold the eoffee-sippers entranced. Monsieur
Napoleon Boffard, of the ingratiating smile, is to
run the show. The idea is to help along the
stylish interests of this hub during the Flemington
Autumn Carnival, and it ought to go. The Cream
dearly love a nibble of a wafer and music to help
along gossip.

Stephen Webley Parry, recently dead at
Yamburra (Q.), aged 71, arrived in Victoria from
England in 1856, and became owner of Murray
Downs station. Afterwards, he was in the horse-
dealing business in Adelaide, and shipped the
first lot of Australian horses to India. Later, he
went to Abyssinia, and took part in Napier’s
Magdala expedition, the time Theodore, the
Emperor, tried to argue things with a flint-lock
gun and failed. Then he returned to Australia,
and was connected with Burke and Wills explor-
ing misery. His later life was comparatively
restful.
The last word has probably been said
about William Shiels, Victoria’s croaking Trea-
surer. The electorate of Normanby, which he
represented for 26 years in the Victorian Assem-
bly, presented a memorial address to Mrs. Shiels,
the late politician’s widow. Very few were
invited to be present—only Jimmy Munro, Allan
M'Lean, Iceberg Irvine, and Thomas Bent.
Three of these were Premiers under whom Shiels
had served. But only Bent, who goes to every-
thing, and the Iceberg turned up. Poor old
Jimmy Munro, paralysed and speechless, spends
his days being wheeled about in an invalid’s
chair, and M'Lean was somewhere else. Still,
the Iceberg and Bent made speeches to one
another, and to Kirby, ex-Mayor of Casterton,
who ran the show, and tried to behave like a large
and enthusiastic public meeting.
Apropos the Wigram Allen widow (B.
22/2/’O6). This lady was so harassed by beggars
when resident at the Glebe—and she was always
open-handed—that she was compelled to take up
her residence at a far-away suburb ; but the
mendicants, well-dressed beggars and plate-
pushers followed her. Now she goes to the Cold
Country for respite. Miss Eadith Walker, who
used to give money indiscriminately, now gives a
certain society £l5O a year, besides maintaining a
few old pensioners who have been on her books
for years.
“Bill Hiplien ” writes a letter to “His
Majesty, the King of Ingland,” per favor of The
Bulletin :
Honored Sir.—l’m only a humble Australian, born of
poor but imported parents, but I ’ave a word o’ warnin’
for y’.
I ’ave noticed with alarm the deplorable and increasin’
increase in the Labor party in your Parleyment.
If you knew what this meant as well as I do, you’d lose
weight. Yessir, you’d stay awake at nights and sweat
copious. If y’u’d ’ad a s’lection on the Mitchell and was
maltin' your good two quid a week milkin’ 44 cows, an’ all
of a sudden the Labor party gits into power and your best
milker goes blind, and two of yo’ poddy calves gits bogged
in a crik, an’ yo’ ’orse gits the spavins, and yo| two dogs
gits theirselves pisened, and a bush-fire licks up yo’
fencin’, an’ yo’ draw a blank in Tatt’s. while a cove like
Jim Higgs catches a £5 prize, and the missis gets sick, I
asks yo’ what’d y’ think about the Labor party ?
I warn you solemn —look out for yo’self. ’Fore yo’
knows where yo’ are they’ll ’ave yo’ off er yo’ throne,
snatch yo’ habeas corpus, and y’u’ll be streakin’ for the
bush for yo’ life in yo’ shirt. Trustin’ you will be warned
in time, and deal with the brutes afore they gits to you,
I remains, yours very cordially, Bill Hiphkn.

This is the age of cleanliness and common
sense, and the smart woman of the period demands
fabrics which oan be washed frequently, and will
not shrink or lose their color in the process. To
meet this demand the “ Viyella ” fabric has been
invented, a woollen mixture material made in
three varieties: “T—Light; TT—Medium; and
TTT—Heavy.” The first of the list is suitable
for ladies nightwear, dressing-gowns, and those
blushful but useful garments, bloomers; also
blouses, children’s frocks, and so forth. The other
two weights are suitable for either costumes or
men’s suitings. The variety of colors, shades
and patterns is infinite. Garments made of the
lightest material, inlaid (that is not the correct
word, but it’s near enough for a plain man) with
nice torchon lace, or silken borders, or flat inset
lace, are really fascinating, and at the same time
lend themselves to any number of washings, and
a clean underskirt waving over a muddy street
is a thing of beauty and a joy for all time.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
Sydney Telegraph foolishly suggests to
the Sydney boot trade, which is over-producing
just now, “ export to England and America.”
Any boots sent to the United States would be
quite worn out climbing the tariff wall.
The British Order of the Garter has been
conferred on the J apanese Mikado and accepted
“as a sign of the breaking down of the racial
prejudice between Europe and Japan.” Well,
the Order of the Garter was begun in shame; it
fittingly ends in degradation.
From London Morning Leader’s descrip-
tion of Manchester when Balfour went down with a
dull sickening, etc., on polling day :
The streets were simply swamped with handbills and
cartoons, the one which made the most; impression being 1
the Leader’s reproduction of the cartoon from the Sydney
Bulletin : “ These be chy gods.”
Now that Crickolas Willis is obviously not
coming back, what is the N.S.W. Government
going to do with those two minor captures, Hos-
kins and McNair ? The Bulletin foigets whether
they have been remanded 13 or 28 times, but
whichever it is, the interminable dragging out of
their cases has become a public scandal.
The three S.A. delegates to the Chambers
of Manufactures Congress, at Launceston (Tas.),
were accused by a visitor of inconsistency, in
wearing an obviously high-class English-made
tweed while supporting the claims of the local
article. Whereupon they rose as one man and
gave thanks for the compliment paid to Lobethal
(S.A.) cloth, which all three of them were wearing.
State Premier Carruthers of N.S.W. lately
went to look at Monaro, the place he has been
denouncing as quite unfit for an Australian capital,
and came back full of enthusiasm about its beauty
and its climate. Perhaps if he had looked at
Monaro a little earlier he might have saved him-
self a lot of needless complaint and grumpiness.
Some of the men who selected Dalgety knew the
country. Joey apparently didn’t.
A Melbourne printing-ink manufacturer
exposed to the Tariff Commission lately one of
the details of the great “dumping” industry,
“ It puzzles us,” he said, “ to know how imported
ink, after paying 25 per cent, duty, besides freight
and landing charges, can be sold at a lower price
than the manufacturers’ price in the country in
which it was made.” But, of course, all that loss
will be recouped when the Australian manu-
facturer has been wiped out. It always is.
The postmaster at Coraki (N.S.W.), who
held the one position since 1879, with a
total service of 30 years, on a salary of
less than £3 a week, has been “sent up”
for two years for embezzling £776 16s. sd.
He would have been entitled to retire pre-
sently with £350 compensation, and he is
guaranteed for £4OO, so that the post-office loses
little. What can be said of such a salary for a
man 53 years of age, and with such responsibili-
ties? And what sort of an audit was there?

And when the Ereetrade members of the Tariff
Commission heard that evidence, they suddenly
remembered that they had to ask a lot of questions
about the price of pigs in Dublin and other irrele-

Postmaster-General Ward, of Maoriland,
goes to the Postal Conference at Rome to advo-
cate penny postage all over the world. Maoriland
is, perhaps, just a little too agitated about the
convenience of the world, and a little too large in
its beneficence. While its eye is fixed so much on
big things abroad, its own debt grows in a terri-
fying fashion. Just now, things are good, and
the Maorilander sits in peace under his own pine
and big tree, but the debt, if it continues to
increase at the present rate, will eat up him and
his prosperity in the end, and then it will avail
him little even though he has farthing postage to
Greenland.
The Greater Melbourne scheme is steadily
growing. Last year the city corporation annexed
the municipalities of North Melbourne, Fleming*
ton and Kensington as Hopetoun Ward. Now
Brunswick, Fitzroy and Port Melbourne have ap-
plied to be included, and a committee has been
formed to arrange the business. It only wants
Collingwood, Kew and Hawthorn to come in to
unify the northern suburbs. South of the Yarra
there are Prahran, South Melbourne, St. Kilda
and Brighton. South Melbourne, so long as the
present mayor has any power, will refuse to unify,
though the other three might come in. The ad-
vantages to be gained by centralisation in Mel-
bourne are enormous, and the outside municipali-
ties are just beginning to see it.
G. H. Reid’s political stock must be very
low, since he has accepted a challenge to a public
debate on Socialism. The first challenger was
Wilson Wilson, an American visitor to Sydney,
whose Socialism is of the “ extreme ” kind ; but
the N.S.W. Labor League executive wouldn’t
endorse him as its representative, and W. A.
Holman takes his place. Holman is to be compli-
mented for stepping into the breach to ensure that
the Labor party view of Socialism is not misre-
presented ; but the debate is essentially a ridi-
culous notion. It can have no useful effect, except
to give an advertisement to the contestants. But
it shows how hard up G. H. Reid is for a little
notoriety that he drops to this junior debating
society game.
It was during the 1901-2 session of the
Commonwealth Parliament that M'Millan made
the ridiculous statement in the House of Reps,
that one of the great factors in the “natural
Protection ” of Australian-made agricultural
machinery was the heavy freight the imported
article had to pay. In spite of the evidence before
the Tariff Commission, Sydney Telegraph has
gone on, parrot-like, repeating that silly in-
accuracy as an argument on the Harvester ques-
tion. But last week it made a startling discovery
and announced that “it often costs pretty well as*
much to send goods to Westralia as it would to
send them _to London.” The Telegraph, if it
wants to print the whole truth, can easily ascer-
tain that it costs £5 more to send a harvester
from Melbourne to Geraldton or Fremantle than
to ship it to the same ports from New York.
Main reason for the difference seems to be that
the companies composing the Standard Oil Trust
(among which is the Harvester Combine) are
shareholders in what is known as the General
Transportation Company, which is able to cut
freights by using cheap tramp steamers.

An engineer and brass-founder—with liis
eye on Freetrade-Labor Fowler—told the Tariff
Commission that “ the reduction in the tariff and
the raising of workmen’s wages had brought down
the manufacturer; he suggested 10 years of a
settled policy with a 40 per cent, duty.” The
Freetrade-Laborite is like the drunken or gam-
bling house-father who insists that his wife and
children shall be well fed and clothed, but regu-
larly fails to provide the needful funds. And the
world puts him down as a fraud and a hypocrite.
During the year which ended on 30th
June, 1905, the aggregate public debt of the
federated States increased by £2,945,109, and
N.S. Wales was responsible for £2,288,417 of the
increase. Under its Economical and Deform
Government the Ma State is indeed doing well.
Westralia, which added £552,485 to its debt, is
also going strong ; and S.A. increased its moun-
tain of liabilities by £179,900. On the other hand
Victoria, Queensland, and Tasmania all reduced
their debts a little. As for Maoriland, it became
a raging maniac, and added £2,389,785 to its
accumulated troubles.
The glass manufacturers who appeared
before the Tariff Commission had a short and
simple tale to tell. They have to pay 425. for a
week of 48 hours. They, have from 10 to 20 per
cent. Protection, but the difference in hours and
wages gives some of their foreign competitors
fully 20 per cent, advantage all the same. China
is beginning to make glass, and the Chow gets
30s. a month, and works over 12 hours a day,'
including Sundays—3os. for about 400 hours.
Therefore, Australia spends about £280,000 a year
on imported glass, and thereby deprives about
2800 people of a job—a proceeding which is calcu-
lated to make any patriotic Australian go into the
back yard and bray at himself in disgust.
Digby Denham, Q. Railways Minister,
has marked the Cloncurt-y railway on his office-
pad. It is to be pushed on with all speed. The
50 miles of rails which have been rusting by the
Gulf for 16 years are to be steamered round to
Townsville, and then trucked to Richmond, when
work will be commenced in earnest. Digby
declares that the sanctioned link of 90 miles will
be built within two years, and that next Parlia-
ment will see the section authorised right to Clon-
curry. There is a movement on foot already to
extend the line further, and penetrate the
Georgina country, said to be equal in grazing
potentialities to the best Gulf belts. If it could
only be extended into the heart of the Northern
Territory, and Australia had the right population
to send out to pioneer the country, then !!!
Australia seems likely to suffer almost as
much from the misguided enthusiasm of its
friends as from the virulent attacks of its foes.
Nothing can be gained by over-painting a picture.
Rev. E. H. Sugden, head master of Queen’s Col-
lege, Melbourne, told Birmingham (Birm. Mail,
14/12/’O5) that
The Government of Victoria now had 13,000,000 acres
of land still ripe for distribution. Any man who went
out to Victoria could apply for 200 acres of land. If he
had a wife he could apply for 200 acres for her ; and if he
had children over 18 he could have 200 acres each for
them.
That does more harm than good. When the
Prime Minister of the Commonwealth admits
that “ farmers’ sons have left Victoria in hun-
dreds because land is not available ” it is folly to
talk to possible emigrants in England of 13,000,000
acres “ ripe for distribution.”
Joe Cook is about to get snubbed severely
by his leader, the Globular One. In his fierce
hatred of the cause from which he ratted, Joseph
has been denouncing the graduated land-tax pro-
posals of the recent Labor Conference in Sydney
as “ Confiscation,” “ Highway robbery,” and other
atrocities. Joe’s zeal has outrun his discretion,
and has created a pained impression among many
ardent Freetraders. They dislike to see the
deputy leader giving away the fact that the Great
Freetrade and Liberal Party is the party of the
Great Land Grabbers. Just now the demand for
population is urgent, and the Freetrade papers
have been saying quite a lot about “ land settle-
ment ” and the “great natural industries,” and
when Joseph comes along and announces that
his party has no idea of trying to fill up the
country with population, and prefers to let the
mighty landlord keep his millions of idle acres, it
makes those papers look foolish.
The Federal Commission on the Tobacco
Industry has, by 3 to 1, reported in favor of nation-
alising the trade. It is calculated that some
£300,000 a year) additional to the present Customs
and excise revenue, would be gathered in by a
State Tobacco Monopoly. But a change in the
Constitution is necessary before such a step is pos-
sible, and that change is suggested. There is no
doubt that Australia wants that £300,000 a year.
So soon as the Commonwealth begins to do its
duty as a nation—to provide for proper defence
by land and sea, and to establish old age pensions,
&c. —revenue needs will increase, especially as by
then a tariff which has become really Protective
will begin to fail as a revenue-raiser.
One day lately the Tariff Commission was
invited to look down the barrel of the deadly
seven-and-sixpenny revolver and the murderous
thirty-shilling gun that comes into Australia from
Belgium, America and England. Expert Lam-
pard described some of the death-traps which had
been brought to him for repair. One had the
barrel bent double ; it had blown off the gun, but
had been considerate enough to fly up into the air
instead of killing a citizen or so. Another had
burst the breech at a spot where the iron
was one-seventh of an inch think. Belgian
guns, these people judged, were subjected to a
very low test, and American single breech-loaders
to none at all. Rosier, who has been handling
these things all his life, suggested that importers
should be forced to test every gun they brought in
—cost roughly estimated at 2s. Gd. a gun—and
Lampard made the better suggestion of fixed
duties to keep the most hopeless of the cheap rub-
bish out. Australia, as a country where every
person is more or less in the habit of firing a gun,
wants something of the sort if the life-insurance
business is to prosper.
Freetrader Fuller acquired unexpected
information from a witness before the Tariff
Commission. The witness asked for a 20 per
cent, duty on bevelled glass. Fuller: “You
are a Prohibitionist, I gather?” “No. I am
a Freetrader,” “ But you have just been asking
for Protective duties.” “ Well, lam a Freetrader,
but I suppose it is the want of education. I
was brought up a Freetrader, but when Protection
enables me to employ 20 men as against 10 at pre-
sent, it makes me think.” Australia is full of men
like that, violent Freetraders, whose demand for
Protection in their own industries is like the cry
of a starved wolf which wants beef handed down
to it.
Japan, which is said to have a great famine
on hand and to be in need of assistance from out-
side, has just commenced paying off its public
debt of about £182,000,000 (roughly about £4 per
Jap). And Australia, which owes between £5O
and £OO per head, and reckons itself so poor that
it has to keep on borrowing, and which hasn’t
managed yet to scratch together a copper for its
essential transcontinental railways, and can’t (or
thinks it can’t) even keep up a semblance of an
army and navy for its own defence—Australia has
been appealed to for assisw.nee on account of the
alleged Jap famine. It is a curious world.
Wm. Sandford, of Wm. Sandford Ltd.,
during a recent visit of a State Ministerial ptrty
to the Eskbank (N.S.W.) ironworks, stated:—
Before entering into this contrast (with the Carruthers
Government for iron and steel supplies) Mr. Thornley and
myself had an interview with Mr. Reid and Mr. Cook,
when Mr. Reid said, “ When it comes to a question of 10
or 12j( per cent, duty you won’t find me against you.”
Thus the great Freetrade leader tries to nobble
Protectionists’ votes by promising to support
Protective duties. And seemingly Cook agreed
with his alleged leader—at all events Cook didn’t
repudiate the idea with the indignation one might
expect.
The old crowd which used to say that the
Queensland Laborites were not to be trusted be-
cause they knew nothing of business methods is
wishing to Heaven now that they didn’t know so
much. Brisbane City Council, pushed on by the
land-owners, went to Premier Kidston, the other
day, and asked him to give it the old Lands Office
in George-street, so that it might continue the
now-blind Adelaide-street through to North
Quay. “See,” said the representatives of the
property-owners—“ see what an advantage it
would be to have the street open right through.”
“ Yes,” said Kidston, “ and I see who would reap
most of it.” And never a bit would he budge
from his attitude that, if the State gave up the
land, it must be paid, just as any other business
concern would demand payment —as the Gas Co.,
for instance, did a while ago.
“The colonies” serve all sorts of pur-
Eoses, and furnish all kinds of cranks with a
andy excuse. The latest instance is the wail of
a Dublin Orange Lodge, which says that if
Princess Ena of Battenberg becomes a Roman
Catholic in order to marry Alfonso of Spain, the
event will “imperil the ties binding the Pro-
testant colonies to the reigning family in Great
Britain.” If the Dublin Vegetable Lodge is
serious in saying this, it greatly over-estimates
the importance which “the Protestant colonies”
attach to the little Battenberg girl’s religion.
It is questionable if one of them would turn a
hair if Henry Battenberg’s daughter became a
Buddhist. If she is willing to change her belief
in order to get a husband she is a mighty poor
Protestant, and she will make a very unimportant
Roman Catholic, and Alfonso is welcome to his
bargain.
Prime Minister Bannerman, of Britain,
states proudly that the recent general elections
have “ given Protection its quietus,” and doubt-
less the poor, ordinary old man really believes
this. A Roman emperor who was much younger
and less ordinary than Bannerman, gave Christi-
anity its quietus once m like manner; also there
was once a person who climbed up a ladder to blot
out the sun with his hat. One of the first acts
perpetrated under the wretched Bannerman
Government, according to the cablegrams, has
been the handing over to the Chinese autho-
rities, on demand, of a policeman in British
employ, who is said to have accidentally
touched a mandarin’s flunkey with his stick while
directing the traffic, and thus brought discredit on
the mandarin. The miserable wretch received
1000 lashes, and was then sent to a Chinese gaol
for a long term. Presumably this was done lest
the Chinese should get angry and shut out two-
penn’orth of British calico. Under Freetrade the
British market is flooded with about £150,000,000
worth of foreign manufactured goods annually;
the Britisher has to find foreign markets some-
where to make good the loss of his home business ;
and he has to eat dirt at the feet of all manner of
niggers and heathens in the struggle after “trade
outlets,”
SENATORS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Argus recommends William Zeal, Simon Fraser
and George Turner for a trio of Senators to be elected
this year ; because “ they are men for whom the pro-
ducers, the Protectionists, and the low-tariff party could
all vote, for they are sound in their opposition to Social-
ism, and in other matters they are not extremists.”
Oh ! when ’tis time to scratch the names upon the Senate
lists,
Let’s vote for dummies—neither high nor lowly tariffists—
Who’re sound in their adhesion to their fog-bound policy
Of letting things be as they are, however bad they be.
Unbiassed by conviction and unprejudiced by sense,
Vote for three bulky negatives a-sitting on the fence.
Let’s vote for Turner, Fraser, Zeal—blank zeros sound
they are,
Who moderately vote for nothing in particular ;
Their gravity is weighty, and their feet are ever placed
On the road which leads to nowhere and with no un-
seemly ha:.te.
Vote for three drear hypotheses to guide our Common-
weal ;
Three solid hunks of meat and gloom—Sim Fraser, Turner,
Zeal 1
