SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Y.R.C. Autumn fixture seems to have
gained a lot of local public interest from the sifting of
“ form ”at the recent Caulfield meeting. A fortnight ago
the Victorian horses gave little promise of making a
strong opposition to the vaunted visiting cracks, but if
Caulfield tests were at all trustworthy, the invaders from
over the border are not going to have the sprints to them-
selves anyhow. In the long distance events they may
repeat their superiority of four months ago. Lady Wal-
lace looks a very good thing for the Leger, and in the
Australian Cup list there is only Circuit among
the Victorians with a visible chance of beating such
proved performers as Tartan, Blue Spec, Scot Free and
Ossian. At time of writing the placed horses for the
Melbourne Cup—Blue Spec, Scot Free and Tartan—are
almost worth backing against the field for the Australian
Cup, and if they all go to the post in their best fettle it
will not be 20 to 1 against them finishing at the head of
the procession in the same order as before. But the pre-
sent scribe, in preference to predicting a miraculous coin-
cidence, will plump for Tartan to win this time, with
Spec and Scot following him home.
As for the Newmarket Handicap, a scramble
that ought to produce a lot of reasonable betting in a
fairly open market, the balance of chances now appears
to be in favor of the local stuff. It was a revival of old
times on Saturday to see Gladsome come with a rush and
win the Futurity Stakes from the imported 3-year-old
Duke of Melton, with old Pendant only a head behind
the youngster. And again when Palindrome and Medal-
list “ pipped ” Istria at the finish of the Hawksburn Han-
dicap, six furlongs (1.14 J). If Gladsome were to fly off
the mark next Saturday, the mare should win the
Newmarket, for she meets her five best followers
in the Futurity upon terms that will be easier,
or not more unfavorable to her. But Gladsome is
not a quick beginner among sprinters as a rule, and
perhaps Lachlan (7st.), who ran well enough last Satur-
day (and will have an advantage in the weights
over Duke of Melton, Pendant and Pompous) may con-
trive to do the trick. The Newmarket looks open to all
the eight nags aforementioned, without including
Machine Gun (11.3) and Wandin among the very probable
ones. Last year’s “form” is represented by Medallist
who ran third to Playaway and Lord Ullin’s Daughter, and
when the somewhat infirm Medallist gets thoroughly fit,
as now, he is one of the best. Guessing at the Newmarket
result from what was seen at Caulfield and what was
heard about the luck of the running, this prophet places :
Lachlan 1
Medallist 2
Pompous 3
• * » *
The bookies got a bad showing up by the tote
the other day at some amateur races at Walcha.
Hooke, of Jeweller fame, reckoned he had the Cup as good
as won with„a horse named Keeper. But Keeper died a
day or two before the race and he started another (Blue
Belle) as a forlorn hope. There were only four starters,
but the books opened out and laid 10 to 1 Blue Belle.
But the amateurs were running a blind “parimutuel ” on
the race, and on this Blue Belle, who won, paid 95 to 1.
Imagine anyone asking Moses for 95 to 1 about a horse in
a field of four—or in a field of 14 for that matter.
* * * *
At a Broken Hill race meeting last week the
stewards heard something novel in the way of an excuse.
They had the owner and jockey of a certain horse up to
explain the animal’s running. The owner maintained that
he had backed his steed and was an innocent and
disgusted man who might be vainly searched for informa-
tion. Not so the jockey. The horse ran well, he said,
till they got to the far side of the course. Here a number
of camels were camped and the odor so broke up the noble
animal that it refused to gallop anj r more. The explana-
tion was so far accepted that the parties got off with a
caution.
* * * *
The audacity of John Wren Esq. seems to have
over-reached itself in his letter to Premier Bent offering
to rent the V.R.C. racecourse from the public for £25,000
a year, paying one year’s rent in advance. By persistently
advertising himself in this vainglorious way Wren is
merely hastening the day when the suppression of his
Tote scandal will be forced upon the Vic. Police and
Crown Law departments. Wren’s offer was used last week
as a means for poking borak at Premier Bent, the man
who makes a hot perspiring display of autocratic activity
in some directions, but is so strangely passive as regards
“ the Curse of Collingwood.” Somebody wrote to him
offering to pay “ an annual licence fee of £35,000 for the
right to run and conduct the totalisator in Victoria for a
term of ten years, with a view to removing the totalisator
from its present undesirable environments, and placing
its working in a healthj r and clean atmosphere, and
ensuring that the people as a whole shall get a fair share
of the profits of the machine.” Bent, being blind to the
fact that this satire on Wren’s proposal was a satire on
Bent’s Government, handed the letter to the Press as a
harmless joke.
* » * *
Time was when John Wren endeavored to keep
his identity a secret from the Melbourne public. That
was the time when the Collingwood Tote stood in some
danger of suppression by the Law. Now that it is a privi-
leged scandal, one sees a litbo. portrait of Mr. John Wren,
the face framed in a horseshoe, pasted upon all the hoard-
ings. The identity of the inflated Wren has come to be a
pictorial advt. of his ponv-race meetings, and he is pro-
bably the first racecourse proprietor who has billed him-
self as a special attraction. This must be very gratifying
to Samuel Gillott, the political head of the local Police
Department.
* * * T
M.L. Trotting Association has refused a license
to a lady to train horses.
* * * *
The buck-jumping contest that seemed likely to
eventuate between Skuthorp’s “ Dargan’s Gray ” and one
of Martini’s riders (Willie Waite for preference) is now off,
and both parties have withdrawn their stake from this
office. Skuthorp has issued a writ claiming £5OOO damages
from Martini for alleged breach of contract. This
contest, providing no settlement is effected, will be
fought out in the Supreme Court ring, where Gentleman
One John Mulligan will stage-manage for Skuthorp, and
Messrs. McCarthy and Maxwell act as Martini’s ring-
masters.
In the meantime the angry Skuthorp has gone
back to Queensland to bring his own show over to Sydney,
where it will open on the 24th of March. It is a fearsome
show, according to all accounts, and among the other
novelties a number of wild and woolly backbloxmen will
ride bullocks even wilder than themselves. A horse is one
of Nature’s gentlemanly creations, and even the wildest
buckjumper is content when he has thrown his rider, and
only once in a century tries to bite him when he is down.
But a bullock !! Skuthorp is running his show on lines
which will fetch out thebest horsemanship in the State. He
has announced a buekjumping contest open to all comers—
even Martini’s rough-riders can compete—for £lO0 —first
prize £75, second £2O, third £lO. A further prize of £2O
is offered for the best bucking| horse produced. Skuthorp
himself, the man who did the famous insanity act
known as “ The Adam Lindsay Gordon Leap,” will ride
anything in the shape of a horse that is brought along,
but he draws the line at bullocks—bullocks are infra dig.
Martini’s show was unusually bright on Satur-
day night. First of all a man in the audience accepted
the proprietor’s challenge to ride a vicious rearer for one
pound, and, walking into the arena, divested himself of
his coat and vest. It is usual in contests of this sort to
allow the rider to be seated, but Martini’s men handed the
nag over to the man to mount himself. The horse
reared and plunged, and the stranger, cool
as a cucumber, stood from under the striking
forefeet, and, seizing his opportunity, made ineffec-
tual attempts to mount the brute. People, afraid
that he would be killed, yelled to him to desist, whilst
others cheered him on, and all the time the young man
never turned a hair. There was a rear, a sudden leap, and
he was in the saddle, and had gained the stirrups. The
gee-gee plunged, reared and pranced round the ring, but
the cool youngman sat still. When the horse was ridden
out a man rushed out and smote him on the nose with
a knotted rope, and threw sawdust at him to
make him buck. The crowd yelled execrations
and threats of murder. And then the rider dismounted
and collected his pound. Wild liberality of prizes will
not break up the Martini show. The same night
“ Jigger ” Laval had a return contest with Bobs. Martini
announced that it was not a contest for money—he was
paying the “Jigger” to ride Bobs, but he was not
paying him to fall off. Bobs arched his back like a cat in
love and kicked out furiously. The “Jigger” held
round the ring, and then one more effort found him
standing up in the stirrups; another, and he and
the horse had parted company, and done it artis-
tically. Writer would like to see the “ Jigger ” ride,
or try to ride, Bobs for £lOO stake. The “Jigger”
looks like a horseman.
Buckjumper riding is almost a fine art, and
really ought to rank among the sciences. If it is a life
and death struggle between the man and the horse,
the horse has no show—the man could kill him every time
if the man knows his business. Martini has been show-
ing a number of horses, but none of them get their heads
down between their knees —not even Bobs. The horse
that doesn’t get his head between his knees is not a true
bucker; and the horse that puts his head between his
knees is lost—if the man can survive the first buck or
two—for a skilful horseman will let his head go down sud-
denly, and then a stunned horse rolls over. But the
trouble is to stay on board long enough to do the trick.
Hurdle racing and steeplechasing are sadly over-
done in England. The first half of the season—till the
end of December —saw 459 races decided for a stakes
total of £31,000. Average, £67 a race.
***•*•
Notwithstanding the fact that two previous
wins by the Petrel had decided the destination of the
Prince Alfred Yacht Club championship, no matter what
won Saturday’s event, the steamer was crowded. The
wind from the E.S.-E. was fairly strong in a spasmodic
manner, but was often light. The»yachts made a
flying start, rating allowances to be adjusted at the
finish, and they flashed across the line like a covey of
great white-winged birds. It was one of the best starts
seen on Port Jackson for many a long day. Sunbeam,
the scratch boat, struggled to the front and led round
the buoy at Manly, and thence to Shark Island, the Fort,
away to Watson’s Bay pile-light and home. But Scotia,
with a time allowance of lOmin. 35sec., finished only
smin. 42sec. behind the scratch boat, and thereby were
the Scriptures fulfilled, and the (second) last became the
first. Petrel finished behind Culwulla, third, but wins
the Championship with seven points ; Scotia is runner
up with five points, Culwulla third with four points,
while Magic and Fleetwing score one each.
* * * *
The much-talked-of match between the Maori-
land-built yachts Rawhiti and Bona duly came off on
Saturday last in Port Jackson, with the assistance of an
E.S.E. wind. Mr. Pittar’s Rawhiti is quite a new boat,
having been off the stocks only a few months, while the
Bona was constructed some years ago for Mr. J. H. Chin-
nery, who sold her to Mr. Binnie. On account of the dif-
ference in the rating-, the Rawhiti was called upon to give
Bona an allowance of 35sec. The race ended in a victory
for the older boat, which was more skilfully handled than
the new-comer, by 52sec., or 17sec. over and above the
rating allowance. Rawhiti is not a boat for inside sailing,
but the way it flew along and overhauled Bona when the
wind freshened indicated that under a stiff breeze and
outside conditions it is the boat to put the money on.
* * * *
Sydney Flying Squadron on Saturday sailed
two heats and an exciting final over the usual course.
George Ellis, having abandoned Vision, found himself at
the tiller of Blanche ; while “ Billy ” Dunn had replaced
brother Charles in the Crescent, and Federal and Merlin
both had new skippers in the persons of T. Leach and J.
Smith respectively. The first heat was won by Desde-
mona, which caught a southerly puff and ran in first,
with Australian, Alert, and Crescent behind in that
order. On the way to the starting point in the second
heat Merlin turned clean over, and Stella, after a bad
start, bent over and, sailing through the fleet, won easily,
with Zena, Donnelly, and Blanche behind. This brought
out nine for the final. Blanche, Donnelly, and Stella
were all late getting away. Ellis was reduced to a state'
of silent despair by his crew getting the jib overboard,
which event wrecked all hope of being in the first flight. -
At Clark Island, Crescent caught a puff and went to the
front, Federal went over, and Stella and Australian had
trouble with their ballooners. Crescent increased its
lead, and in the end won easily from Zena and Desde-
mona. Meanwhile Ellis has started out to instruct his
forward hands how to handle the jib.
* * * *
Yet another Australian oarsman has his eye on
the Diamond Sculls race at Henley (Eng.). F. S. Kelly is
on the spot already; T. Bourke left recently; and now
Victorian sculler Roy Adam is bent on a similar quest.
Although very young, Adam is all grit; but Bourke (all
going well) seems better than his opponent.
• * * *
N.S.W. Rowing Association doesn’t exactly
know whether to stick to its guns and send an eight-oar
team to Westralia or not. At any rate, the association
has started scraping up a shilling subscription for funds
to cover expenses, and has intimated that if the “ bobs ”
don’t roll in by a general subscription, it isn’t financially
able to stay the bottom from falling out of the proposal.
There has recently been quite an overdose of collecting
for amateur sporting proposals—a, circumstance which
isn’t likely to boost amateurism in the State. Rowing is
certainly the most charitable sport—the owners of the
river banks taking the bulk of the proceeds; still the
courtesy of the other State associations in sending along
teams which create the interest in Sydney should be
returned in similar manner.
* * * *
The Kieran memorial fund has none of the
celerity of the man whose fame it seeks to perpetuate.
So far, only £146 has been subscribed, and of this the
dead swimmer’s companions on the training ship are
responsible for £65. The same lads are giving an enter-
tainment at Hunter’s Hill Town Hall to-morrow (Friday)
night, to swell the fund a little.
. - • * *
The Board of Control for Australian cricket has
practically won its fight. Melbourne Club, which was
the backbone of the opposition, was brought to sudden
collapse. It was hard at work intriguing with the Mary-
lebone Club in England to prevent that body recognising
the Board’s existence. In this effort it was nobly
seconded by the bulk of the Australian XI. According to
Laver’s book they represented to the Marylebone Club
committee that the Board was a hole-and-corner mush-
room that would soon perish. Wonder what the Maryle-
bone Club thinks of these gentry now? For it was
apparently through their intriguing- that the Marylebone
Club made an ass of itself by declining the Board’s invita-
tion to send out a team in 1906-7 ; and if a team doesn’t
come under the Board’s auspices, it might as well
stay at home. Then the Melbourne Cricket Club saw the
wraith of electoral cricket rise in its path, and
once that materialised the Melbourne Cricket Club
would fall to the level of a mere district affair. The only
way to prevent that was to get representation on the new
Board, and, dropping its tomahawk, M.C.C. g'ot down on
its marrowbones and prayed. Its prayer was heard, and
the Board’s constitution altered to allow of one Victorian
delegate being nominated by the M.C.C. When the
Marylebone Club got a straight letter or two from the
Board’s secretary which told it plainly enough that if it
wanted its team to play with first-class represen-
tative players in the three eastern mainland States,
that team must come out under the auspices
of the Board of Control, it probably cursed the XI.
for having landed it in the dirt. It then affected
to be standing in a position of • strict impartiality,
and announced that it was “ perfectly willing to
negotiate with any body authorised to speak for
all the important cricket interests of Australia” —
whatever that may mean. Probably it was a mere jumble
of words to conceal the fact that the Marylebone Club
felt bad. Meanwhile Darling, on behalf of the S. Aus-
tralian Association, wants the financing of Australian
teams in England left optional. The Board will not hear
of it, and Darling says he holds in his pocket a written
invitation from the Marylebone Club to take an Australian
team to England in 1908. He may have the invitation,
but the Marylebone Club won’t recognise the team he
could raise in opposition to the Board of Control. The
time has come for the Board to hit Darling and his
precious Ass. hard by fixing a date up till which they
still may come in out of the wet, otherwise—stay out and
be swamped. If S. Australia finds itself being dropped in
the inter-State and English matches it will soon place
the Association where the man put the puppies, overboard
in a bag.
Seems to have been some deadly bowling in the
Melbourne v. Richmond match. The Melbourne club
only made 55 in the first innings. Three of the batsmen
failed to break their shells ; even Trumble only got 17,
and the side withered away under the deadly bowling of
Goss, who took seven of Melbourne’s wickets for 23.
When they got to the sticks, the Richmond men did
somewhat better, but nothing to crow about, and at
close of play had lost five wickets for 45. Is Goss The
Bowler YVho Is To Come ?
Contrary to expectation, the latest round of
Sydney first-grade cricket did not produce any wild
changes in the premiership lists. Waverley still leads,
with 10 points now, and Glebe and Burwood still run
neck and neck for second place with nine each. Behind
them North Sydney, with only a shadow of its former
greatness, toils miserably with six, and further behind
the other eight clubs are all something below zero.
Altogether it was a day of steady, consistent batting, the
only century being Pite’s 136 (not out) for Sydney District
against Paddington.
The bright particular feature of the day’s play
was Burwood’s marvellous recovery against Redfern.
Redfern, the previous Saturday, ran up 179 for the first
innings, and then took eight of Burwood’s wickets for 75.
Wallace and Garnsey were the not out men; the
former had made 13, while the latter had not started to
score. This left Burwood with 104 to make and only two
wickets to fall. Wallace added 16, when he was bowled,
and 87 were still wanted when the last man, O’Connor,
went to the wicket. It was a time for mighty slogging,
and Garnsey made 25 in fivers alone, and O’Connor also
hit a fiver. O’Connor had made 38 when he was caught
by Hopkins off Wadsworth, and Garnsey carried his bat
out for 48, and these, with 13 for sundries, gave Burwood
a first innings victory by nine. In fact, it was the byes
that won the game for Burwood, for, like the winning
margin, they numbered nine.
But it was a day of sensations. Waverley had
notched 305 the previous Saturday against the University,
and the collegians’ case looked hopeless. But Harvey
and Lane batted away, and the first wicket fell for 140,
and the Uni’s hopes began to rise. At 162 another wicket
fell, and then the sky of their prospects became overcast
once more. Fuller’s bowling began to tell, and a rot set
in till Fisher arrived, and, with the help of Penman and
McElhorte, carried the score from 212 to 299. Already
the Uni. barrackers were cheering for a win, but silence
fell like an avalanche as Hill held the ball at mid-on.
Stack scored one and then Newell clean bowled him,
leaving Waverley winner by five.
* * » *
Sydney Church of England Grammar School
and Sydney Grammar School both put up marvellous
performances in the Great Public Schools Premiership
contest on Saturday. The C.E.G.S. inflicted a crushing
defeat on the King’s School at Parramatta, winning by
an innings and 98 runs. One C.E.G.S. boy, H. Massie,
made 115 himself, which was only three short of the
total score of the King’s School first innings. At Hunter’s
Hill, the Grammar School beat St. Joseph’s College by
304 runs on the first innings. C. V. Gale made 144 to hi's
own bat, and E. Barbour backed him up with 80. Some
of these Grammar School boys are evidently going to be
heard of in big cricket shortly.
» * * *
A group of 12 representative N.S.W. cricketers,
published early in 1875, represents the team that played in
December, 1874, against Victoria at Melbourne, the Vic-
torians winning by six wickets and one run. The 12 in-
clude Joe Coates, George Gilbert, Nat Thompson, Ned
Sheridan, Charles Bannerman, Ted Powell, Ned Gregory,
Fred Spofforth, George Morgan, Edward Tindall, W.
Wood, and Edmund Barton, 12th man and umpire.
Coates, Gregory, Thompson and Morgan are dead.
Gilbert is, or was until lately, camping out some-
where. Sheridan had a tobacconist’s shop in Bris-
bane awhile ago. Bannerman, a professional umpire,
Powell, a solicitor, and Tindall, a police court official, are
in Sydney. Spofforth lives in England. W. Wood is, or
was until recently, postmaster at Campbelltown, and
Edmund Barton is a Justice of the High Court of the
Commonwealth.
* * * *
N.S.W. Championship Lawn Tennis Tourna-
ment will commence at Sydney Cricket Ground on March
2nd, and be continued every day to and including the
10th. Fourteen events are set down for decision, and as
all teams are practising hard, and the very best players
in Australasia will be present, play is expected to surpass
that at any previous tournament. The Sydney Lawn
Tennis Club will control and manage the tournament.
* * * *
Victoria has managed, by begging from Deme-
trius Phriedfish of Sparta, to get both its champion
athletes away to Athens for the Olympic games. The
usual money difficulty faced the Amateur Athletic Asso-
ciation at first. The sum of £135 was raised, but though
that would take Wheatley for the middle distance events,
quite another £lOO was wanted to send George Blake for
the Marathon race. The committee scraped together
another £5O, and then the bright idea struck it of cabling
to Greece. The Greek Consul was approached, and he
wired to his Government, asking it to help this great,
rich country with £5O. The cable was sent on a Satur-
day, and the £5O arrived from the beggared little king-
dom on Monday. It would have been a lasting diso-raoe
to Victorian athletics if Blake had been left behind. 8 H
is far and away the best distance runner Victoria has
produced so far, and an infinitely better man than Flack
over a distance, yet Flack just missed winning the Mara
thon race. Blake, if he goes out in anything like form"
should easily annex the 22-mile event. ° ’
* * * *
The Australasian Amateur Athletic Union has
decided to be represented at the Olympian games at Rome
in 1908. There is plenty of time for the Onion to pick
out a strong team of Australasian athletes to make the
trip. There have never been any plums in Australasian
amafeurism ; and the probability of a trip to Rome mav
put life into the languishing business. Between the
Roman show in 1908 and the Athenian show this year
ancient history is looking up. J
**' * •
N.S.W. Athletic League is pushing professional
pedestnamsm with much vigor. Thusfar this season
2100 athletes and 129 clubs are under the League’s wing
—in fact, it carries sufficient weight to scare away
almost all opposition. The League is also keeping Mo
from dabbling too deeply in the sport, and professional
pedestrianism at present has a much needed cleanliness
about it.
* * * *
Said that Cecil Healy may be dispatched at the
end of this week to represent Australian swimmers at the
Olympian Games in Athens. The Healy brothers are
steadily improving, and just at present are the most pro-
mising swimmers on the continent.
* * * *•
Outback, in Mangaweka (M.L.), a couple of
top-form girls had a row about a bat, and a challenge to
fight after school was accepted—conditionally. The
more particular girl of the two wanted her mother’s per-
mission to engage in war. The mother refused to have
her daughter’s clothes torn off her, and kept her at home;
so a valiant she-mate took her place, and the scratch-as-
scratch-can culminated in the whole school taking sides,
and going for each other with unbelievable ferocity. The
spectacle of 40 or 50 lumps of girl clawing and mawling
and screeching, and scratching and losing hair-
ribbon, and getting utterly wrecked, was remem-
bersome, and drew a large crowd ; but it was only when
the excited girls got to fighting when lying on the ground
and tearing each others’ stockings and things off that in-
terference was suggested. There was many a sad little
heart in Mangaweka that night when parents added their
contribution to the bat dispute.
* * * *
The ineffable cheek of some people. An Inver-
cargill (M.L.) woman, Mrs. Nora Cavanagh, drew £4OOO
in Tatt.’s sweep, and one Lumsden waited on her,
and offered to identify her at the bank as “the light
party.” For this onerous service he asked £lOO. The
woman agreed, but thought better of it, and at the bank
got him to sign a paper accepting £5O as payment in
full. There was, of course, no trouble about her getting
the cash ; she didn’t require the interference of any
outsider, but in her ignorance of sweeps and things she
parted up. Then the man thought he should have had
the other £5O, and as it was refused with much en-
thusiasm, he actually sued for it. In court he denied
that he was agent for Tattersall, and the court, in giving
judgment against him, said he had no claim for com-
mission at all.

Turns out that the Germans have been using
three military automobiles against the Hereros in South-
West Africa. So far, no reports have been made as to
their effectiveness—but it is a fine, fascinating method of
warfare. The Raid of the Armored Motor-cars !

How tlie bird does fly ! Already they are draw-
ins up the Rugby programmes, and on April 7th the
“ flannelled fool ” and his wicket will pass and make way
for the “ muddied oaf’’and his gpal. April will be de-
voted to trial matches, and the competitions start in May.
There will he no bye this year, as the Union has decided
to admit one new club —either Manly or St. George. Now
that the heat of summer is here, the Sydneysider looks
forward with pleasure to drifting home after the football
match through the purple haze that bathes the dusty city
in picturesque glory in the month of May. vv hen Ma)
comes he will find the purple haze is cold as well as hazy.
A pretty (and petty) little Rugby squabble is
causing a lot of gas in Maoriland blown-leather circles.
When Bedell-Siveright’s English team toured the State
long ago, its members told the Canterbury Rugby Union
officials that they really didn’t want a banquet, so the
officials merely liquored them up to the extent of £4 65.,
and sent along the account to the Maoriland lvugb} 7-
Union. But the latter, which would cheerfully have
borne the cost of an expensive feed, disallowed the £4 Os.
for swankey. This riled the Canterbury Union, which
refused to 'stand the amount and said so. Then the
Maoriland Rugby Union disqualified Canterbury Rugby
Union until the amount was paid. As this disqualification
includes all players, the inference was that the Canter-
bury men playing in England would be included. The
Maoriland R.U. then saw its mistake, but stood firm and
announced that the players in question were under the
Maoriland R.U. and weren’t included in the disqualified
mob. Canterbury Union is fighting the thing out; the
travelling ’bailers are to be informed that they are dis-
qualified ; the Englishers are to be told that they have
been playing against disqualified men and also that the
laws of professionalism had been infringed, and the English
Union is to he asked what it thinks of the whole blessed
business. It would have paid the Maoriland R.U. to have
allowed that £4 6s.
* * • •
The way of a shark in the water has been the
subject of much debate recently, and a mass of evidence
has been brought to light which completely smashes up
a number of venerable popular beliefs as to the habits
and abilities of this objectionable frequenter of bathing
places. Many swimmers and more paddlers were under
the impression that sharks never enter breakers, and as
long as one remained in the actual surf there was no
danger. Now it is announced that one species of shark
practically lives in broken water, and can be seen rolling
in the breakers at any time. Another blissful belief was
that no ■ shark can swim in less than 6ft. of water,
but the man who was so cruelly mutilated in
George's River was in a comparative shallow, and
tales are told of 12ft. sharks floating in 18in. of water off
the Corso at Manly. Even the ancient belief that the
shark had to turn" on his back to bite has gone by the
hoard, and.a cloud of witnesses have testified to seeing
him make his breakfast with the right side of him upper-
most. There are many species of sharks, and they vary
in their habits and attributes—in everything, in fact,
except that they are dangerous to human beings
in the water. Bathing on open beaches in any part
of Australia is gambling with death, hut if people
will risk their lives, the danger, it seems, would be
materially reduced by wearing- black bathing dresses,
covering the whole figure. There is a sort of phosphor-
escent quality about the human body in the sea-water
that makes it visible a long way off, and attracts the
attention of the sea-scavenger. In the inner harbor at
Napier (Maoriland), which is infested with sharks and
stingrays, bathers used to clear the neighborhood by ex-
ploding dynamite cartridges in the water previous to
taking a dip. The shark has a stringent dislike for
dynamite, and after he has lost a scrap of fin or bit of a
tail in an explosion a blow on the water with the flat of
an oar will send him scurrying for his life.
* * * *
Santos Dumont, the Brazilian who went
around with steerable balloons in Paris, has been bitten
by a new bug. His latest craze is to try to reach the
North Pole in a 15,0001 b. dirigible gas-bag. His machine
is to be 195 ft. long and driven by a 70 h.p. petrol engine.
He has found another man as reckless as himself, and
the two will make their dash from Spitzbergen at the
end of the present year. In the meantime Dumont, who
is a man of fortune, should have a real good time, lest
his stay on earth he short.
“A.P.R.”: Is it a fact that the art of
“shooting” the breakers was originated by Island boys,
and was only introduced to Sydney about eight years
ago? It is very difficult to “beach” in a wave ; the
number of expert shooters in Sydney is probably less
than 100. In Manly an expert at the game commands
respect. In the breakers you hear muscular brown men
explaining to small, thin, white men : “ Get in the wave
just as it is curling, take one quick stroke, kick your leg,
and in you go. Here’s a wave—now, off ! ” And the
little man jumps forward too late, and the wave breaks
on him, turns him over three times, and dumps his nose
in the sand. Having swallowed a pint and a half of salt
water he arises, and has just scooped the sand from-his
ears and the seaweed out of his eyes when another
breaker knocks him over and a “ shooter” kicks him on
the jaw. Then the big brown man claps him on the back
and says, “ Good sport, old chap ! ” But the small, thin,
white man has had enough, and flees to the beach, with
the latest thing in seaweed still adhering to his eye.
•* * * *
Mr. R. Curnow writes: Cyclists’ Avenue, at
Moore Park, was not constructed, as stated in a recent
Bulletin paragraph, out of loan money. Part of the
land on which it lies belongs to the City Council, and
that body paid half the cost of making the path, the
remainder being raised by public subscription. The path
was maintained by the cyclists as long as funds permitted,
and then had to be allowed to get out of repair. As
honorary secretary of the movement I can absolutely
vouch for these facts.
* * ■* *
N.S.W. bike authorities have made an innova-
tion in connection with forthcoming Sydney Thousand.
Heats have been drawn, and the 120 entrants grouped
into 10 heats of 12 men each ; but competitors will lie
kept in darkness as to which heat they will run in till the
night on which it comes off. This should stop pre-
arranged teaming. *
Rutt’s time for the Adelaide Wheel Race, a
two miles event, was 4min. 2sec., not 2min. 4sec. as
erroneously printed last week.
* * * *
Australian Amateur Cyclist T. R. Hopkins,
who won the half, one, and five-mile Amateur Champion-
ships of the continent this season, has announced that he
is tired of racing for the silver trophy which will not
cyanide for the genuine thing, and will appear as an ama-
teur no more. He does not admit that he will be found
among the professionals, but that is a way the fast ama-
teur has. He has grown up in the idea that there is some-
thing not quite respectable about racing for filthy lucre,
hut his soul is sick of the brummy trophy. Then, in a
little while the idea of winning the Sydney Thousand
ceases to be horrible ; next, it seems almost respectable,
and finally—well, he finds that he 'is a full-blown profes-
sional.
* + * *
N.S.W. League of Wheelman intends restricting
its championship events to genuine Australian riders. It
is a commendable reform.
* » * *
The Motor-Cycle Club of Victoria will probably
hold a 100-mile road race shortly, over secluded and little
used country roads. .So far motor-bike road racing has
not caught on in Victoria, and on both occasions that the
big Dunlop Rubber Co. has tried to run one in connection
with the Warrnambool road race, the attempt has had to
be abandoned owing to heavy rains. Now, however, that
a more powerful class of motor-bike is on the roads the
contest will be almost sure to eventuate.
• * * *
The police raid on a Sydney betting shop last
week only captured a clerk and three old men. The Law
is evidently becoming convinced that it will have to get
up very early to successfully raid betting shops from the
front door. It was a story of manoeuvres in which the
police were badly left.
* » » *
Sydney National Sporting Club, last week, saw
the fall of ancient fighter Bill Smith. Paddington Pet
Peter Kling burst on him like a whirling tornado, and
the fists flew round in an indescribable manner. Smith
whirled his mauleys considerably, but the young man’s
carried the most sting. Smith tried all kinds of defence ;
he backed away, he fled, but the other pursued him, and
was faster on his feet. He fought back desperately, but
Kling was the stronger and more skilful fighter.
He clinched, but Kling would have none of it,
and, keeping both hands free, belted away ferociously.
Then he stood up and faced the gale. Kling’s wild charges
gave him a few openings, of which he took every advan-
tage, and it was obvious that a chance blow might sav e
William after all. But there was no chance blow, me
third round saw Smith beaten to the floor when he stood
up to his adversary, and belted on the back of the head
and shoulders when he ran, and when at last, he was laid
out the referee ended it by declaring Kling the winner.
It was a shockingly unequal struggle.
* * * *
L. Truscott mot R. Quigley at the Gaiety Club
(Sydney) on Saturday night, but the crowd had not
forgotten his walloping at the hands of Whitelaw at the
National Sporting Club, and declined to take him seri-
ously. Nevertheless, he put up a game struggle under
depressing conditions, and took his punishment, which
was heavy, like a Trojan. In the 18th round the referee
stopped the match, with a declaration that Quigley had
won. Truscott’s showing was an improvement on his
National Sporting Club display. #
The 21st half-yearly issue of J. J. Miller’s
Racing Chronicle covers the space from August 1,1905,
to January 31, 1906, and its prompt publication comes as
a boon to punters who are looking for recent facts and
figures to guide their erring notions of what’s what, lhe
Racing Chronicle, that started in fear and trembling
10 years ago, has been steadily gaining subscribers, but
there is still an army of haphazard punters who lose
several pounds on the gees, without thinking to spend
£1 per annum in a subscription to Miller’s handy history
of “form.” These neglectful speculators are advised to
diminish their yearly losses by applying for the biennial
book to the publishers at Post Office Place, Melbourne.
* * *
In their haste to ensnare the subscriber’s
“ brown,” some of the Sydney newspapers publish fishing
columns, and a whole column is sometimes devoted to
fishing notes. They mainly consist of pars, stating how
Messrs. Stiggins and Wiggins, of Alexandria, fishing all
day at Muddy Creek, caught six flathead, or how Mr.
Johnson, of Pitt-street, had a good afternoon in Taylor
Bay, where he would have caught a black bream only it
fled. As the heroes of these pars, are unknown to fame,
their doings must mostly be supplied by themselves, and
they are very dreary deeds.
“ T.D.” : The onrush of the alien in Eng-
land. In the West of London electorate, where I
have diggings, the two official candidates were
respectively German Jew and Italian by parent-
age ; but it is pleasant to be able to record that
the Hebraic Teuton was wiped out in this in-
stance. The only straight-out Englishman figured
at the foot of the poll with a few hundred votes.
A glance down the election lists reveals many
other German-Jewish names, likewise several
Indians, but up to the present there are no Chow
or Jap candidates. I foresee a time, however,
when the Jap, who is now bidding for places 1 n
Piccadilly clubdom, and moving in swell circles
with considerable diamond on his shirt front, will
also begin to offer himself for the allegedly British
House of Commons.
It was the brutal murder by the German
commanding officer of a chief who came to talk
over the trouble that caused the desperate Horeros
outbreak in German south-west Africa, and that
outbreak has run the Mailed Fist to several mil-
lions, and seems as far off finality as ever. Lately
the German Parliament seemed indisposed to
spend any more money on the little African war,
and the result is something like a general up-
heaval. Trouble has now broken out m German
East Africa, and is expected to extend to the
British territories in Nyassaland. Down in
Natal the Zulus have flung down the White Man’s
burden with an oath, and something like actual
war has commenced there. And the Briton, who
talks about holding the land as long as the sun
shines, imports Chinese slaves to fill the country,
instead of a sturdy white population which would
be a certain guarantee that it would become a
white man’s country in the fulness of time.
The struggle the N.S.W. law is making to
decide between two Wagga coclcies, who both
want the same piece of State land, has advanced
another stage. One Matthews was originally
successful in obtaining the land in February, 1904,
whereupon his rival (Graham) deposited £5 with
the Land Appeal Court and appealed. The court
granted bis appeal, but Matthews said that the
president of the court was dead, and he had not
heard of the gazettal of his successor, and that
part of a court was no court at all, and declined
to budge. Further, Matthews took his contention
to the Full Court, which upheld it. Meanwhile
the local land agent, as soon as the part of the
Land Court had given its decision, sent back to
Graham his £5 deposit. Graham, being bowled out
by the Full,Court, waited for several months before
another Land Appeal Court president was ap-
pointed, then sent along another £5 note and
again appealed. Matthews took no notice, and
the court again ousted him and gave the land to
Graham. Matthews again declined to budge, and
appealed to the Full Court once more on the
ground that Graham was allowed by law only 28
days from the date of the original decision (Feb.,
1904) in which to lodge his £5 and appeal, and,
having taken back his £5, his chance of appealing
was hopelessly lost. On this ground the Full
Court again blew Graham clean out, and so
Matthews succeeds to the land, and will remain in
possession unless the High Court is applied to,
and it decides differently. As things stand,
Graham has lost his land—(l) Because the State
Government supplied him with only part of a
Land Court to appeal to when he needed a whole
one, and (2) because a State Government official
sent back his £5 deposit when he shouldn’t have
done so. Which seems rough on Graham.

BEALE- PIANOS.
TONE QUALITY in a Piano depends upon many things, of which the
first and most important are the SCALE and the SOUNDING-BOARD.
By Royal Letters Patent.
512
Mo
Made in Australia.
jpP«®j|HE SCALE of a piano is the scientific plan of its sound-producing parts, the length, thickness, and radiation of the strings, and the striking-line of
W the hammers. The calculation of the scale is by far the most difficult part of piano manufacture, and can only be accomplished after many years’
9 m experience. Most of the makers of foreign pianos simply buy their frames (or “plates”) from foundry people who are in most cases quite ignorant
BawtcH of the mathematical requirements, and devoid of all scientific knowledge. One piano “ manufacturer,” in writing a “ Handbook of Piano-Manu-
facture,” recommends that a frame should be taken out of some well-known piano, and a casting be made from that, thus saving the trouble of
calculating a scale ! But this cannot be done successfully, for there is an exact allowance to be made for shrinkage in casting. The only correct and
scientific way is to first calculate the scale, making allowance for two shrinkages, then cast a pattern, and from that cast the frame to be used. This is the
method followed in the production of the BEALE AUSTRALIAN PIANOS.
The SOUNDING-BOARD is the second important feature. No ordinary care is required in its manufacture; the highest skill and the most
perfect knowledge only will assure satisfactory results. The timber must be carefully selected, of the very best quality, and the grain must be graded from
fine in the treble to coarse in the bass. The strips of timber, never more than five inches in width, must also be tapered in thickness from treble to bass.
The amount of camber, or convexity (like a violin-belly), of the complete sounding-board must be arrived at to a hair’s-breadth, so as to assure the best
quality of sound when the strings are vibrated.
In the Beale Australian Pianos all these details are most carefully watched by specialists of many years’ training, in their turn guided by a man whose knowledge -
of the whole result to be obtained—and the method of obtaining it—is unsurpassed in any part of the world. The materials are the very best that money can buy. Our
great aim is always “ How much better can we make it ? ” And to this end we spare no pains, giving our every thought to the perfecting of an Australian product that is
already far ahead of its old-world competitors. That this is so you can verify for yourself by comparison. Call and inspect our pianos, and then examine the goods of our
competitors. Our faith in our product is shown by the fact that we guarantee every Beale Australian Piano for Twenty-five years, whereas no importer will guarantee his
goods for more than Ten. Try, and see.

STRENGTH
KUTNOW’S POWDER is one of the most beneficial remedies for the improvement of health and the prevention of disease ; in fact, it is the one remedy
which enjoys the support and approval of the general body of medical practitioners. The reason for the recognition of its value b / u .™
physicians as PROP. LAWSON TAIT, M.D., SIR MORELL MACKENZIE, M.D., SIR SPENCEtt WELLS, M.D., and SIR ANDREW CLARK,
M D is not difficult of comprehension. Kutnow’s Powder is appreciated by doctors because they know that it contains nothing which is injurious, no
mercurial poison, nothing painful or drastic, and nothing but what is beneficial. The most delicate person can take it, even very young children and
invalids find it agreeable ; moreover, its action is so gentle and soothing that insensibly to the patient it efficiently does its work. It clears and Hushes le
entire system of all noxious debris and poisonous liquids, which action is invaluable to both sick and well. It gradually changes certain morbid conditions
of the system, and establishes healthy tissue, which alone is able to bring about a sound constitution. It stimulates the vital processes to renewe
activity, and enables the kidneys and bowels to remove waste matter which ought to be eliminated, one of the most important of which is URIO A .
In this way Kutnow’s Powder alters, corrects and purifies the fluids, gives tone to, and re-establishes the healthy function of, all the digestive and other
organs of the body. It cleanses and lubricates the human machinery. Kutnow’s Powder is an alterative which possesses tonic laxative, stimulan
and diuretic properties. It is a mild aperient which flushes the whole alimentary canal. It is a stimulant which has the power of increasing the vital
activity of the body. It is a cholagogue which never fails to make the liver act properly. Its alkaline action neutralises excessive acidity in the stomach
and bowels. It is an antiseptic which prevents putrefaction. These are the qualifications of Kutnow’s Powder, and, needless to say, they nevei fail
to assist in the maintenance and re-establishmenfc of Health.

The Brickbat.
(Slung by Various Contributors.)
Adelaide University students have offended
the dignity of the Chancellor so often by singing
“Ta-ra-ra-ra Sammy Way” and other ribald
ditties at successive Commemorations that it has
been decided to shut them out from future
functions. Their crowning wickedness was to
pour confetti on Governor Le Hunte and the
Chancellor at the last Commemoration.
* * * *
“ Edyson ” : I have just been reading a
serious account of a Poodles’ Garden Party
in London, the said garden party being a
parade of the pet dogs of duchesses and minor
dames of fashion at a tea and cake function in the
magnificent grounds of a countess’ town mansion.
The costly tykes were highly appreciated for their
diminutiveness, their breeding, and their “ man-
ners,” for it would seem there is actually an
etiquette for dogs, and Lady-Something’s toy
terrier, Bounce, was disqualified for undignified
conduct in rushing the small marquee in
which luncheon was provided for the pampered
“ purps.” The dogs were expensively gowned
and jewelled, and their behavior on the whole
gave a gush of gratification to the lost souls who
seriously reported the exhibition. 1 he portrait
of the dog whose table manners and general de-
corum earned him first prize for behaviour is
printed in the paper, and the splendid creature,
almost as large as a Queensland spider, wears the
haughty expression of a well-fed British footman,
which is exactly the same expression, as, that worn
by the familiar little lordliug with the face of. an
anguished rabbit who represents England’s old
nooility in many walks of life nowadays. An
Australian girl, just back from London, swears
that these dogs, as displayed by fussy female
aristocrats on Rotten Row, look down on mere
people with unmistakable insolence and hauteur.
8he also declares that they do not bark, but utter
a sound like “ haw, haw ! ” And she saw one with
a monocle suspended round its neck by a gold
chain.
* * * *
“ H. Drummond ” : Some little time ago I
struck Bankstown, five miles out of Rook-
wood, but it might as well be five miles from Rip
Van Winkle, so slow is it. We were after some
declarations from the oldest and stupidest inhabi-
tants in connection with an application to bring
some land under the Real Property Act. We
walked out from Rookwood—the landowner, the
J.P., and myself—to the landowner’s house at j
Upper Bankstown. Some indefinite coach was
due to run at some indefinite hour, but we did not
wait for that. I asked the owner what about a
trap to drive round in, but as only a bullock dray
was available we decided to walk. Ihe J.P, had
done a bit of bailiff’s work in his time, and told
a few of his experiences while waiting for lunch.
Lunch, when it appeared, consisted of four boiled
eggs. The owner took one, the J.P. one, and my-
self one. Each seemed to think of the walk ahead,
and the owner, the J.P., and I looked at one
another and at the extra egg. I saw by the
look in the J.P.’s eye that he had made up his
mind on that egg, but I got there first, just as
be was putting his hand out for it. After
lunch the owner thought he would go a couple
of miles down the road, where he had a
friend who would swear anything. This was
done, and our guide informed us that if we struck
across country three or four miles he was sure
that we could get another declaration. The J.P.
was rather fat, and he lost all sense of the dignity
of his position during this trip. He seemed to
look with scorn on his untitled companions, and
sat on each fence as we came to it and drank
whisky out of his own huge flask. When we got
there the friend was out, so we started a mile or
so along the road for the next oldest inhabitant.
Down the road we ran into a casual who looked
like an old-age pensioner twice over, and who
informed us he had known the property for 50
years. So we drew up a declaration for him, on a
stump by the roadside, and drew a plan on a
sheet of foolscap on the ground. But the de-
clarant could not sign his name, and the J.P.,
who was now pretty drunk, insisted on the
declaration being re-drawn to comply with the
(hie) statutes. A few miles down the road we got
one or two more venerables, and then we started
back for Bankstown. About half a mile along the
road the owner thought of a friend about a mile
back whom he had forgotten. The J.P. stoutly
refused to come, and we left him sitting
in the middle of the road. Saw the
friend, told him what we wanted, drew
a declaration for him, and asked him to
bring it to Sydney next time he came and swear it
before a J.P. “All right,” he said, “ but 1 don’t
know when I’ll be coming. I was down there one
night just on 19 years ago, but the Town Hall
clock kept me awake so long that I don’t think
I’ll go down again.” We left him and got back
to the J.P., who was sound asleep with an
empty flask for a pillow. We managed to get
him to Bankstown, and left him in a pub, un-
conscious. of the fact that he was a J.P. The
owner went home, and I walked wearily into
Rookwood ; and in my pocket were five declara-
tions with the breath of the J.P. heavy upon
them. Of the five people who swore three could
not sign their names.
This “ pome ” of welcome appeared in last
issue of Young Australia : —
And there is no law under which Nicholas the
Honorable can be trundled out of Parliament —
Nicholas who says, “the language . . . have
evolved.” Have it, indeed ?
The sterling value of personal identifica-
tion received a huge shock in Maoriland the other
week. A little girl, about to give evidence against
a man charged with having assaulted her, was
asked to point out the culprit. The girl did not
know the geography of courts, and after carefully
scanning the jury box indicated the foreman as
the criminal. Which was awkward, and moved
the man in the dock to great hilarity. Writer
has had grave doubts about the value of personal
identification for a long while. Walking into
Sydney Public Library one evening he was seized
with an uncontrollable desire to whack the gong
which is used to give the signal for closing up.
An awful clang reverberated through the build-
ing, readers sprang to their feet, attendants
poured from all quarters, and the offender stood
spell-bound, having dropped the drum-stick.
Before he could think of a coherent lie a fussy
little man in a frock coat demanded why he had
not seized the larrikin, and then went on to ex-
plain to the attendants that the man who hit the
gong bolted out as soon as he committed the
atrocity. He described him accurately —short,
small features, dressed in grey serge suit, with a
wide soft felt hat. The attendants went out to
secure this monster of iniquity, if he was still
visible, but returned empty-handed. Then the
writer, after breathing heavily, went off to stay
himself with lemon squash, and wonder what
would have happened had he stabbed the attendant
instead of striking the gong, and a man had
passed dressed in a grey serge suit and wearing on
his head a wide grey felt hat.
A MODERN SPANISH MATCH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the days of Blanky Mary
And her sportive sister, Bess,
Divers schemes and sundry plots were wont to hatch ;
These were oft inclined to vary
As to method, more or less,
But they all were formed against “ The Spanish Match.”
For the sweet and gentle Mary
Married Philip, King of Spain,
Who was most devout (as are most royal rakes).
And though Protestants were wary
Their discretion was in vain,
And they got cold chops for breakfast, or hot stakes >
It was chiefly through Queen Mary
And h r bygone Spanish Match
That the Reformation got its strongest hold.
(This is somewhat legendary,
We perhaps should not attach
Much importance to the story as it’s told.)
But the thing that once had been, a-
Las, perchance may come again,
And my meaning all true Protestants will catch ;
For it seems the Princess Ena
Means to wed the King of Spain,
And to furnish forth another Spanish Match !
To the Protestant persuasion
This engagement must seem wrong
(Though the amorous young couple no one blames)
And appears to give occasion
For a protest stout and strong,
And for pouring oil upon the troubled flames !
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.
WELCOME TOEARL JERSEY AND THE COUNTESS OF JE RSEY,
(By Hon. Nicholas Hawkeo, M.L.O.)
The language of the ages have evolved
No better word than friend.
Friend is the title decorating Jersey,
Australia’s order of affection ;
That Time may ever point with pride
To noble welcome to the gracious peer,
Australia’s welcome.
Then New South Wales in gratitude,
Give hands and hearts
For the Continent;
That evermore may be remembered
The Jerseys, man and wife,
Selected from the great
As Australia’s friends.

popular with singing clubs and societies.

Business, Robbery, etc.
Despite the supposed evil effects of early closing-
and half-holiday legislation the number of small shops in
Sydney and suburbs continues to increase out of all pro-
portion to the increase in population. Streets which had
only one shop five years ago have now three or four,
while in some parts fully a quarter of the residents have
something in their windows for sale. This appears to be
a common tendency of the time, and the effect on prices
and qualities must be considerable. Few of those small
shops can afford to buy in large quantities ; most of them
dea! with retailers, who themselves buy from retailers a
little better oft, and the last purchaser pays tribute to a
long line of middlemen or middlewomen. That last pur-
chaser, therefore, suffers either in price or quality, but as
he is mostly in debt he can’t go elsewhere. He generally
levels up transactions, however, by leaving a good part of
the lent unpaid. Then the small shopkeeper fails to pay
the landlord and the bigger shopkeeper, and the landlord
puts in a bailiff and gets very little, and the bigger shop-
puts more dead things in the tea to make good his
* * * •
The Recorder of Cardiff (Eng.) told a jury that
he had heard that the pawnbroker’s sign of three balls
represented the odds of two to one against an article
pawned ever being redeemed.
* * • *
“Drifter ” : Travelled through a N. S-W.
mountain mining district recently, and was struck bv
the drab, poverty-stricken appearance of the miners’
habitations. The expression of squalor within- and
around them ached l.ke a thankless tooth. Seldom a
flower or green patch of garden was planted to brighten
the unloveliness. The m.ners’ mansions were principally
3-roomed structures, slung together on slack heaps, with,
slabs, mud and weather-stained kerosene tins : grinning
mockeries of home, sweet home. They had broken window-
panes stuffed with rags, crazy front doors, unutterably
ugly sapling and clay chimneys, and a mass of jam tins:
and rubbish littering the adjacent scenery. Swarms of
ragged children playing around almost every diminutive'
hovel gave an impression that they must roost on the
rafters at night for want of space within. In summer
beneath the low roofs of corrugated iron, the tempera-
ture must be as Sheol with the bellows blowing.
In winter, if the little children ; do not die w-holesale of
lung complaints, it can be only because of the hardv
stock from which they spring. There can be no quiet
nook for the toiler from the black pit to doss in peace in
the daytime; no pleasant home life—no nothing, save a
long avenue of gloom and grime leading to a rheumatic
old age. These men of the pick and safety lamp with
their toiling wives and sturdy ragged children, deserve a
better fate.

“T.D.”: After viewing the shipbuilding yards.
,of flarland and Woolf and others at Belfast recently and
lv&tching the launching of an 8000-tonner, and the fitting
up of the huge Cunarder America, I dreamed a dream of
the wonderful unseized possibilities of Sydney for ship
construction purposes. At Belfast the ships as they take
to the water seem in imminent danger of hitting one
of the river wharves, and all navigation is impeded
until the splash is over and the vessel has been safelv
moored out of the way ; whereas in Sydney there are many
miles of foreshore that would lend themselves admirably
to the construction of the hugest kind of imaginable ark.
And just to think of the fleet of steamers built in
the United Kingdom every year solely to engage in the
Australasian trade. A demand by such companies as the'
Union, and Howard Smith’s, and Huddart Parker’s and
the A.U.S.N. for steamers to be built on the spot would
soon create the necessary firms and the essential first-
class marine architects, but so long as those concerns are
largely iuu with Biitish capital, the British shareholders-
will have a prejudice, and sometimes a pecuniary advan-
tage, in keeping the ship-building work for Belfast or the
Clyde. It’s a pity to think of such a lot of natural advan-
tages running to waste.
* * * *
Parsons, the so-called inventor of the steatn-
turblne, splendid marine specimens of which drive the,,
steamers Loongana and Maheno in Australian waters
should have his name coupled with that of George
Stephenson. Stephenson, broadly speaking, took one of
Watt s vertical engines and made its wheels touch ground
so that when they revolved the machine moved forward'
In like manner Parsons took a Pelton water-wheel—a
thing in use as a high-speed water-motor for many
years—and substituted a jet of high-pressure steam for
the jet of water which, impinging on the buckets on the
rim of the wheel, made it revolve. Stephenson and
Parsons deserve the title of /“great,” but neither oU
them was an inventor of the particular machine with
which his name is identified. They saw how another
man s invention could be improved, and they carried out-
his ideas.
•*, * . *
Owen Daly, better known on Westralian gold-
fields as “Micky t.he Priest,” first discovered gold at the
Black Range, 10 years ago. He was one of the first on
the Murchison, where he worked one or two good shows
Later on, lie wandered out along the Lawler’s road, and
prospected in the Black Range, leading a hermit’s life for
months. With some of his savings he purchased a
battery, but results were disappointing, and “Micky”
lost everything. Still he believed in the place, and hired
himself out in order to get money to continue prospect-
mg ; but meantime others came along and secured the
prize. Micky is still there, but has not done any good
Originally intended for a priest, he has a quaint way oi'
speaking—bog Latin jostling the jargon of the goldfields.
He lives now in a cave. Mining at the Range is on the
up grade, the principal show paying regular dividends.
Hans Irvine, who was mainly instrumental in opening up
the mines there, is expected to reach Fremantle, on his
way out from England, early in March, and will t>o direct
to the field.
HOLES IN THE GROUND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A pub, a store, an old bark hut,
Some fossickers—just so
Drowses the town (the mines were shut
Full forty years ago) ;
And through the streets of Diggers’ Flat,
Where gold like dirt was found,
A weary cow now gazes at
The blank holes in the ground.
Across the bar the beery band
With mildewed grief o’erflows ;
For metal comes like water, and
Like wind the metal goes.
Once diggers lit their pipes with notes,
Played skittles with champagne ;
But such great times and such dry throats
They’ll never see again.
With reefs blown out the district’s dead,
The diggers spread like chaff ;
High on some rotting poppet-head
Grim kookaburras laugh.
The shafts are filled with water now,
Broken each battery;
Along the creek a carrion Chow
Sluices eternally.
Like one vast sheet the Golden Town
Spread tents, a thousand head ;
No Southern sun looked laughing down
On life more rich and red.
Twas gold quick spent, gold easy won
’Twas golden work and play ;
They sifted dirt till set o’ sun,
They drank till break o’ day.
These roaring days, these royal sprees
They spun too swift to last;
Now grass and humble raspberries
Trail o’er this golden past.
A boozer lifts his head to grin
(Still Fate twirls trouble round),
A cocky’s cow has tumbled in
Some blank hole in the ground.
VIC.
LOUIS ESSON.

Queensland Government has sunk £500,000
in sugar mills. Under the old regime of black labor
coupled with the awful Philp, the mills were
nearly broke, and Queensland’s chance of getting
its money back looked very blue. But now under
the care of a Labor Government (practically) and
a white Australian policy things are looking
healthy. When Treasurer Kidston states that the
financial condition of the mills is satisfactory,
Queensland can rest assured it will get its money.
Amusing to remember that the old Tory gang,
which made such an awful hash of all things,
used to taunt the Labor party with its lack of
“ business experience.”
“Drifter”: I have a deadly, uncon-
querable hatred of people who pester the inoffen-
sive with puzzle problems. Once I was robbed of
a night’s sleep and got a black eye through a fool
conundrum. We were crossing from Africa on a
troopship at the time. Seven hundred of us were
slung up in hammocks, like so many flying foxes
suspended from the lower deck. “Lights out” had
sounded, and several of the troopers were already
asleep, when a husky voice cautiously croaked, “Are
You awake, Ryan?” From one of the second
r ow of hammocks came the drowsy reply, “ Yes ;
what’s up? ” “I have it,” croaked the first voice,
‘‘he would be my own father!” “Shut up!”
cried several men in chorus. A laugh sounded
from Ryan’s hammock. “Wrong!” he ejacu-
lated. “ Tell me it again,” demanded the dis-
turber of the peace. “ Right. Listen . . . Sisters
and brothers I have none, but that man’s
father was my father’s son; what rela-
tion is he to me ? ” In three minutes, the
space between decks became a jabbering pande-
monium. Dim figures rose up from hammocks
in the gloom and commenced to argue fiercely.
Soon all hands were dragged into the whirlpool of
contention, and the place became a roaring In-
ferno. The night officer rushed down with his
guard, thinking some one had scuttled the ship.
A dozen excited men, slung up near the com-
panion way, fell upon him to decide what relation
somebody’s grandfather’s father’s son’s brother
was to themselves. He finally fled with
his brow clasped tightly in both hands, cursing
vigorously. Towards the centre of the ship the
battle was raging furiously. Numbers of the men
got abusive, and cast hot aspersions on other
men’s lack of brains, on their ancestors, and on
their probable descendants. Soon, from the
murk, arose “ language ” -like blasts of brimstone
from the bottomless pit. Two men in hammocks
running parallel with mine began to call each
other hard and unnecessarily bitter names.
Through the babel I.heard one threaten that his
vilifier would £ ' stop something ” if he called him
that particular name again. The challenged.
individual reiterated the slur with considerable
embellishment. Ten seconds later an ammunition
boot hurtled onto my left eye, and the roar of
voices momentarily faded from my ears. When
the constellations of whirling stars and dazzling
comets subsided I drew a sharp knife, and
cautiously dismounted from the hammock. One
savage slash across the head-rope of the boot-'
thrower’s nest and 1 was avenged. I heard a
despairing yell, then his head crashed into the
hard floor six feet beneath. Then I got my
blanket and crept away through the darkness to
the end of the ship to finish the night, nursing my
damaged eye on an uncomfortable bed of life-
belts. And all through a fool conundrum.

WHAT IS CATARRH?
Catarrh is inflammation of
the lining membrane of the
nose and adjoining passages.
If this inflammation is not
arrested it invades the pas-
sages which lead from the
nose to the head, ears, throat
and lungs. It injures the
sight and hearing, destroys
the sense of taste and smell,
renders the breath offensive,
breaks down the affected tis-
sues, consumes the nasal car-
tileges, and rots away the
small frontal bones of the
skull. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and sto-
mach, causes dyspepsia, also
consumption. Do you want
relief and cure? If so, try
our great remedy.

Peels Like A New Woman.
Dyspepsia, Nervousness, General Debility,
Resulting prom Shock, Cured by Mother
Seigel’s Syrup.
The trials and tribulations of motherhood are,
even in the ordinary course of events, numerous
and severe. When to them is added a tragic
event, such as befell the family of Mrs. Johns, of
Elizabeth-street, off Weymouth-street, West
Adelaide, S.A., who had the misfortune to lose
her little son, he being accidentally burnt to
death, then even the most heroic mother may be
expected to collapse.
It was immediately after this terrible event
that Mrs. Johns’ health broke down. Writing
on the 22nd of March, 1905, she says:—“For
quite a number of years my health was very in-
different, and I suffered from dyspepsia, nervous-
ness, and general debility. I was never so ill
that I had to lay up entirely ; but, on the other
hand, I was never really well. During the heat
of summer, especially, I was always run down and
out of sorts, without appetite, sleepless, afflicted
with frequent headaches and pains in my side and
back.
“Doctors’ medicines failed to bring me any
lasting benefit. I persevered in taking them until
I was sick and weary, and at last gave them up in
despair as useless to me. But I owed it to my
children to leave no stone unturned to recover my
health, if possible, and so determined to make a
trial of Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup—a
medicine which had proved wonderfully beneficial
to some friends of mine at Hawthorn, near
Melbourne.
“The very first bottle relieved me from pain.
That was early last summer, and I was so de-
lighted at the prospect of recovery that I gladly
continued its use. Before long the medicine had
toned up my nerves, restored my digestion, and
built me up generally to such an extent that I felt
like a new woman. Though my health: is at
present good, I always keep a bottle of; Mother
Seigel’s Syrup in the house, and it never fails to
set me right when, from any cause, I happen to
be ‘ out of sorts.’ ”*
