The Pride of Life.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
GOD cast a deep sleep on the Man,
Then set a help-meet by his side. .
As he awakened I began,
And all the earth was glorifi id.
As gods we walked in Eden vale,
Lord of green herb, and fowl, and brute ;
Then came the Serpent writhing pale,
And showed the sweet forbidden fruit.
I plucked it, I ! That hour was mine !
All naked, unashamed, we stood.
Wisdom new-born, and half-divine.
Rode kingly over leaping blood.
But God came down at cool o’ the day
And feared our deed, and named it Sin.
He drove her forth and bade me stay.
Not I ! God could not keep me in.
’Mid thorns and thistles, then Man fought
And wrestled with the grudging soil.
I went before him as he wrought—
Lo, he rejoiced in sweat and toil!
And when the mother of all men lay,
His mate, in travail for his child,
I knelt beside her and straightway
Thro’ sharpest pangs she knew, and smiled.
Men lived and multiplied, a race
Of giants. Led by me they reared
A tower that reached to Heaven’s high place.
God saw it, and again He feared.
With tongues’ confusion cursed He them,
Let loose among them Hate and Strife,
Then shook in scorn His garment’s hem,
And let them battle for their life,
I drew from halting tongues the word
Of might, the challenge sounding far.
I laughed aloud when all men heard,
And dressed the panoply of war.
I lived in play of bow and spear,
Swift onset, shock of line on line;
My race of heroes knew not fear
Great deeds, great deeds, great deeds were
mine !
I spake, they builded cities seven
That shone like jewels on the plain.
Laughter and song rose up to Heaven—
And God looked down in wrath again.
He spake : “ This is the end. No more
Shall vex mine ears Man’s insolent mirth.”
He bade the great deeps yawn and pour
Their waters over all the earth.
I saw my strong men weep and rave,
My queenly women shrink and flee,
God strove with Man, and none to save—
The pains of death got hold on me.
Up from the nethermost despair
I rose ; I woke with throes of pain.—
I saw a white dove cleave the air ;
I followed back to Man again.
God strove with Man ; but such as I
God could not slay. Up clomb the sun,
Seven-hued the great bow spanned the sky—
Our truce-flag. I and Man had won.
Seed-time and harvest, gift for gift
The seasons grant and take again,
Still the lark’s song climbs up the rift,
And still the sunshine gems the rain.
I call; still leap the ancient fires
To life, and still the strong man wrests
Gladness from living, still desires
The whiteness of a maiden’s breasts.
Mid clash and strain and war of powers
I move alert to do, to dare.
To live ; I know not empty hours,
I breathe no enervating air.
The ecstasy of countless springs
Leaps in my veins, makes quick my will.
I stand above the wreck of things
And laugh, for I am conqueror still.
Q.
W.
My Land Again.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AT Fremantle I found Australia again
—most lovable yet foreign. By-and-by
I saw the reason for this strangeness—
there was nobody in rags as in Europe, and no
beggars whining for pennies. But it was not
quite Australia yet, because Australia is empha-
tically not the Australian city. Sydney is like
Naples, and the people of Sydney are Italian in
their love of pleasure—and British in their
stolidity. Melbourne is Americanly rectangular
and American in its business habits. Brisbane is
as New Orleans with the Spanish element left out.
Adelaide is also rectangular in its streets, and
therefore precise in its ways ; its conduct a pecu-
liar mixture of democratic progress and Cornish
religiousness; its austerity modified by the
climate. Fremantle is indeterminate. Broken
Hill had the distinctive Australian quality during
its first rush, so had Kalgoorlie. Now that fixed
conditions have come, we must look for Australia
further out.
80' I hafLbeen six weeks in Australia before I
felt the old Australia again, and when I did I was
camped about h'affAvay to the centre of the conti-
nent —between Leigh’s Creek and Lake Frome.
Two days before that, and at Quorn, something
of the real Australia had come to me. After a
night of swapping sixpences for whisky, with some
men who had grass and no cattle and others who
had cattle but no grass, men with beards to
the lower ribs, clear in the eye and big of voice
and strong as lions, I awoke to a marvellous
dawn, soft and blue and gold and tasting like
wine.
The night before there had been a Salvation
Army making the world hideous with a dolorous
song of direction—
Cling- to the right,
Co in the fight.
Lookin’ up ter catch the lovin’ smile er Jesus.
Lookin’'up ter catch the smile er Jesus na-ow—
The smile er Jesus now-.
And. the dawn was all the more beautiful because
those raucous voices had gone with the flying fox
and the obscene birds of the night.
A man who had beaten the drought and was
taking his family to Adelaide for Christmas, was
being shaved for the first time in six years, and
his beard, tough as fencing-wire, called all the
energies of the perspiring barber into play. We
wished him a good time down South and went
North ourselves. There was a youth who had
left his girl at Terowie, and at first was subdued
because he had to go to Birdsville, anil then glad
because he would see her again in a year or two ;
and a handsome trooper starting out on a 700-
mile patrol ride, and advisory of a gossamer bag
for the head to barlie the plague of flies born of
the break-up of the drought.
Two nights later we camped at Patsy’s Springs
by half a mile of bones, where sheep and lambs had
filled themselves with water, and, finding not a
twig of saltbush or blade of grass on the bare
earth, had lain them down to die.
We boiled the Jack Sheas by a heap of bones.
The night was very cold, and the camp before the
dawn was a freezing one. In the starlit night the
bones that marked the tragedy of the sheep were
not visible—the great plain barred by the hills
looked to the sky unrultled and mysterious as a
virtuous woman, and seemed filled with maternal
promise.
An hour before the dawn I awoke and lay there
in my blankets, my feet towards the east. The
marvellous stars moved as if they were in a stage
sky. The morning star leaped*on its way to the
zenith six feet at a bound, and the crescent moon
lifted in the same manner. Alpha and Beta Cen-
tauri flamed together, seemingly as close as lovers,
but in reality separated by the span of half the
universe. The Cross was jerked below the
southern horizon, and the stars were taken out of
the sky as if they had been suddenly unhooked by
a man in a hurry behind the scenes. The sky
seemed to shiver ; then the dawn spread fanwise
as quickly as that lighthouse flash at Leghorn;
there sounded the griding call of the plover, as
if it were the cue for the sun to enter ; and, as
quickly as it has been told, the sun strode up, and
the day was come again.
I began to feel the charm.of the wilds again,
and in the weeks that followed I enjoyed the
twelve-hours day of work and the ten-hours
long of sleep; travelling in the early dusk over
wide plains and egg-bald hills, through drifts
that looked pumiceous ; camping by creeks alive
with mosquitoes and having tlie joy of baffling the
energetic little devils by retreating to higher
ground. And there was one day of fierce ram-
rain that fell in streams, and ran in at our collars
and out at our boots ; of lightning so close that
the flashes put themselves almost tangibly in our
hands like walking-sticks ; of thunder that burst
just a foot over our heads like a million drapers
tearing calico.
Our objective was a despairing mining settle-
ment, and after two days of wandering in those
break-neck hills we struck a sudden gorge and
turned down it, and by the track found a black-
bearded, grey-haired young man, hatless, his
eyes bloodshot from liquor and the attacks of
flies, and his lips black, sitting by the ruins of a
burned-o.ut hut.
“ Bad luck, mate ? ”
“Yes, Mister, I’ve been burned out... I got
burned out three months ago, too.”
“Boozed?”
“Somethin’ like that. . . What’s the dab ,
Mister ?”
“ Twenty-first of December.”
“Is that so? Well, well! You see it was
this way. Five of us have been mining up here
about three years, so we thought we’d have a
jollification this Christmas. We sendk down for
thirty-six gallons o’ beer, and ten gallons o’ wine
and two cans o’ whisky. It come here four days
ago—or a week—l don’t know, an’ Banks, he said
it was Christmas Day, and we drank nearly the
lot. We must ’a’ been a week out o’ the
reckonin’.”
“ That’s bad luck.”
“Yes, Mister, I’m the unluckiest cow in
Australia. While I was up there boozin’ and
drinkin’. I’m a great man. Up comes a nigger,
an’ he Says, ‘ ’Day, Mister; plenty big fella fire
alonga your camp.’ Well, yer know, Mister,
when the drink’s in, yer don’t care if-your uncle’s
your aunt, do yer ? Yer think you’re the king o’
the world, don’t yer?”
“Yes, I know.”
“ Well, I gays to the nigger, ‘ Get out, yer black
cow—let the camp burn.’ An’ this mornin’ I
ain’t got a hat to me name. Burned up me coats,
too—an’ all me pick-handles ; I had ten ton o’ ore
bagged for Port Augusta, an’ all the bags is
burned off. . . Look at this. What is it ? ”
“Bit of hoop-iron? ”
“Yes, now; but it toas me portmanter. Never
mind. I won’t want a portmanter for years. , .
See that ? ”
He pointed to a long tortuous burned rope.
“Yes.”
“That was the best vegetable-marrer vine in
the’ole Commonwealth. . . . Oh! I’m a poor cow
I am. . . . See that thing like a burned noos-
paper ? Well, that was the best lot o’ lettuce
between here an’ Quorn. . . . An’ I had a rose-
tree there with two buds on it. . . . I’ll ’ave to
crawl to Leigh’s Crik, an’ get tucker. I haven’t
got any boots ’cept these slippers. . . . Yes, I’ll
have to walk. I c’d have gone down by a dray on
Wednesday if I’d known the blasted place was to
be burned down a Thursday. Me poor little cat
burned, too. Oh, I’m an unlucky cow.
“An’, o’ course, minin’ don’t pay here. I’ve
sent away 130 ton o’ 20 per cent, stuff, an’ I’m
eighty quid in debt. No drink neither. Just
tucker an’ explosives.... Poor, misfortunate wretch,
me !....Y’ don’t know Ashford, in Kent, Mister?
I come from there. Well brought-up, t00....1’m
a tradesman—sharpen all me own drills. I’ve got
to—l work single-handed, an’ I drag all the stuff
down that mountain on a sledge. Poor cow, me.”
The flies gathered in his eyes, and he scarcely
cared enough to drive them away. My horses,
standing head to tail, swished the flies from one
another continually. So bad was the pest that
when the thicker-skinned horse forgot his duty for
a moment he was recalled to his labor by a remind-
ing bite of his rump.
“ Yes, I’m a poor cow; but I’ll get another start
in life. Thank Gord, I’ve got a bit o’ soap, an’
I’ll have a wash first.”
Further away there was a bough camp, and in
it a sun-dried woman, nursing a baby.
The morality of that remote settlement is as
primitive as that of the Bosjesmen. The people
seemed to be gradually reverting to the char-
acteristics of the blacks, as they already imitate
their habits.
It was good to be away from them all, and back
into the cleanliness of the track and the majesty
of the night on those long and scrubby plains. I
had thought, having once felt the beauty of the
art of Italy, that never again should I be satisfied
with a country that is sordid in many ways. But
art merely imitates the beauty of primitiveness,
and my land is beautiful in every rock and tree —
even if only because of its illimitable spaces. And
all are of equal value in their kind—Ghiberti’s
doors and Freeling Heights; Giotti’s tower and
Pichi Richi pass; the brown walls of Florence
and the hills at Patsy’s Springs ; Fowler’s Gap
and the Arch of Peace that dominates Milan.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
The Dethronement.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f | baby couldn’t make it out!
u His seven-year-old sister his hitherto
nurse, attendant, serf—had smacked him ;
and his world had turned suddenly Upside-down.
He had screamed for the maternal power to inflict
swift punishment on the audacious slave, and the
bolt had failed to fall. Instead had come the
paternal mandate, stern and unfeeling—“ Keep
that confounded baby quiet ! ” and the baby had
received another shock.
It was the indignity of the whole thing that
hurt the baby, as, yelling in anger, lie was carried
away to console himself in the barrenness of the
wash-shed. He had been a prince whose voice
was omnipotent, and at whose appalling screams
someone had inevitably suffered. There were
“ steps ” of children who had all been his slaves
and worshippers ; but recently they had refused
to give him their toys to break, and declined- to
have their hair pulled any longer. When the baby
had endeavored to poke out his brother’s eye his
intelligence was extolled, and the brother was
punished for non-appreciation of the delicate
attention. And now when the prince lifted up
his voice against the defection of his subjects, his
chief slave, his body guard, had raised against him
her impious hand. He writhed in humiliation
and impotent wrath, meditating revenge.
Then he heard a woman speak—surely it was
his mother ! Vengeance was at hand ; and he
lifted up his voice mightily to call it down on the
heads of the recalcitrants. But the vengeance
fell otherwise. His father—that father who had
hitherto enforced loyalty on his (the prince’s) sub-
jects—approached his highness truculently with a
strap.
“Stop it! you young devil ! Stop it! —you’ll
wake the baby.’’
The baby ! Why, he was the baby. How
could he wake himself when he wasn’t asleep?
The wonder of the idea held him silent, with
mouth open, until his father had turned to go.
He had never felt so sore before. Now he realised
that his empire was no more; and, rendered
wiser by suffering, he dropped into a disgusted
sulkiness. His thoughts were revengeful thoughts,
but he had a vague expectation that when mother
came back, there would be a restoration of the
status quo.
At last the father came and lifted the deposed
prince to his arms, and, thinking that perhaps his
prestige was restored, his highness was meditat-
ing a roar, when he was carried into a bedroom.
The other children were there, but not one came
to clap hands at him or to prattle baby English to
him. Their enthusiasm was now for someone else.
Another prince ! —a smaller morsel of humanity
than even himself ! And they told him he would
have to love it ! But he would not —no, never !
They put him on the floor, and he crept patheti-
cally towards his mother, but somehow he dared
not cry. He fingered the counterpane near her.
and she looked at him and smiled, but imniedh
ately her gaze returned to the new prince.
And the" deposed prince buried his face in the
bed-clothes.

According to Mr. Harold Rylefct, English
journalist, now touring Australia for Londoii
Tribune :
The principal cause of the great reverse suffered by th@
Tories is the resentment felt by many people who honestly
supported the S’African policy of the late Government.
These people feel that they have been wickedly deluded.
They see now, that the war had no noble purpose or
genuine justification, but that it was, indeed, the result
of what was little, if anything, short of a criminal coil*
spiracy.
Imagine the fate of the “ Pro-Boer ” who even
ventured to hint at such sentiments a short year
or two ago !

Mr. C. Wickes, Waiwera, Wardell-road, Petersham, this city, says:—

from daily occupation, and the many cures

you that it enjoys a greater demand and

'pile remedy ever placed on the market.

Agincourt Alley.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IS this Agincourt Alley ? ” inquired Mr.
James Pyson, from Chancery Lane, of the
dilapidated individual who was using a
lamp-post as a temporary spinal cord.
The person addressed gazed in undisguised
astonishment, tempered with disdain, at the frock-
coated old gentleman before him, and withdrew a
rank clay “ cutty ” from his mouth for sufficient
space of time to enable him to mutter “ Aye.”
Agincourt Alley certainly was not all that the
first half of its title would lead one to expect. It
was more Alley than Agincourt. Mr. Pyson
seemed to feel this. He removed his glossy hat
and mopped his forehead testily. It was summer,
and a hot one at that, as the neighboring drains
clearly notified. Still, business was business, so,
with a sigh for the immaculateness of his white
spats, he carefully stepped over the gutter, along
which numerous grimy urchins were festooned,
engaged in nautical pastimes with paper-boats,
and entered the narrow alley, to the aston-
ishment and alarm of the youthful inhabi-
tants so engaged. As he passed each dis-
hevelled yard, carefully noting the numbers
on the battered cottages, rows of apparel
of a private nature attested to the fact that it was
washing-day. Mr. Pyson eyed the “wash ”
askance, and observed afterwards at the club :
“ There is nothing quite so—uin —indelicate as a
suburban clothes-line on washing-day.”
As he came abreast of No. 33, the back door
was violently opened, and a ragged urchin, accom-
panied part of the way by a loud frying-pan,
rocketted forth, and, with chamois-like bounds,
reached the roof of a friendly wood-shed.
“Yer little scut! wait till I gits me ’ands on
yer ! ” shouted a stout lady in distressfully short
petticoats from the doorway. The youth on the
top of the shed, making a mental note that it
would be a long time before such a contingency
came to pass, placed a “ bull’s-eye” in his mouth
and philosophically settled himself to an enforced
and lengthy esle. With an eye alert for further
flying ironmongery, Mr. Pyson, despite the
savage and noisy attack of a small, unclean and
ill-fed mongrel, approached the door and knocked
on it with his cane.
It was not tili the third assault that a face
appeared with flattened nose at the window. At
his imperious gestures it disappeared, and the
door, opening quarter way, disclosed a young lady
of some eight years in a state of much undress.
She eyed him suspiciously.
“Does Mrs. Delaney live here?” inquired Mr.
Pyson, politely. The young lady, with mouth
agape, regarded him with an admixture of curiosity
and awe, while much scrambling anjl muttering
inside, and the appearance of numerous youthful
persons fighting fiercely for places at the grimy
window, evidenced the excitement which his
arrival caused. He repeated his query, “Does
Mrs. Delaney live here ? ”
“Dunno,” said the young lady, with infinite
caution. “ I’ll go an’ arst ’er.”
She carefully closed the door, and bolted it.
After waiting some five minutes and listening im-
patiently to the mutterings from inside, Mr.
Pyson knocked again. The door was opened some
three inches, and the lady of the house, putting as
much of her face as was possible through the aper-
ture, hissed at him, “Blast yer ! go away ! We
can’t pay yer nuthink this week. Th’ old man’s
outer work an’ ”
“ Tut, tut, my good woman,” interrupted the
scandalised Mr. Pyson, “I am not here to de-
mand payment for anything. I do not want to
collect the taxes, nor yet to insure your life, nor sell
you an encyclopaedia."
The door opened a trifle wider.
“ Wodjer want ? ”
“ Are you Mrs. Delaney ?”
“ Yqs, I ham,” said that lady desperately, com-
ing into the open, and placing her soap-suddy
arms akimbo, “ I ham, an’ see ’ere, I’ve ’ad four-
teen child’en, an’ ’eaps o’ trubble, an’ me ole man’s
outer work—you’re frum the sewin’-machine
people, Is’pose?”
“ No, my good woman, I am not.”
“Ho!” sniffed Mrs. Delaney, but with evi-
dent relief, “ Ho ! an’ oo may I harst are y’u
callin’ yer good wunnnan ? Grashus me, if De-
laney ’eard y’u a-callin’ me like so ’e’d—why, I
dunno wat ’e wouldn’t do fer y’u,’’ she added
vaguely.
Mr. Pyson waved that aside with his hand,
while the neighbors, grouped along the fence on
either side, threw words of encouragement and
advice to Mrs. Delaney.
“lam the bearer of good news,” said the old
gentleman.
The lady snorted incredulously. Good news is
rare in neighborhoods where bailiffs anu babies
are not. Disregarding, however, the remarks as
to his personal appearance, passed by the neigh-
bors, and the reiterated invitations of a moulty,
once-white caged bird to “kiss cocky,” Mr. Pyson
proceeded.
“You did the washing for the late Mr. Wilson,
I believe ? ”
The lady admitted she had done so “ fer a good
ten year,” and started on a panegyric of that
gentleman’s virtues, tempered with remarks
about his extreme fastidiousness as to the gloss
of his shirts, and the hard and untruthful check
which his housekeeper kept on their quantity.
When she had exhausted her stock of reminis-
cences and supply of breath, the lawyer con-
tinned :
“ I am happy to inform you, Madam, that he
has left you a legacy of £l5, and, for some reason
or other, stipulated that I should communicate
with you personally.”
“ What! ” shrieked Mrs. Delaney, grabbing his
arm with a moist hand.
“What!” echoed her innumerable offspring,
who formed a picturesque though ragged back-
ground.
“What!” gasped the neighbors enviously,
craning their necks over the fence.
. “Are y’u pullin’ me leg?” inquired the lady.
Mr. Pyson blushed to the roots of his few remain-
ing hairs. “ I should be very sorry so to do,” he
answered with dignity.
Assured of the genuineness of the information,
Agincourt Alley gave itself over to a scene of
wildest excitement, during which Mr. Pyson,
making a wide detour to avoid the rubbish-box
and the family wash, and beating off with his
cane several determined flank attacks on the part
of the low-born hound afore-mentioned, reached
the gate and made good his escape.
When Mrs. Delaney’s “old man” emerged
from “The Frog and the Stick,” where he had
passed the afternoon “looking for work,” and
wended his way homeward for the evening meal,
it was with genuine astonishment that he-received
hearty congratulations and back-slappings from
numerous acquaintances. At first he was inclined
to regard these overtures as same sort of practical
joke. But when the numerous invitations to
drink (which he hastily accepted) were made
good, he realised that something unexpected had
happened, and ventured to inquire the cause of
his sudden and undeserved popularity. After
great difficulty it was made clear to his fuddled
intellect, and with a whoop of joy he made for the
bosom of his family. He arrived there at full
speed, treading the mongrel underfoot.
“ Heaven rest his sowl this very night,” he said
piously that evening to the immense concourse
sardined into his kitchen. “A grate an’ noble
malm—lib’ral-’anded an’ lion-’earted.”
Miss Murphy from next door sniffed sarcasti-
cally : “Am I wrong in thinkin’ that on’y las’
wake y’ called the same Mister Wilson a mane
sumthink skinflint?”
Mr. Delaney glared at her.
“Y’ are, ma’am,” he said with more dignity
than truth. “An’ phwat’s more, had ye bin a
malm, I’d give ye sich a poonch on ther jore
as ”
Miss Murphy laughed shrilly, and, pushing
forward her unwilling brother, danced excitedly
up and down in the confined space, and invited
her host to “dale wid Tim,’ere. Mr. Delaney
was about to accept the invitation, when the
simultaneous entrance of his daughter and two
large jugs of beer restored peace and placed beer
before the thirsty.
Then once more did Mrs. Delaney recount the
adventures of the afternoon, corrected, to her
intense disgust, by her numerous progeny when-
ever she allowed a warm and active imagination
to stray from the narrow path of facts.
“ I alus did fancy ’e wuz ther least bit swate on
me,” she murmured, coquettishly smoothing her
faded locks with large red hands. The company
tried its best to look credulous —except Miss
Murphy, who sniffed disdainfully. A sniff in
Miss Murphy’s hands —or rather nose—was a
terrible weapon, more expressive than words, loud
and disconcerting.
Then began the delirious pastime of spending
the money in imagination. Everybody had an
opinion to offer. Thousands and thousands of
pounds were squandered in the minds of Agin-
court Alley that night, and numerous and varied
were the suggestions for disbursement offered?-
usually in a form whereby everyone could partici-
pate—such as a picnic, or a social. Then j ust as
Mrs. Delaney, with a fine flight of fancy, was
depicting herself, in a new green dress with red
braid, wheeling the twins, in purple plush, in a
brand-new perambulator, Miss Murphy remarked
thoughtfully : “P’raps it’d be jist as well ter
settle fer ther sewin’-machine ! The man must
git tired o’ callin’ round so often.”
There was a dead hush (the Delaneys’ diffi-
culties with the time-payment machine were no
secret). Mr. D. glared, opened his mouth several
times as if to speak : then, finding he had nothing
to say, said just that much. This unpleasantness
being tided over by the timely arrival of more
beer, procured from the corner hostelry on the
strength of the legacy, the company started
afresh to squander money, and, when the grey
dawn looked from behind St. Mary’s steeple, it
discovered Messrs. Delaney and Murphy seeing
each other home up and down the alley, and
founding breweries, and talking in millions.
Next morning Mr. Delaney, under the depress-
ing influence of a severe bilious attack, held pessi-
mistic views about the genuineness of the alleged
legacy. “For,” said he, mournfully, “it do seem
too good fer to be thrue.” But the arrival of the
postman with a letter for his wife requesting her
to call, at the end of the week, on Messrs. Pyson
and Gilchrist, of Chancery Lane, helped to dispel
his misgivings. Two pints at “The Frog and the
Stick ” completed the cure. After abstruse and
brain-racking calculations, Mrs. D. rushed out
that afternoon and purchased the green dress with
the red braid, which had so attracted her aesthetic
eye ; also a perambulator for the twins—the latter
on time payment.
Great were the doings when Saturday came
round, and, when at last the household set forth
to collect the legacy, it was the entire population
of Aginccurt Alley that turned out to see them
off. Bursting with pride and with the tightness
of her new bodice, Mrs. D. headed the procession,
pushing the pram, and followed by her family in
various stages of cleanliness and discomfort. Mr.
D. brought up the rear with a sack (which was to
act as purse) neatly folded under his left arm, and
a stout piece of timber, for defensive purposes,
grasped in his strong right hand. The Delaney
dog skirmished round the flank of the cavalcade
with innumerable disreputable friends and rela-
tions, while small boys passed sarcastic remarks
from a safe distance.
* * * #
A seductive smell of frying sausages was being
borne softly down on the breeze, and Dusk was
rapidly giving place to Dark when the cavalcade
returned to Agincourt Alley. But it did not
return as it had set out. It looked dejected and
travel-stained, and the Alley knew that catas-
trophe had overtaken it by the deep and heartfelt
oaths, which could be.heard issuing from Delaney’s
lips, whenever there was a lull in' the screaming
of the twins. While his wife was chasing her pro-
testing brood to bed, Mr. D. explained their mis-
fortunes to a sympathetic group of neighbors.
“Have yez iver met wid a gar—a gar—a gar-
nish-ee order? Wal, den avoid one loike yez
would ther bailiff’s mahn. It’s ther very divil an’
all dis same g g-gar-nish-ee order ! D’yez see, we
all ovus went up to dis thievin’ divil ov a serli-
citer an’ we sez, ‘We’s called fer the legicy an’-
thank yer,’ sez we. ‘ Oh,’ sez ’e, doin’ a song an’
dance wid ’is ’ands, ‘ I regrit to hinform yez that
ther sewin’-machine compiny, as put a gar-a-
gar-nish-ee order on ut.’ ‘ An’ phwat ther divil
may thot be?’ said I, strivin’ ’ard to restrain
mesel’ frum dyin’ wid apprehenshun. Then ther
thievin’ devil give us a heap o’ langwidge an’ back
talk, an’ told us a g-gar-msh-Se order wuz a ting
Wat wuz put onto yez money when y’u ’ad none
by sewin’-machine companies fer wat y’u owed
’em ! Oh, it’s a hell ov a ting dis same gar-nish-ee
—avoid ut loike ther D.Ti’s; Be gob though ! I
dealt it out to the old thief. It tuk sivin ov ’em to
put me out—so it did.”
Inside, Mrs. Delaney turned to her ironing and
sighed deeply twice —once with relief at the
thought of the cessation of hostilities on the part
of the sewing machine-canvasser, and once with
apprehension when, with prophetic foresight, she
pictured the future attentions of the perambulator-
vendor’s collector.
Then she bowed her head on her arms and wept.'
ALF. BRASOH.
The Gippsland farmers had no sooner
cooled down after their bushfire exertions than
they started to rush the Treasury for grants for
bush roads. Bent bellowed at them in a tired
tone of voice, and remarked that South Gippsland
alone had had special road grants of £BO,OOO dur-'
ing the last seven years, and showed plans and :
photos, proving that the worst fires' and greatest
loss of life occurred where there were good roads.
But the Age is barracking in the farmers’ aid, and
they’ll probably get a fat slice of Bent's next-
surplus.

A Serious Question.
Where can I obtain relief ? After so many failures,
whom can I trust? Who is the most thoroughly
experienced and reliable physician and surgeon in
Australasia?
The above qualifications, gained from the World s
Most Famous Universities, should be an all-sufficient
